201 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Warren


No. 79356-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


165 Wn.2d 17; 195 P.3d 940; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 1053


November 6, 2007, Argued 
November 20, 2008, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Warren v. Washington, 129 S. Ct. 2007, 173 L. Ed. 2d 1102, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 3046 (U.S., Apr. 20, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from King County Superior Court. 02-1-05060-0. Honorable Michael C Hayden.
State v. Warren, 134 Wn. App. 44, 138 P.3d 1081, 2006 Wash. App. LEXIS 1438 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed judgments of the King County Superior Court (Washington) convicting him of one count of first-degree child molestation and three counts of second-degree child rape.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that even though the prosecutor committed misconduct during defendant's first trial by repeatedly misstating the burden of proof during closing argument and suggesting that defendant did not enjoy the benefit of any reasonable doubt, defendant failed to show that he was prejudiced because the trial court judge interrupted the prosecutor's argument to give a correct and thorough curative instruction, curing any error. The court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion by prohibiting defendant from having contact with his wife as a condition of his sentence because she was the mother of the two child victims of sexual abuse for which defendant was convicted, defendant attempted to induce her not to cooperate in his prosecution, and she testified against defendant. Defendant's criminal history included convictions for murder and for beating his wife. The court held that the order prohibiting contact did not violate defendant's fundamental right to marry because it was reasonably necessary to achieve a compelling state interest, namely protecting his wife and her daughters.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed.

 

202 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Lane v. City of Seattle


No. 80204-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


164 Wn.2d 875; 194 P.3d 977; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 1035


February 28, 2008, Argued 
October 16, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from King County Superior Court Docket No: 05-2-07351-9. Judgment or order under review. Date filed: May 30, 2007. Honorable Michael S Spearman.
City of Seattle v. Okeson, 137 Wn. App. 1051, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1434 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff ratepayers brought an action against defendants city and public utility in the King County Superior Court (Washington), seeking to recover for hydrant payments they made for the preceding three years. The ratepayers also sought to enjoin newly raised city taxes on the utility, which had resulted in the utility's raising its rates. The trial court held that past hydrant payments were illegal taxes. The ratepayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: For years, the utility paid for hydrants by charging its ratepayers a hydrant fee. After a court decision that a similar charge on streetlights was an illegal tax, the city began to pay for the hydrants out of its general fund. To make up the cost, the city raised taxes on the utility, which raised rates on ratepayers. The court held that the charge was a tax, not a fee, because the purpose of charging for hydrants was to increase revenue for the city and ratepayers paid the same cost whether they used hydrants or not. Because the city did not declare the charge to be a tax until 2005, the charge was unconstitutional under Wash. Const. art. VII, § 5. The court held that the ratepayers were within the interest zone of Wash. Rev. Code § 80.04.440 and had standing to challenge the tax and rate increase, but that the city had complied with the constitution by explicitly stating it was taxing the utility, adopting the tax, and making the tax subject to referendum. The court held that the ratepayers were entitled to 12 percent interest on both the pre- and postjudgment award for the past illegal charges pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code §§ 4.56.110(4), 19.52.020, and 80.04.440.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's holdings that providing fire hydrants was a government responsibility for which a government must pay, that the city's tax on the utility was constitutional, and that a municipality must pay the city for hydrants within its boundary. The court reversed the trial court's award of only one percent interest on the past invalid hydrant fees, holding that the ratepayers were entitled to 12 percent interest.

 

203 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Bahl


No. 79988-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


164 Wn.2d 739; 193 P.3d 678; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 1032


February 28, 2008, Argued 
October 9, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Snohomish County Superior Court. 04-1-02484-1. Honorable Pro Tem Judge.
State v. Bahl, 137 Wn. App. 1021, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1010 (2007)
State v. Bahl, 137 Wn. App. 709, 159 P.3d 416, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 324 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Washington) affirming defendant's convictions of second degree rape and first degree burglary and his sentences including a statutory maximum of life in prison, a minimum term within the standard range, a community placement term on the burglary charge, and a mandatory life term of community custody on the rape charge.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's community custody sentence included conditions against possession of pornographic materials or sexual stimulus materials and against frequenting establishments that sold sexually explicit or erotic materials. Defendant objected to the conditions on the ground that there was no evidence to suggest that pornography played any part in his offenses. The trial court and the court of appeals rejected defendant's overbreadth claim. The court held that preenforcement vagueness challenges to conditions of community custody could be raised for the first time on appeal and that defendant's vagueness challenges, under Wash Const. art. 1, § 3, were sufficiently ripe. The conditions prohibiting defendant from possessing pornographic materials or sexual stimulus were unconstitutionally vague. Any restrictions implicating defendant's First Amendment rights had to be clear and reasonably necessary to accomplish state needs and public order. Defendant did not establish that the condition barring him from frequenting businesses whose primary business pertained to sexually explicit or erotic material was vague because the term "erotic materials" in the condition was sufficiently definite.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's sentence and remanded for resentencing.

 

204 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Alvarado


No. 81069-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


164 Wn.2d 556; 192 P.3d 345; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 941


May 20, 2008, Argued 
September 18, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Whatcom County Superior Court. 06-1-01271-5. Honorable Steven J. Mura.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the exceptional sentence imposed by the Whatcom County Superior Court (Washington) following his conviction for residential burglary, first-degree theft, two counts of first-degree possession of stolen property, second-degree possession of stolen property, unlawful possession of marijuana, and unlawful possession of a dangerous weapon.


OVERVIEW: The trial court imposed an exceptional sentence on the burglary offense and ordered that it was to run concurrently with the other convictions. In imposing sentence, the trial court remarked that an exception sentence was appropriate because defendant had committed multiple current offense, because his offender score was the highest the judge had ever seen, and because sentencing defendant within the standard range would result in five current offenses going unpunished. On review, the court held that the trial court properly imposed an exception sentence on the burglary count under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.535(2)(c). Defendant, due to his current offenses and past criminal history, had a calculated offender score of 21. If a standard range sentence had been imposed, he would have received no punishment through confinement on any charge but the burglary conviction because the remaining offenses did not increase the standard sentence range he faced on the burglary count. As such, the record supported the trial court's conclusion that, given his high offender score, some of defendant's current offenses would have gone unpunished if a standard sentence range had been imposed.


OUTCOME: The sentence imposed by the trial court was affirmed.

 

205 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Am. Legion Post No. 149 v. Dep't of Health


NO. 79839-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


164 Wn.2d 570; 192 P.3d 306; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 937


November 6, 2007, Argued 
September 11, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Superior Court. Hon. Gary R. Tabor, Judge. Thurston County Superior Court (06-2-01384-8). Judgment Date: January 26, 2007.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court of Thurston County, Washington, granted respondents', Department of Health (DOH) and Kitsap County Health District (KCHD), motions for summary judgment and dismissing appellant American Legion Post's (Post) case with prejudice. The Post had sought to preclude KCHD and the DOH from prohibiting smoking in the Post. The Post appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Post argued that the Smoking in Public Places Act (Act), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 70.160, did not prohibit smoking in a private facility, such as the Post, that was also a place of employment. The supreme court determined that if a facility was a "place of employment," regardless of whether it was a "public place," smoking was prohibited. The exception for private facilities applied to the definition of a "public place," not a "place of employment" and Wash. Rev. Code § 70.160.060 did not apply when a place did not meet the definition of a "public place." The Post lacked representational standing and was precluded from asserting that the Act violated its members' liberty interests without due process of law. Prohibiting smoking in places of employment was rationally related to protecting the public health and the Act did not violate Wash. Const. art. I, § 7. As there was no privilege involved, the Act did not violate Wash. Const. art. I, § 12 or the Fourteenth Amendment. The Act overcame the Post's void-for-vagueness challenge. KCHD and DOH were prevailing parties and awarded costs and attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

206 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



In re Pers. Restraint of Dyer


No. 79872-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


164 Wn.2d 274; 189 P.3d 759; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 759


November 27, 2007, Argued 
August 7, 2008, Filed


NOTICE:

As corrected by 172 Wn.2d errata.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at In re Pers. Restraint of Dyer, 175 Wn.2d 186, 283 P.3d 1103, 2012 Wash. LEXIS 588 (Wash., Aug. 23, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: In re Pers. Restraint of Dyer, 157 Wn.2d 358, 139 P.3d 320, 2006 Wash. LEXIS 603 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner was convicted of first degree rape against two women. He was sentenced to 240 months of imprisonment. The prisoner filed a personal restraint petition (PRP) alleging respondent Indeterminate Sentence Review Board (ISRB) abused its discretion and violated his constitutional rights when it repeatedly denied him parole.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner was convicted by a jury of two violent rapes. The prisoner maintained his innocence and thus could not participate in the prison's sex offender treatment program (SOTP) because it required "full candor." The ISRB repeatedly denied the prisoner's requests for parole on the grounds that he was an untreated sex offender and his risk of reoffense was very high. The court remanded to the ISRB a denial of parole in 2002 because the ISRB abused its discretion by supporting it parolability decision with speculation and conjecture. In 2006, the ISRB conducted a new parole hearing and set forth in detail the reasons for the denial of parole. The court found that the burden rested with the prisoner to prove he was a fit subject for release. The prisoner failed to demonstrate his complete rehabilitation, and the ISRB did not abuse its discretion by adding 80 months to his minimum term. The ISRB properly adhered to its statutory mandate under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.95.009(3) to make public safety its paramount consideration and did not violate the prisoner's constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The ISRB's decision was affirmed.

 

207 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Bellevue John Does 1-11 v. Bellevue Sch. Dist. No. 405


No. 78603-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


164 Wn.2d 199; 189 P.3d 139; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 757; 36 Media L. Rep. 2217


March 22, 2007, Argued 
July 31, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Superior Court. Hon. Douglas A. North, Judge. King County Superior Court (03-2-16548-4). Judgment Date: April 24, 2006.
John Does v. Bellevue Sch. Dist., 129 Wn. App. 832, 120 P.3d 616, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 2589 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, 15 public school teachers, sought to enjoin respondents, their respective school districts, from releasing their names in response to a request from respondent/intervenor, a newspaper, for names of teachers accused of sexual misconduct against students. The Washington Court of Appeals ordered disclosure unless the allegations of misconduct were patently false. Three teachers appealed. The newspaper cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: Allegations of sexual abuse of children by school teachers needed to be thoroughly investigated by school districts and, if the allegations were substantiated, the media could request records containing the identity of the perpetrating teacher. However, when such allegations were determined to be unsubstantiated, the identity of the teacher was exempt from disclosure to a public records request because such disclosure would have violated the teacher's right to privacy. Thus, the identities of public school teachers who were subjects of unsubstantiated allegations of sexual misconduct were exempt from disclosure under Washington's public disclosure act (PDA), former Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.255 (1992), amended and recodified as Wash. Rev. Code § 42.56.050 (2005). Letters of direction to teachers could be released to the public, but where a letter simply sought to guide a teacher's future conduct, did not identify an incident of substantiated misconduct, and the teacher was not subject to any form of restriction or discipline, the name of the teacher and other identifying information had to be redacted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals in part, holding that a teacher's identity was to be released under the PDA only when alleged sexual misconduct was substantiated or when that teacher's conduct resulted in some form of discipline, even if only a reprimand, and that letters of direction and related documents were to be disclosed under the PDA, subject to redaction in certain circumstances.

 

208 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Grande


No. 81068-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


164 Wn.2d 135; 187 P.3d 248; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 749


March 18, 2008, Argued 
July 17, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Skagit County Superior Court. 06-1-00654-1. Honorable Dave Needy.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a motion for discretionary review of the decision of the Skagit County Superior Court (Washington), which reversed an order of suppression from a district court and which upheld defendant's arrest.


OVERVIEW: A police officer pulled over the car in which defendant was a passenger. After detecting a moderate smell of marijuana coming from the car, the officer arrested defendant and the driver. Thereafter a search of defendant was conducted yielding a marijuana pipe containing a small amount of marijuana. Upon review the court determined that Wash. Const. art. I, § 7 protected individual privacy, meaning that individuals were free from unnecessary police intrusion into their private affairs unless a police officer could clearly associate a crime with the individual. Unless there was specific evidence pinpointing a crime on a person, that person had a right to his own privacy and constitutional protection against police searches and seizures. The court held that the smell of marijuana in the general area where defendant was located was insufficient, without more, to support probable cause for arrest. Where no other evidence existed linking defendant, as a passenger, to any criminal activity, an arrest of defendant on the suspicion of possession of illegal substances, and any subsequent searches, was invalid and an unconstitutional invasion of that defendant's right to privacy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's decision and reinstated the district court's order of suppression.

 

209 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Gonzales Flores


No. 79135-0


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


164 Wn.2d 1; 186 P.3d 1038; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 608


September 11, 2007, Argued 
June 26, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Appeal from Okanogan County Superior Court. 01-1-00276-5. Honorable Jack Burchard.
State v. Flores, 134 Wn. App. 1024, 2006 Wash. App. LEXIS 2183 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Washington Court of Appeals that confirmed his convictions of unlawful delivery of a controlled substance in violation of former Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.401(a)(1)(i), involving a minor in an unlawful drug transaction in violation of former § 69.50.401(f) (1998), recodified as Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.4015, and possession with intent to deliver in violation of former Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.401(a)(1)(i).


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued that insufficient evidence supported the convictions of involving a minor and that the imposition of an exceptional sentence based on judge-made findings violated his constitutional right to a jury trial. While the court affirmed defendant's convictions for possession with intent to deliver and unlawful delivery, it ruled that the evidence was insufficient to convict defendant of involving a minor in a drug transaction. The evidence showed only that defendant's stepdaughter was present when he engaged in drug transactions. Allowing a minor to remain present during a drug transaction was not prohibited by former Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.401(f) (1998), recodified as Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.4015. The statute required evidence that defendant committed some act, directed at the minor, to bring or attempt to bring the minor into the transaction. Further, the trial court erred in imposing an exceptional sentence because the trial court, not the jury, made the factual determination that an aggravating factor existed in finding that the offenses committed were major violations of the Uniform Controlled Substances Act, Wash. Rev. Code ch. 60.50.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction of two counts of involving a minor in an unlawful drug transaction was reversed. Defendant's sentence was also reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

210 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Davis


No. 79068-0


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


163 Wn.2d 606; 184 P.3d 639; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 482


October 9, 2007, Argued 
May 22, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Davis, 133 Wn. App. 415, 138 P.3d 132, 2006 Wash. App. LEXIS 1043 (2006)
State v. Davis (In re Davis), 133 Wn. App. 1003, 2006 Wash. App. LEXIS 1463 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted defendant of harassment, malicious mischief, violation of a protection order, fourth degree assault, and unlawful imprisonment. Based on the jury's response to a special interrogatory, the trial court imposed an exceptional sentence. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant petitioned for discretionary review on the exceptional sentence issue.


OVERVIEW: The State alleged that the crimes against a minor victim were aggravated by her "particular vulnerability." Under then-applicable provisions of the Washington Sentencing Reform Act of 1981 (SRA), the trial court was required to find the existence of an aggravating factor. The trial court submitted the aggravating factor to the jury by special interrogatory rather than make the factual determination itself. The instant court concluded that the jury's response to the special interrogatory was void and could not support defendant's exceptional sentence because the trial court exceeded its authority by delegating its factfinding duty to the jury. At the time defendant was tried, the SRA required the trial court, not the jury, to find the existence of the statutory aggravating factor at issue, according to the preponderance of the evidence standard of proof. The trial court could neither delegate its factfinding duty to the jury, nor impose a more demanding standard of proof. Even though the jury expressly found, beyond a reasonable doubt, the victim was "particularly vulnerable," that finding could not support the imposition of an exceptional sentence under then-existing law.


OUTCOME: Defendant's exceptional sentence was vacated, and the case was remanded for resentencing.

 

211 of 2278 DOCUMENTS




State v. Recuenco


No. 74964-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


163 Wn.2d 428; 180 P.3d 1276; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 364


March 13, 2007, Argued 
April 17, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Washington v. Recuenco, 546 U.S. 1166, 126 S. Ct. 1317, 164 L. Ed. 2d 45, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 1126 (2006)
State v. Recuenco, 154 Wn.2d 156, 110 P.3d 188, 2005 Wash. LEXIS 363 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged by information with second degree assault with a deadly weapon pursuant to former Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.125 (1983) and former Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.310 (1999). The trial court (Washington) imposed a 36-month firearm enhancement. After appeals, the U.S. Supreme Court remanded to the court to consider whether the failure to submit the firearm sentencing factor to the jury was subject to harmless error analysis.


OVERVIEW: The firearm enhancement was three mandatory years, while a deadly weapon enhancement was one mandatory year, pursuant to former Wash. Rev. Code Ann. § 9.94A.310(3)(b), (4)(b), recodified as Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.510. The information charged defendant with second degree assault "with a deadly weapon, to-wit: a handgun." At trial, the jury returned a special verdict form finding that defendant was armed with a deadly weapon. The information did not contain an allegation that a firearm enhancement applied, nor did the jury return a special verdict concluding that defendant was armed with a firearm. Defendant argued that the 36-month firearm enhancement violated his Sixth Amendment rights; only a 12-month deadly weapon enhancement was appropriate because the firearm issue had not been submitted to the jury. The court found that the trial court lacked authority under Apprendi to sentence defendant for an enhancement that was not charged nor found by the jury. Further, defendant lacked any notice that he could be sentenced under the firearm enhancement. The court held that harmless error analysis could not be applied to this error.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the firearm sentence enhancement and remanded the case for correction of the sentence.


 

212 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



McNabb v. Dep't of Corr.


No. 77359-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


163 Wn.2d 393; 180 P.3d 1257; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 363


May 11, 2006, Argued 
April 10, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: McNabb v. Dep't of Corr., 127 Wn. App. 854, 112 P.3d 592, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 1232 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sued respondent Washington Department of Corrections (DOC), seeking to have DOC's force-feeding policy declared unconstitutional, illegal, and invalid as applied to him and to enjoin DOC from enforcing the policy. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of DOC. The Washington Court of Appeals, Division Three, affirmed the trial court's judgment. The inmate petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that an independent state constitutional analysis of the right to refuse artificial means of nutrition and hydration was warranted under Wash. Const. art. I, § 7. However, the State's interests in applying DOC's force-feeding policy to the inmate outweighed his right to refuse artificial means of nutrition and hydration. The State had a compelling interest in maintaining security and orderly administration in its prison system. DOC's force-feeding policy as applied to the inmate promoted the State's mandate to provide basic medical services to prisoners. Allowing the inmate to disrupt the policy was inimical to the State's interest in orderly prison administration. The inmate did not suffer from a terminal condition and application of DOC's force-feeding policy did not merely temporarily relieve a chronic condition but restored the inmate to a naturally healthy condition. The State had a compelling interest in preventing suicide. Death resulting from the inmate's refusal of artificial means of nutrition and hydration would consummate his intent to die. Under these circumstances, the State had a compelling interest in preventing the inmate's intentional death.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

 

213 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



In re Pers. Restraint of Hall


No. 75800-0


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


163 Wn.2d 346; 181 P.3d 799; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 265


March 13, 2007, Argued 
April 3, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Washington v. Hall, 548 U.S. 923, 126 S. Ct. 2979, 165 L. Ed. 2d 984, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 5191 (U.S., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate was convicted of assault in the first degree. The sentencing court imposed an exceptional sentence of 366 months. The Washington Supreme Court granted the inmate's personal restraint petition (PRP) because imposition of the exceptional sentence violated his Apprendi/Blakely Sixth Amendment rights. The judgment was vacated and remanded by the United States Supreme Court for further consideration.


OVERVIEW: In order to obtain relief on his PRP, the inmate had to show by a preponderance of the evidence that he was actually and substantially prejudiced by a violation of his constitutional rights. Imposing an exceptional sentence based on aggravating circumstances found by the trial court, rather than the jury, violated the inmate's Sixth and Fourteenth Amendment jury trial right. The supreme court had to consider whether the failure to submit a sentencing factor to the jury could be a harmless error. The supreme court found that the legislature's explicit assignment of the finding to the trial court precluded assigning the finding to the jury. It would have been procedurally impossible to obtain a constitutionally valid jury finding. Where the legislature directed that the court, not the jury, would make a finding, and established that the standard of proof shall be a preponderance of the evidence, rather than beyond a reasonable doubt, the quantum of evidence introduced to support the finding was immaterial--the error could not be harmless.


OUTCOME: The judgment was remanded to the trial court to ensure that the record reflected that the inmate was properly sentenced to a term within the standard sentencing range.

 

214 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Abrams


No. 79481-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


163 Wn.2d 277; 178 P.3d 1021; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 263


November 6, 2007, Argued 
March 20, 2008, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant moved to dismiss three counts of first degree perjury, arguing that Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.72.010(1) was unconstitutional. The Washington trial court agreed, declaring the entire perjury statute unconstitutional and dismissing the charges against defendant. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: Wash. Rev. Stat. § 9A.72.010(1) was unconstitutional insofar as it required the judge to determine the materiality of a false statement as a matter of law in a perjury prosecution. The State's argument that the statutory language did not prohibit a jury from determining materiality, but merely required a judge to determine materiality as well, was rejected as it was contrary to the statute's literal language that the judge was to determine materiality as a matter of law. There was no set of circumstances under which a judge could constitutionally apply a statute that required a judge alone to determine the materiality of a false statement as a matter of law. Such a procedure violated defendant's right to a jury trial on every element of the crime with which he was charged, a right that is specifically declared inviolate under Wash. Const. art. I, § 21. The remainder of the perjury statute was upheld by severing and striking the offending language as directed by Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.04.010(4). Finally, the new rule did not apply retroactively on collateral attack as it was not a watershed rule of criminal procedure.


OUTCOME: The clause instructing a trial judge to determine the materiality of a false statement as a matter of law was unconstitutional. The case was remanded for trial with the issue of materiality to be submitted to the jury.

 

215 of 2278 DOCUMENTS




York v. Wahkiakum Sch. Dist. No. 200


No. 78946-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


163 Wn.2d 297; 178 P.3d 995; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 240


May 8, 2007, Argued 
March 13, 2008, Filed


NOTICE:

As corrected by 165 Wn.2d errata. Also corrected by 169 Wn.2d errata.


PRIOR HISTORY: York v. Wahkiakum Sch. Dist. No. 200, 110 Wn. App. 383, 40 P.3d 1198, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 330 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, parents of student athletes, sued respondent school district, alleging that the district's random and suspicionless drug testing of student athletes violated Wash. Const. art. I, § 7. A Washington superior court ruled that the district's random drug testing, without any individualized suspicion, was constitutional and granted summary judgment in favor of the district. The parents sought direct review.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that the district's policy of conducting random and suspicionless drug testing of student athletes was unconstitutional and violated student athletes' rights under Wash. Const. art. I, § 7. Although the district asked the court to adopt a "special needs" exception to the warrant requirement to allow drug testing of student athletes, the court declined to recognize such an exception. The court's state constitutional analysis hinged on whether a search had authority of law--in other words, a warrant. A student athlete had a genuine and fundamental privacy interest in controlling his or her own bodily functions. Although the urinalysis test was by itself relatively unobtrusive, a student was still required to provide his or her bodily fluids. Even if done in an enclosed stall, this was a significant intrusion on a student's fundamental right of privacy. The court could not countenance random searches of student athletes with its jurisprudence regarding Wash. Const. art. I , § 7. No argument was presented that would bring the random drug testing of student athletes within any reasonable interpretation of the constitutionally required authority of law.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.


 

216 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Ventenbergs v. City of Seattle


No. 76954-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


163 Wn.2d 92; 178 P.3d 960; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 165


March 22, 2007, Argued 
February 21, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Ventenbergs v. City of Seattle, 125 Wn. App. 1043, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 909 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Washington superior court granted summary judgment in favor of respondents, city, Seattle Public Utilities (SPU), and SPU director, on petitioner's, waste collection company owner, claim, finding that hauling solid waste within the city was not a fundamental right. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed, reiterating that a city had broad discretion to take action under its police powers. The owner petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The owner argued that by granting contracts exclusively to two companies, the city violated Wash. Const. art. I, § 12. The supreme court found that because the power to regulate solid waste collection was with the legislature and local governments, the owner had no fundamental right of citizenship to provide this governmental service. The city's decision not to contract with illegally operating businesses was not unreasonable. The city was concerned with the health and safety implications of waste management. The city determined to limit the number of contractors so that it could establish uniform delivery standards, while at the same time promoting competition. The city did not infringe upon respondent contractor's right to freely alienate his property as he was required to employee the services of the two companies only if he chose not to haul the debris himself. The provisions of Wash. Rev. Code § 35.21.156 did not apply to the companies. The city did not exceed its authority when it chose to contract solely with the two companies. The court of appeals acted within its discretion when it refused sanctions.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

217 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Minor


No. 79003-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


162 Wn.2d 796; 174 P.3d 1162; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 47


October 9, 2007, Argued 
January 17, 2008, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Minor, 133 Wn. App. 636, 137 P.3d 872, 2006 Wash. App. LEXIS 1323 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Washington trial court convicted defendant, a juvenile, of first degree unlawful possession of a firearm juvenile court adjudication. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant petitioned for reversal and dismissal of his firearms adjudication.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court affirmatively misled him when it failed to check the appropriate section of the order. Defendant argued that since former Wash. Rev. Code § 9.41.047(1) mandated notification, violation of the statute required a remedy. The supreme court found that, by failing to check the appropriate paragraph in the order, the predicate offense court not only failed to give written notice as required by the statute, but affirmatively represented to defendant that those paragraphs did not apply to him. There was no onerous burden placed on the State to provide notice. At the time of the disposition hearing, defendant had just turned 15 years old. It was reasonable to believe a person of that age would not necessarily know the degrees of crimes or how their crime might impair their rights. Any person in defendant's position would rely on the representations of the court.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed, the adjudication was vacated, and the underlying charge was dismissed.

 

218 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Resident Action Council v. Seattle Hous. Auth.


No. 80006-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


162 Wn.2d 773; 174 P.3d 84; 2008 Wash. LEXIS 1


May 31, 2007, Argued 
January 3, 2008, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent tenants' council sued appellant city housing authority to challenge the validity of the authority's rule prohibiting the posting of signs on the exterior of tenants' apartment doors. A Washington superior court permanently enjoined the authority from enforcing the rule in any way that infringed on tenants' rights to use their doors for expressive purposes. The authority appealed.


OVERVIEW: The rule banned all signs, flyers, placards, advertisements, or similar material from exterior walls, interior common area walls and doors, and the surface of unit doors that faced the hall or outside. The rule stated that indiscriminate posting created "a negative appearance," and the authority claimed that some displays had been creating hostility among residents. The supreme court agreed with the tenants' council that the rule violated the tenants' rights under the U.S. Constitution's First Amendment because: (1) the tenants retained control and dominion over the outer surfaces of their doors; (2) like an ordinance in City of Ladue v.Gilleo--a U.S. Supreme Court case which held that a city's residential sign ordinance violated First Amendment rights--the rule banned too much speech, as a total ban on signs was unnecessary to support the authority's claimed interests in avoiding the cost of refinishing damaged doors, reducing clutter, or avoiding conflict between residents; and (3) Gilleo made no distinctions between privately-owned residences and publicly-owned surfaces leased as part of a residence to a private tenant.


OUTCOME: The superior court's judgment was affirmed.

 

219 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



In re Disciplinary Proceeding Against Day


No. 200,429-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


162 Wn.2d 527; 173 P.3d 915; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 955


May 10, 2007, Argued 
December 20, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court March 12, 2008.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at In re Pers. Restraint Petition, 2008 Wash. App. LEXIS 1766 (Wash. Ct. App., July 22, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: In re Day, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 748 (Wash., Oct. 18, 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent attorney appealed the Washington State Bar Association Disciplinary Board's unanimous adoption of a hearing officer's recommendation to disbar the attorney.


OVERVIEW: The attorney was convicted of first degree child molestation and sentenced to a term of confinement of 60 months to life. The attorney had molested a former client, who at the time was 11 years old. The attorney violated Wash. R. Prof. Conduct 8.4(b) and (i). The court concluded that while the appropriate presumptive sanction for the attorney's violation of Rule 8.4(b) was suspension under the ABA Standards for Imposing Lawyer Discipline, the hearing officer did not err by deviating to disbarment based on the attorney's abuse of the victim and the victim's mother's trust. The ABA Standards were not applicable in determining the appropriate presumptive sanction for violations of Rule 8.4(i) involving acts of moral turpitude. The appropriate presumptive sanction under Rule 8.4(i) for conviction of a Class A felony involving an act of moral turpitude was disbarment based on Washington case law. Although the hearing officer erred in finding that the aggravating factor of dishonest or selfish motive did not apply, he did not err in his conclusions regarding the remaining factors. The sanction of disbarment was not disproportionate when compared to other cases similarly situated.


OUTCOME: The Board's unanimous recommendation to disbar the attorney was affirmed.

 

220 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Locke v. City of Seattle


No. 79222-4, No. 79381-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


162 Wn.2d 474; 172 P.3d 705; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 948; 26 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1859


June 26, 2007, Argued 
December 13, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Locke v. City of Seattle, 133 Wn. App. 696, 137 P.3d 52, 2006 Wash. App. LEXIS 1285 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, a firefighter trainee and a police officer's personal representative, sued petitioner city for injuries sustained during employment. After verdicts were returned in the firefighter's and representative's favor, the city appealed. The Court of Appeals of Washington affirmed the verdicts, rejecting the city's constitutional challenges to Wash. Rev. Code § 41.26.281. The city appealed. The cases were consolidated for appellate review.


OVERVIEW: Wash. Rev. Code § 4.96.010 waived the city's sovereign immunity for suits under the Washington Law Enforcement Officers' and Fire Fighters' Retirement System Act (LEOFF), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 41.26. Allowing LEOFF members to sue their employer for negligent or intentional harm did not create a new municipal duty not otherwise existing for private parties. The city lacked standing to argue that Wash. Rev. Code § 41.26.281 violated Wash. Const. art. I, § 12, because the constitutional provision did not apply to municipal corporations. The firefighter trainee was entitled to sue under Wash. Rev. Code 41.26.281 where the evidence showed that he had passed the firefighter examination, accepted an offer of employment, enrolled in the LEOFF plan, and received a letter confirming that he was a member of the plan. Thus, he was serving in a position that required passing a civil service examination for firefighter within the meaning of Wash. Rev. Code § 41.26.030(4)(a). The trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying the city's motion for a new trial or remittitur as the evidence showed that the firefighter trainee's permanent injuries would have required continued treatment.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed with respect to the conclusion that the city's sovereign immunity had been waived. The city did not had standing to assert a Washington privileges and immunities claim. The lower court and trial court were affirmed on all other issues in the firefighter trainee's case. The consolidated cases were remanded for further proceedings.

In re Marriage of King


No. 79978-4


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


162 Wn.2d 378; 174 P.3d 659; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 875


May 31, 2007, Argued 
December 6, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court December 11, 2007.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother challenged the judgment of the Washington superior court, denying her motion for a new trial and request for appointed counsel at public expense, in the dissolution proceedings with respondent father.


OVERVIEW: The parties were married for approximately 10 years and had three children. The parties separated and the father filed for dissolution of the marriage. The father was represented throughout the proceedings by private counsel. While the mother had counsel for part of the proceedings, at trial she was unrepresented and proceeded pro se. At the trial's conclusion, the superior court entered a parenting plan granting primary residential care of the children to the father. The plan granted the mother visitation rights. The mother then obtained assistance of counsel and filed a motion for a new trial, a motion that the superior court denied. The mother claimed her fundamental parental liberty interest was at stake in the dissolution proceeding, and the order of the superior court deprived her of the care, custody, companionship, and control of her children. The reviewing court held that fundamental constitutional rights were not implicated in a dissolution proceeding. The outcome of a dissolution proceeding could not be equated with a dependency or termination proceeding. Also, Wash. Const. art. I, §§ 3, 10 and 12, did not provide a right to appointed counsel in a dissolution proceeding.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

In re Pers. Restraint of Elmore


No. 70233-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


162 Wn.2d 236; 172 P.3d 335; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 872


January 4, 2007, Considered 
November 21, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Elmore, 139 Wn.2d 250, 985 P.2d 289, 1999 Wash. LEXIS 640 (1999)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After he pleaded guilty to aggravated first-degree murder with special circumstances and he was sentenced to death, petitioner inmate filed a personal restraint petition, alleging that his counsel was ineffective.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was convicted for raping and murdering his stepdaughter. On his petition for review, the court held that counsel was not ineffective for allegedly failing to conduct a competent investigation into the inmate's mental deficiencies before entering a guilty plea because: (1) counsel's team did an in-depth investigation in preparation of a mitigation report; (2) there was no indication that the inmate was incompetent, insane, or under the influence of any condition that would have diminished his capacity; (3) from the time he surrendered to authorities, the inmate was determined to take responsibility for his actions and plead guilty; and (4) counsel's strategy was to rely on the inmate's remorse and willingness to take responsibility, which would have been undermined by an attempt to diminish his culpability by presenting mental health experts. Counsel was not ineffective for failing to negotiate a plea bargain with the prosecutor because the inmate failed to show that, but for counsel's failure, there was a reasonable probability that the prosecutor would have agreed to a reduction in the number of aggravators or that he would not have pleaded guilty and gone to trial.


OUTCOME: The inmate's personal restraint petition was denied.

 

223 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Wash. State Farm Bureau Fed'n v. Gregoire


No. 78637-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


162 Wn.2d 284; 174 P.3d 1142; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 871


November 28, 2006, Argued 
November 21, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court November 30, 2007.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court was petitioner State's and respondent interest group's challenges to the trial court (Washington) determination that parts I and II of Engrossed Substitute House Bill (ESHB) 2314, 2005 Wash. Laws ch. 514, were invalid absent voter approval under former Wash. Rev. Code § 43.135.035(2)(a), and that part XI of ESHB 2314 did not trigger the voter approval requirement.


OVERVIEW: Engrossed Substitute Senate Bill 6896, 2006 Wash. Laws ch. 56, § 7(6), being a constitutionally valid enactment, was effective in establishing the fiscal year 2006 state expenditure limit. Because the ESHB 2314 taxes did not generate revenues in excess of the fiscal year 2006 expenditure limit as established, the interest group's challenge failed. The court found no basis on which to invalidate the 2006 amendment to former Wash. Rev. Code § 43.135.025. The 2006 amendment was a valid exercise of the legislature's plenary power to enact laws. While the 2006 amendment operated retroactively, it did so within the limitations placed on retroactive statutes and without raising a separation of powers issue. It was neither the prerogative nor the function of the court to substitute its judgment for that of the legislature in enacting laws unless those laws clearly contravened state or federal constitutional provisions. Because the taxes enacted in ESHB 2314 parts I and II did not generate general fund revenues in excess of the fiscal year 2006 expenditure limit thus established, the interest group's challenge failed.


OUTCOME: The portion of the trial court's order ruling regarding parts I and II of ESHB was reversed, and the case was remanded for entry of summary judgment in favor of the State.

Wash. Citizens Action of Wash. v. State


No. 78844-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


162 Wn.2d 142; 171 P.3d 486; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 816


May 8, 2007, Argued 
November 8, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: City of Burien v. Kiga, 144 Wn.2d 819, 31 P.3d 659, 2001 Wash. LEXIS 625 (2001).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The King County Superior Court, Washington, granted judgment in favor of respondents, three nonprofit corporations and Whitman County, by holding that an initiative, I-747, violated Wash. Const. art. II, § 37 because the text of I-747 told voters that they were reducing the general property tax levy increase cap from two percent to one percent rather than from six percent to one percent. Appellant State sought review.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that the text of I-747 led voters to believe that it would generally reduce the property tax increase limit from two percent to one percent. In fact, the initiative generally reduced the property tax increase limit from six percent to one percent. The State argued that the purpose of Wash. Const. art. II, § 37 was satisfied because the Voter's Pamphlet made it clear that there was an ongoing challenge to the validity of I-722, and if that law were struck down, I-747 would have the impact of reducing the property tax increase limit from the lesser of six percent or inflation to the lesser of one percent or inflation for most districts. The supreme court found that the text of the initiative misled voters about the substantive impact of the initiative on existing law. The parties were correct to conclude I-747 was amendatory in character and thus art. II, § 37 was applicable. I-747 violated Wash. Const. art. II, § 37 because a voter reading the initiative could believe that he or she was voting to reduce the property tax limit by one percent instead of by five percent.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Biggers v. City of Bainbridge Island


No. 77150-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


162 Wn.2d 683; 169 P.3d 14; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 784


March 16, 2006, Argued 
October 11, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:

As corrected by 166 Wn.2d errata.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Samson v. City of Bainbridge Island, 683 F. Supp. 2d 1164, 2010 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 7444 (W.D. Wash., Jan. 27, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: Biggers v. City of Bainbridge Island, 124 Wn. App. 858, 103 P.3d 244, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 3708 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent property owners filed their original complaint after petitioner city enacted an ordinance. The property owners sought a declaratory judgment. The trial court granted summary judgment for property owners. While appeal was pending, the city enacted Bainbridge, Wash., Ordinance 2003-34, which extended a moratorium for another year. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed the trial court, and the city sought review.


OVERVIEW: The city adopted moratoria that imposed a freeze on private property development in shoreline areas. At the heart of the suit was the construction of structures that was designed to protect the property owners' property along the shoreline. In their complaint, the property owners argued that the moratoria (1) was invalid under Wash. Const. art. XI, § 11 because the moratoria conflicted with the state laws, specifically, with the with Shoreline Management Act of 1971, Wash. Rev. Code ch. 90.58; (2) was invalid because the moratoria exceeded the scope of the City's statutory authority under Wash. Rev. Code §§ 35A.63.220 and 36.70A.390; (3) was invalid because impermissible purposes; and (4) was invalid because the City's Shoreline Master Program (SMP) was amended. The enactment of the city ordinances in this case was a clear violation of the property owners' rights. Local government had limited power over shorelines. The supreme court concluded that there was no statutory authority for the moratoria. The local goverment's usurpation of power violated Wash. Const. art. XVII, § 1. The city did not have police powers to adopt the moratoria under Wash. Const. art. XI, § 11.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed both the trial court and the unanimous intermediate appellate court's decision invalidating the ordinances.

State v. Day


No. 78187-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 889; 168 P.3d 1265; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 785


January 23, 2007, Argued 
October 11, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:

As corrected by 172 Wn.2d errata.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at State v. Day, 144 Wn. App. 1012, 2008 Wash. App. LEXIS 1012 (Wash. Ct. App., Apr. 24, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Day, 130 Wn. App. 622, 124 P.3d 335, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 3109 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant's motion to suppress evidence seized after a search of his car, he was convicted of manufacturing methamphetamine. Defendant appealed and the Washington Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant filed a petition for review.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant argued that the officer exceeded his authority under the Washington State Constitution by stopping and searching him merely on suspicion of a parking infraction and, therefore, that the fruits of that search should have been suppressed and his conviction vacated for lack of lawful evidence. The supreme court agreed, finding that defendant's vehicle was parked, backed into the bushes with its engine off, when the officer suspected that the vehicle did not have the required permit to park along the river and approached the vehicle. For constitutional purposes, this was a civil infraction, not a traffic infraction. As such, there was no ground for a Terry stop and, it followed, there was no ground for a Terry frisk. As such, the trial court's admission of the evidence seized from defendant's vehicle was improper.


OUTCOME: The trial court's admission of the evidence seized from defendant's vehicle was reversed; his conviction was vacated without prejudice; and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

Rickert v. Pub. Disclosure Comm'n


No. 77769-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 843; 168 P.3d 826; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 716; 35 Media L. Rep. 2505


June 29, 2006, Argued 
October 4, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Rickert v. Pub. Disclosure Comm'n, 129 Wn. App. 450, 119 P.3d 379, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 2186 (2005)



DISPOSITION:  The decision of the court of appeals was affirmed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Public Disclosure Commission (PDC) imposed a $ 1,000 penalty on respondent candidate for violating Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.530(1)(a). The superior court affirmed, but the Court of Appeals (Washington) reversed, holding that Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.530(1)(a) violated the First Amendment. The PDC and its members sought review.


OVERVIEW: The court agreed that Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.530(1)(a) was unconstitutional on its face. The interest asserted by the legislature--protecting political candidates (including themselves)--was not a compelling interest in support of Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.530(1)(a). Moreover, even assuming that protection of political candidates could have been a compelling interest, there was no requirement that the prohibited statements tended to be harmful to a candidate's reputation, i.e., defamatory. Thus, the statute was not narrowly tailored to serve the State's asserted interest in protecting candidates. Additionally, Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.530(1)(a)'s exemption for candidates' false speech about themselves demonstrated that the statute was not narrowly tailored to serve the State's alleged interest in preserving the integrity of elections. Further, Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.530(1)(a) was also fatally flawed due to its enforcement procedures, which were likely to have a chilling effect on speech.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was affirmed.

State v. Reep


No. 79969-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 808; 167 P.3d 1156; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 709


June 12, 2007, Argued 
September 27, 2007, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions of four counts of voyeurism in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.115(2)(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for taking pictures of children playing in his neighbors' fenced backyards that he had saved on his computer. On appeal, the court held that the second search warrant, resulting in the seizure of the photographs supporting defendant's convictions, violated the Fourth Amendment's requirement for particularity. Accordingly, the evidence seized pursuant to that warrant had to be suppressed, and defendant's convictions for voyeurism were reversed. The search warrant authorized seizure of any evidence supporting the suspected criminal activity of narcotics/child sex. The fictitious crime of child sex was broader and more ambiguous than the term "child pornography," and consequently the warrant allowed an officer unbridled discretion to decide what things to seize and permitted the seizure of items that may have been protected under the First Amendment, such as pornographic drawings of children. Therefore, the warrant failed for insufficient particularity.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were reversed.

State v. Yates


No. 73155-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 714; 168 P.3d 359; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 710


November 30, 2006, Argued 
September 27, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Yates v. Washington, 554 U.S. 922, 128 S. Ct. 2964, 171 L. Ed. 2d 893, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 5062 (2008)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at In re Pers. Restraint of Yates, 177 Wn.2d 1, 296 P.3d 872, 2013 Wash. LEXIS 229 (2013)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay granted by Yates v. Sinclair, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 15743 (W.D. Wash., Feb. 7, 2014)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at In re Pers. Restraint of Yates, 180 Wn.2d 33, 321 P.3d 1195, 2014 Wash. LEXIS 241 (2014)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at In re Pers. Restraint of Yates, 183 Wn.2d 572, 353 P.3d 1283, 2015 Wash. LEXIS 701 (2015)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Transferred by State v. Yates, 2016 Wash. App. LEXIS 2282 (Wash. Ct. App., Sept. 27, 2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury of the Pierce County Superior Court, Washington, convicted defendant of two counts of aggravated first degree murder. At a special sentencing hearing, the jury found insufficient mitigating circumstances to warrant leniency. Defendant was sentenced to death. Defendant appealed his convictions and sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was a serial killer, murdered two prostitutes in Pierce County. Pursuant to a plea agreement reached in Spokane County, defendant pleaded guilty to 13 counts of first degree murder (10 in Spokane County, 2 in Walla Walla County, and 1 in Skagit County) and 1 count of attempted first degree murder in Spokane County. In exchange, the Spokane County prosecutor agreed not to seek the death penalty. The court concluded that Pierce County was not precluded from seeking the death penalty for the two murders that defendant committed in Pierce County. The trial judge properly rejected defendant's equitable estoppel argument. The record contained no evidence that Pierce County ever promised defendant that it would forebear seeking the death penalty, nor did the record establish that Pierce County authorized the Spokane County prosecutor to include the Pierce County murders in a plea agreement eliminating the death penalty. Any rational trier of fact could have found that the Pierce County murders were part of a common scheme or plan. Defendant's sentence of death was not excessive or disproportionate to the penalty imposed, considering both the crime and the defendant.


OUTCOME: The judgment and sentence were affirmed.

In re Pers. Restraint of Borrero


No. 78999-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 532; 167 P.3d 1106; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 700


May 3, 2007, Considered 
September 13, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Borrero v. Wash., 128 S. Ct. 1098, 169 L. Ed. 2d 832, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 869 (U.S., Jan. 14, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Borrero, 147 Wn.2d 353, 58 P.3d 245, 2002 Wash. LEXIS 594 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed a personal restraint petition to challenge his convictions for first degree kidnapping under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.40.020(1)(b) and attempted first degree murder under Wash. Rev. Code §§ 9A.28.020(1), 9A.32.030(1)(a) based on alleged violations of U.S. Const. amend. V and Wash. Const. art. I, § 9.


OVERVIEW: During a drug transaction, the inmate took marijuana from a victim at gunpoint, forced the victim inside of a duffle bag after tying his hands and feet with wire, transported the victim to a river, and threw him inside. The inmate was convicted of first degree kidnapping while armed with a deadly weapon and attempted first degree murder. The convictions were affirmed on appeal, but the inmate sought relief from a personal restraint petition. In dismissing, the supreme court used the "same evidence" test. The test required further refinement in cases where one crime was an attempt. The supreme court was required to examine the actual facts constituting the "substantial step" under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.28.020(1) to see if the inmate's double jeopardy rights had been violated. Because the charging instrument did not identify the facts comprising the element of "substantial step," the inmate argued that the trier of fact relied on the facts tending to prove both crimes. The fact that the victim was thrown into the river was not necessary to the proof of kidnapping. Moreover, the intent element for the two crimes was different in law and required different proof.


OUTCOME: The personal restraint petition was dismissed.

City of Pasco v. Shaw


NO. 77195-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 450; 166 P.3d 1157; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 697


May 9, 2006, Argued 
September 13, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion denied by Shaw v. Pasco, 128 S. Ct. 1651, 170 L. Ed. 2d 385, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 2579 (U.S., Mar. 17, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: City of Pasco v. Shaw, 127 Wn. App. 417, 110 P.3d 1200, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 969 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed a trial court's finding in favor of respondent city, which held that Pasco, Wash., Ordinance 3231 did not violate Wash. Const. art. I, § 7 and the Fourth Amendment. Petitioners, landlords and tenants, challenged the constitutionality of the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court found that the city ordinance required a landlord to submit a certificate of inspection, but it did not authorize the city itself to search for housing violations. If a private inspector found code violations, the ordinance did not require the inspector to turn over the findings to the city. A landlord could remedy any violations found by an independent inspector, submit to another inspection, and obtain a license based on the new inspection, without the city ever being notified of the original violations. The landlords and tenants did not show that landlords and the privately engaged inspectors were state actors. Neither the Fourth Amendment nor Wash. Const. art. I, § 7 was violated. The inspection required by Pasco, Wash., Ordinance 3231 did not exceed what was already allowed by the Washington Residential Landlord Tenant Act of 1973. The language of the ordinance was not so vague that an ordinary person would not understand its meaning. Arbitrary discretion was limited by the standards set forth in relevant housing codes and regulations.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

232 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Voters Educ. Comm. v. Pub. Disclosure Comm'n


No. 77724-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 470; 166 P.3d 1174; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 701


May 25, 2006, Argued 
September 13, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Voters Educ. Comm. v. Wa Pub. Disclosure Comm'n, 553 U.S. 1079, 128 S. Ct. 2898, 171 L. Ed. 2d 811, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 4636 (U.S., June 2, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a committee and others, sued respondent Washington State Public Disclosure Commission (PDC), seeking a declaration that the committee's advertisements were protected under the First Amendment and Wash. Const. art. I, § 5. The King County Superior Court (Washington) granted the PDC summary judgment, holding that the committee was a political committee under former Wash. Rev Code. § 42.17.020(33) (amended 2005). The committee appealed.


OVERVIEW: The definition of political committee in former Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.020(33) (amended 2005) was not unconstitutionally vague as a person of ordinary intelligence would have had a reasonable opportunity to understand the meaning of the phrase "in support of, or opposition to, any candidate." The committee met the definition of a political committee because its advertisements had contemporary significance only with respect to the target's candidacy for political office and was clearly in opposition to that candidacy. As a result, the PDC did not unconstitutionally infringe on the committee's free speech rights by seeking to compel it to register as a political committee and to disclose its contributions and expenditures. Because the regulation at issue was not vague, the appellate court did not need to reach the issue of whether the trial court correctly applied the distinction between express advocacy and issue advocacy. Wash. Const. art. I, § 5, did not provide greater protection against disclosure requirements than did the First Amendment as neither constitutional history nor preexisting state law proved an intention to protect against disclosure requirements.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of the committee's constitutional claims was affirmed.

 

233 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Futurewise v. Reed


NO. 80430-3


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 407; 166 P.3d 708; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 586


September 6, 2007, Argued 
September 7, 2007, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought to prohibit respondent, the Secretary of State of the State of Washington, from placing Initiative 960 (I-960) on the November 2007 general election ballot. The King County Superior Court (Washington) granted summary judgment for the Secretary and dismissed appellants' action for injunctive and declaratory relief, holding that the initiative was not properly subject to preelection review. Plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: Appellants challenged only the initiative's voter and legislative approval requirements relating to tax increases, which would make the act of "raising taxes" subject to Wash. Rev. Code 43.135.035(1) and (2)(a). The initiative also would make all tax increases potentially subject to referendum by providing that, pursuant to Wash. Const. art. II, § 1(b), tax increases could be referred to the voters for their approval or rejection at an election. At issue was whether the court should review the validity of the challenged provisions of I-960 prior to the November 2007 general election. Appellants first argued that the initiative's referendum provisions exceeded the people's legislative power because they would effectively alter the state constitution's referendum process without complying with the procedures for amending the constitution. Appellants also challenged the super-majority requirement of I-960. The court found that neither challenge was subject to preelection review. I-960 did not purport to amend the constitution, whatever its practical "effect" might be. Prior to election, the court would not entertain an argument that the initiative would be unconstitutional if enacted.


OUTCOME: The court held that appellants' challenge to I-960 was not subject to preelection review, and thus, the initiative could be placed on the general election ballot.

 

234 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Bennett


No. 78377-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 303; 165 P.3d 1241; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 577


March 1, 2007, Argued 
August 30, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Bennett, 131 Wn. App. 319, 126 P.3d 836, 2006 Wash. App. LEXIS 65 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in a Washington trial court of first degree escape in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.76.110. The Washington Court of Appeals, Division Two, affirmed the conviction. Defendant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the burden of proof instruction given, commonly referred to as the Castle instruction, violated his constitutional right to due process by diminishing the State's burden of proof. The court concluded that the Castle instruction given in defendant's case satisfied the minimum requirements of due process. The State was not relieved of its burden to prove every element of the charged crime beyond a reasonable doubt. However, the court also exercised its inherent supervisory authority to instruct Washington trial courts not to use the Castle instruction. Although the Castle instruction was constitutionally adequate, the court did not endorse it, finding the instruction to be problematic. The Castle instruction emphasized what the State need not prove instead of describing the State's burden of proof. The Castle instruction did not enhance the clarity of the reasonable doubt instructions. Until a better instruction was approved, the court instructed Washington trial courts to use Wash. Pattern Instructions Crim. No. 4.01 to inform the jury of the government's burden to prove every element of the charged crime beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was affirmed.

 

235 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Potter v. Wash. State Patrol


No. 79172-4


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 335; 166 P.3d 684; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 580


January 25, 2007, Argued 
August 30, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration granted by, in part Potter v. Wash. State Patrol, 2007 Wash. LEXIS 966 (Wash., Aug. 30, 2007)
Remanded by Potter v. Wash. State Patrol, 165 Wn.2d 67, 196 P.3d 691, 2008 Wash. LEXIS 1059 (Wash., Nov. 26, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: All Around Underground, Inc. v. Wash. State Patrol (In re Chevrolet Truck), 148 Wn.2d 145, 60 P.3d 53, 2002 Wash. LEXIS 788 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a judgment was rendered holding that respondent Washington State Patrol's (WSP) mandatory vehicle impoundment policy exceeded statutory authority, appellant vehicle owner brought a class action suit against the WSP seeking damages based upon the alleged wrongful impoundment of vehicles. The WSP's motion for summary judgment was granted. The owner appealed, and the Washington Court of Appeals certified the case for further review.


OVERVIEW: The owner's class consisted of all owners cited for driving while license suspended whose vehicles were impounded by the WSP under the mandatory impoundment policy of Wash. Admin. Code § 204-96-010, a policy that had recently been determined to exceed statutory authority. Applying Restatement (Second) of Torts § 265 (1977), the trial court found that the officers had authority to impound and that the manner in which they exercised that authority was reasonable. On review, the supreme court held that the privilege under § 265 was not applicable as it applied only to cases involving the reasonableness of acts by individuals. Here, the owner was not alleging that the harm resulted from the conduct of any individual officer, but rather from a WSP directive that unlawfully required impoundment and eliminated individual discretion. Thus, the WSP's argument regarding the reasonableness of WSP officers' actions was not controlling. The WSP could not shield itself from liability for its policies based on a privilege that applied only to individuals and presumed authority to act. As such, the WSP could possibly be liable for conversion, and the summary judgment in its favor was improper.


OUTCOME: The trial court's summary judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings.

 

236 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Lord


No. 77472-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 276; 165 P.3d 1251; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 579


October 26, 2006, Argued 
August 30, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
State v. Lord, 128 Wn. App. 216, 114 P.3d 1241, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 1533 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Washington affirming his conviction of aggravated first-degree murder and death sentence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for abducting, raping, and killing the victim. On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it allowed spectators to wear buttons bearing the victim's in-life picture for a portion of the trial. The buttons were not inherently prejudicial, and defendant failed to show that he was actually prejudiced, and therefore he was not denied a fair trial. The buttons had no words and portrayed an ambiguous message that would be reasonably understood as a show of sympathy and support for the victim's family. The buttons were removed after the third day of trial, both in-life and crime scene photographs of the victim were admitted during the trial, and defendant did not move for a mistrial or a curative jury instruction. The court rejected defendant's contention that the murder charge should be dismissed on the ground that the State failed to disclose allegedly exculpatory dog tracking evidence because there was no Brady violation, as the State disclosed all of the information it had, namely a report showing that the dog track was done.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentence were affirmed.
