State v. Benn


No. 78094-3


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 256; 165 P.3d 1232; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 573


January 23, 2007, Argued 
August 23, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by Benn v. Washington, 128 S. Ct. 956, 169 L. Ed. 2d 720, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 18 (U.S., 2008)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Benn v. Wash., 128 S. Ct. 2871, 171 L. Ed. 2d 813, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 4627 (U.S., June 2, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Benn, 130 Wn. App. 308, 123 P.3d 484, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 2904 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was retried and convicted of two counts of first degree murder. The jury found that the evidence supported the single act aggravating factor. The trial court sentenced defendant to life imprisonment without the possibility of release. The Court of Appeals affirmed the conviction, vacated the single act special verdict on double jeopardy grounds, and remanded for resentencing. The State's petition for review was granted.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the blank verdict question regarding the single act aggravating factor in his first trial constituted an implied acquittal that precluded the State's second prosecution. The first jury had sentenced defendant to death. Thus, the State could have sought the death penalty on retrial. The jury's failure to find the existence of an aggravating factor did not constitute an acquittal of that factor for double jeopardy purposes. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in admitting an unavailable witness's prior testimony where defendant had the opportunity and similar motive to cross-examine him during the first trial. Any alleged error that occurred when the trial court did not allow defendant's attorney to cross-examine the State's experts with learned treatises was harmless where defendant failed to describe how the testimony would have affected the outcome. Review of the trial court's decision to exclude impeachment evidence was not possible where the trial court had sealed the letter and there was no request to unseal it. The trial court's error in admitting one murder victim's hearsay statement was harmless where it actually supported defendant's theory.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed with respect to the holding that the jury's silence on defendant's single act aggravating factor constituted an implied acquittal implicating double jeopardy. The sentence imposed by the trial court was reinstated. The judgment was otherwise affirmed.

 

238 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Morin v. Harrell


No. 79971-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 226; 164 P.3d 495; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 563


May 29, 2007, Argued 
August 9, 2007, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant caregiver sued respondent employers claiming that she was entitled to overtime pay under Wash. Rev. Code § 49.46.010. A Washington trial court granted the employers' motion for summary judgment, concluding that Initiative 518, the source of Wash. Rev. Code § 49.46.010, violated the subject in title rule of Wash. Const. art. II, § 19. The caregiver appealed.


OVERVIEW: Initiative 518, enacted in 1988 and codified as Wash. Rev. Code § 49.46.010 in 1989, amended the Minimum Wage Act by replacing an exemption for domestic service providers with an exemption limited to individuals employed in casual labor. Wash. Const. art. II, § 19 required that the subject of a bill be expressed in the title. However, the court observed that a challenge to the validity of a statute under Wash. Const. art. II, § 1 was precluded where the statute was subsequently reenacted or amended pursuant to properly titled legislation. In the case at hand, the legislature had amended § 49.46.010 at least three times since 1989 and each time, the legislature had maintained the casual labor exclusion. The employers did not challenge the titles of these later amendments and so they were to be presumed constitutional. These amendments cured any defects in the 1989 amendment process.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's summary judgment and remanded the case for further proceedings.

 

239 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. George


No. 78782-4


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 203; 164 P.3d 506; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 562


May 8, 2007, Argued 
August 9, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. George, 132 Wn. App. 654, 133 P.3d 487, 2006 Wash. App. LEXIS 816 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A Washington trial court convicted defendants of attempted first degree theft by deception. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants argued that the State failed to prove the market value of the truck and therefore failed to prove attempted theft in the first degree. The State argued that because he case involved theft of money, it need not prove the market value of the truck. The supreme court found that the actual value of the truck was not relevant to the measure of value of the stolen property. The cashiers check defendants obtained was the "property of another" they intended to obtain by aid of deception, and its value exceeded $1,500. In deception cases, Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.56.020(1)(b) looked only to the value of the property obtained, not the net result of the exchange. There was sufficient evidence to find that defendants had deprived a victim of more than $1,500.


OUTCOME: The judgments were affirmed.

 

240 of 2278 DOCUMENTS




Burns v. City of Seattle


No. 78449-3


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 129; 164 P.3d 475; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 559


November 14, 2006, Argued 
August 2, 2007, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner ratepayers sued respondent cities, alleging that a contractual provision under which an electric company agreed to pay a percentage of revenues received from the cities' power customers in exchange for promises to forbear from establishing their own municipal electric utilities violated Wash. Rev. Code § 35.21.860(1). The Washington trial court granted the cities summary judgment and dismissed the action. The ratepayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The principal issue in the case was whether the electric company's contractual agreement to pay the cities six percent of its revenues from the cities' utility customers was prohibited by Wash. Rev. Code § 35.21.860(1). Wash. Rev. Code § 35.21.860 prohibited a municipality from imposing a franchise fee or any other fee or charge of whatever nature or description on an electric utility. The electric company's agreement to pay the cities a percentage of utility revenues the company received from the cities' residents did not fall within the statutory prohibition because the cities did not impose the payment provision in exercising their governmental powers of taxation and regulation. Rather, acting in a proprietary capacity, the cities negotiated the payment provision as consideration for a special benefit conferred on the company independent from the privilege of operating a franchise. In other words, the payments constituted valid consideration in exchange for the cities' promise to forbear from entering into competition with the electric company.


OUTCOME: The summary judgment order dismissing the action was affirmed.


 

241 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Madison v. State


No. 78598-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


161 Wn.2d 85; 163 P.3d 757; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 556


June 27, 2006, Argued 
July 26, 2007, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State challenged a judgment of a Washington trial court granting respondents, convicted felons, summary judgment and holding that Washington's disenfranchisement scheme was unconstitutional as to felons who, due to their financial statuses, were unable to pay their legal financial obligations (LFOs) immediately. The felons cross-appealed.


OVERVIEW: The felons, who were seeking reinstatement of their voting rights, alleged that Washington's disenfranchisement scheme, which included Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.637 and Wash. Const. art. VI, § 3, violated their constitutional rights because it denied the right to vote to convicted felons who had not completed all of the terms of their sentences, including full payment of their LFOs. The trial court agreed and held that the scheme was unconstitutional as to felons who, due to their financial status, were unable to pay their LFOs immediately. On appeal, the court disagreed, holding that Washington's disenfranchisement scheme did not violate the privileges and immunities clause of the Washington Constitution, Wash. Const. art. I, § 12, because the restoration of voting rights to felons who had fully paid their LFOs did not constitute a grant of favoritism. The scheme did not violate the equal protection clause of the United States Constitution, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, because felons did not possess a constitutionally protected right to vote and the scheme was rationally related to a legitimate state interest, namely that felons had to complete the terms of their sentences.


OUTCOME: The judgment granting the convicted felons summary judgment was reversed.

OVERVIEW: An officer watched a vehicle as it exited the parking lot; the vehicle crossed a double yellow line and pulled immediately into the far right lane. In re Det. of Ambers


No. 79331-0


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


160 Wn.2d 543; 158 P.3d 1144; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 387


March 1, 2007, Argued 
May 31, 2007, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, detainee, challenged the order of the Washington trial court, denying his petition for an unconditional release trial pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 71.09.090.


OVERVIEW: In 1985 the detainee pled guilty to rape in the second degree and was sentenced to 21 months of confinement. After he was released from prison, the detainee committed two more rapes and robberies, and was sentenced to 171 months of total confinement. In 1998 the detainee stipulated to commitment as a sexually violent predator (SVP). In 2005, the detainee petitioned for an unconditional release trial. The reviewing court held that the detainee must show that he no longer satisfied the definition of an SVP and that this change has come about through continuing participation in treatment. The detainee's expert, an expert in the field of sex offender treatment, concluded that it was his opinion that to a reasonable degree of scientific certainty that the detainee's condition had changed since his commitment in 1998 such that he no longer satisfied the definition of an SVP. The appellate court ruled that since the expert indicated the detainee no longer satisfied the definition of an SVP, and because he stated that this change was due to treatment, the detainee made the requisite prima facie showing.


OUTCOME: The order was reversed and remanded.

State v. Chenoweth


No. 77615-6. En Banc


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


160 Wn.2d 454; 158 P.3d 595; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 384


September 21, 2006, Argued 
May 24, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:

As corrected by 164 Wn.2d errata.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
State v. Chenoweth, 127 Wn. App. 444, 111 P.3d 1217, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 1007 (2005).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After petitioners' motion challenging the validity of a search warrant was denied, they were convicted of, inter alia, possession of precursor materials with intent to manufacture methamphetamine and unlawful manufacture of methamphetamine. The Court of Appeals of Washington affirmed the convictions. Petitioners challenged the judgment.


OVERVIEW: ?Authority of law" was satisfied by a warrant, issued upon probable cause, established by sworn affidavit, that may have been based in whole or in part upon hearsay. However, nothing in the language of the provisions governing searches and seizures suggested a negligence standard had to be applied to misstatements or omissions in a warrant affidavit. Moreover, Washington courts consistently applied the federal Franks standard, requiring a showing of reckless or intentional misstatements or omission of material facts. Thus, the text of Wash. Const. art. I, § 7, preexisting state and decisional law, and the ex parte nature of the warrant process supported the conclusion that a search warrant was invalid only if the warrant affiant recklessly or intentionally made material misstatements or omissions. The prosecutor did not act recklessly during a second warrant application where she had reported what she knew of the informant during the first warrant application, which had taken place 24 hours earlier. The warrant was facially sufficient as additional facts and circumstances established the informant's reliability.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

245 of 2278 DOCUMENTS




State v. Boyd


No. 79371-9 consolidated with No. 79339-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


160 Wn.2d 424; 158 P.3d 54; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 381


March 13, 2007, Argued 
May 17, 2007, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In separate Washington trials, defendants were prosecuted for child pornography offenses. In one case, defendant's request for copies of the State's child pornography evidence was denied, with the State retaining sole possession of the evidence; in the other case, defendants' request for copies of the evidence was granted, subject to protective orders. The parties sought review of the discovery orders.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendants argued that Wash. Super. Ct. Crim. R. 4.7(a) controlled, and under that section, they were entitled to copies of the evidence that supported the criminal charges; this argument was based on the claim that the evidence was necessary to effectively represent the clients and prepare a defense. In neither case did the State argue that access to the images should have been denied; its claim was that defendants' attorneys were not entitled to copies of the evidence. The supreme court agreed with defendants and disagreed with the State, finding that the culpability of the acts depicted might have varied based on when the photos or films were taken, by whom, and what they actually displayed. Defense counsel had to consider these and other defenses in reviewing substantial quantities of evidence. Further, adequate representation required providing a "mirror image" of the computer hard drive and enabling consultation with computer experts. And with a restrictive protective order, copies could be maintained in a manner that protected the interests of the victims while ensuring defense counsel the opportunity to adequately prepare.


OUTCOME: The orders in the case where defendants' request for copies of the evidence was granted was affirmed; the order in the case where defendant was denied copies of the evidence was reversed with directions that an appropriate protective order be entered. The cases were remanded tot he trial courts for further proceedings.

State v. Athan


No. 75312-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


160 Wn.2d 354; 158 P.3d 27; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 379


January 26, 2006, Argued 
May 10, 2007, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State filed first degree murder charges against defendant. He made several pretrial motions, including suppression of the DNA evidence and dismissal of the case. The trial court (Washington) denied the motions. Defendant was found guilty of second degree murder and sentenced to 10 to 20 years. The state supreme court granted direct review of his appeal.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued his DNA was collected in violation of the Fourth Amendment and Wash. Const. art. I, § 7 when detectives, posing as a fictitious law firm, induced him to mail a letter to the firm, from which defendant's DNA sample was extracted. The state supreme court found no privacy interest in voluntarily discarded saliva. The police conduct was not so outrageous or shocking as to warrant dismissing the case under Wash. Super. Ct. Crim. R. 8.3(b). The evidence presented by the State was sufficient to prove defendant was guilty of second degree murder. Defendant made a valid waiver of his Miranda rights when he voluntarily spoke with police and later invoked his rights to end the questioning. It was not error to allow hearsay statements made by the decedent into evidence; the statements fell under the state of mind exception to the hearsay rule. Defendant's confrontations rights were not violated with a detective made a passing reference to an out-of-court statement made by a witness who saw defendant pushing a cart with a large box in it in the area where the body was discovered. The statement was consistent with defendant's admissions.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Jorden


NO. 76800-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


160 Wn.2d 121; 156 P.3d 893; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 296



April 26, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
State v. Jorden, 126 Wn. App. 70, 107 P.3d 130, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 316 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted for unlawful possession of cocaine. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed the conviction. Defendant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: A deputy sheriff conducted a random warrant check of a motel's guests via the guest registry and discovered defendant's presence at the motel as well as the fact of two outstanding warrants for defendant's arrest. Deputy sheriffs then entered defendant's motel room in order to arrest him for the outstanding warrants. Upon entering the room, officers saw cocaine in plain view. The court held that the practice of randomly checking the names of guests in a motel registry for outstanding warrants without individualized or particularized suspicion violated defendant's rights under Wash. The evidence obtained from the motel's guest registry, which led officers to defendant's room, was obtained through unlawful means and should have been excluded.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed.

San Juan County v. No New Gas Tax


No. 77966-0


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


160 Wn.2d 141; 157 P.3d 831; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 297


June 8, 2006, Argued 
April 26, 2007, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent county, along with other political subdivisions, sued appellant political action committee (PAC), alleging that the PAC violated the Washington Fair Campaign Practices Act (FCPA), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 42.17. A Washington trial court issued a preliminary injunction and dismissed the PAC's counterclaims, but denied the county's request for attorney fees. The PAC appealed. The county cross-appealed the denial of attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: The central issue on appeal was whether the trial court erred in characterizing the on-air support of an initiative campaign by two radio talk-show hosts as a campaign contribution that had to be disclosed under the FCPA pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.090. The initiative would have repealed a statewide fuel tax approved by the legislature. The instant court concluded that the broadcasts at issue fell within the statutory media exemption under Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.020(15)(b)(iv) because they aired during the content portion of a regularly scheduled radio program, for which the broadcaster did not normally require payment, on a radio station that was not controlled by a candidate or political committee. The station was exercising one of its core media functions in broadcasting the hosts' talk shows. The hosts' broadcasts supporting the initiative campaign fell within the media exemption, regardless of whether the hosts acted at the behest of the PAC or solicited votes and financial support for the initiative campaign. Because the media exemption applied, the trial court erred in ruling that the broadcasts were a contribution subject to disclosure under the FCPA.


OUTCOME: Because the trial court dismissed the PAC's counterclaims based on its incorrect legal conclusion that the radio broadcasts at issue were a contribution subject to regulation under the FCPA, the instant court reversed the trial court's dismissal order and remanded for further proceedings. The instant court affirmed the trial court's denial of attorney fees to the county.

 

249 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Nelson v. Appleway Chevrolet, Inc.


No. 77985-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


160 Wn.2d 173; 157 P.3d 847; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 295


October 19, 2006, Argued 
April 26, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Nelson v. Appleway Chevrolet, Inc., 129 Wn. App. 927, 121 P.3d 95, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 2682 (2005)



DISPOSITION:  The supreme court affirmed the appellate court


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent buyer sued petitioners, including a car dealership, alleging that the dealership's collection of a business and occupation (BO) tax violated Wash. Rev. Code § 82.04.500. The trial court granted the buyer summary judgment, found that the dealership improperly charged the tax, found that declaratory judgment was proper, and certified a class. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed. Petitioners sought review, which was granted.


OVERVIEW: The buyer purchased a car from the dealership. After negotiating a final purchase price, the dealership added $ 79 for BO tax. The buyer argued that the dealership improperly charged this tax as an additional cost above the final price. The supreme court determined that the dealership improperly charged the BO tax on top of the final price because Wash. Rev. Code § 82.04.500 prevented the dealership from directly imposing the BO tax on its customers since the tax was not a tax on customers, was a tax on business, and should have been part of the operating overhead. Section 82.04.500 did not violate the First Amendment, because the dealership could disclose or itemize costs associated with the purchased item. The buyer could seek a declaratory judgment pursuant to the Uniform Declaratory Judgments Act, Wash. Rev. Code ch. 7.24, concerning his rights under Wash. Rev. Code § 82.04.500 because (1) he had standing since he paid the tax for the dealership and he suffered economic injury, (2) the case was a justiciable controversy, and (3) his restitution claim was a private right of action that allowed him to recover the amount improperly paid as BO tax. Class certification was proper.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the appellate court.

State v. Surge


No. 76013-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


160 Wn.2d 65; 156 P.3d 208; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 293


May 26, 2005, Argued. 
April 19, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Surge, 122 Wn. App. 448, 94 P.3d 345, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 1430 (2004).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 43.43.754, six defendants were ordered to submit to deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA) sampling as a sentencing requirement. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed the sentencing requirement and held the State's collection of the DNA samples constituted a special need for which a warrant was not required. All six defendants petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that the compelled collection of DNA samples from convicted felons did not invade a recognized private affair under Wash. Const. art. I, § 7. The State established that DNA collection under Wash. Rev. Code § 43.43.754 was analogous to the routine collection of fingerprints, photographs, and other vital statistics from convicted felons in the context of state constitution privacy concerns. The constitutionality of fingerprinting convicted persons was unquestioned. The statute did not unconstitutionally authorize disturbance of an individual's bodily integrity by allowing the DNA results to be used for purposes other than identity. Defendants, as convicted felons, had only a minimal privacy interest in their identities. Because the statute narrowly defined its purpose as the production of identifying information and this type of information had traditionally not been recognized as a protected interest of convicted felons, there was no disturbance of defendants' private affairs under Wash. Const. art. I, § 7. The court also concluded that the compelled collection of DNA samples from convicted felons did not violate the Fourth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

 

251 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Ford Motor Co. v. City of Seattle


No. 77167-7, No. 77516-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


160 Wn.2d 32; 156 P.3d 185; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 285


June 15, 2006, Argued 
April 12, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Ford Motor Co. v. City of Seattle, 128 S. Ct. 1224, 170 L. Ed. 2d 61, 2008 U.S. LEXIS 1259 (U.S., Feb. 19, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant car company sued defendants, cities, in two separate actions, to challenge the imposition of a business and occupation (B&O) tax on the company related to the company's wholesale sales to car dealers. The King County (Wash.) court entered summary judgment in favor of one of the cities, and the Pierce County (Wash.) court entered a trial judgment in favor of the other city. The company appealed and the cases were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: The company did not directly sell to consumers in the cities. The company sold cars, automobile parts and accessories to independent dealers located within the cities. These dealers, in turn, sold to retail customers. The company also conducted a variety of business activities within the cities related to the sale of its cars, parts, and accessories to dealers. The cities assessed B&O taxes on the grounds that the company was engaged in the business of making sales at wholesale. The supreme court found that the company admitted that it engaged in activities within the cities that were related to its business of making wholesale sales to dealers. Therefore, the company was properly classified and taxed as one engaging in the business of wholesaling within the cities. The cities properly limited their tax measure to only those sales that were connected to the company's business activities in their respective jurisdictions. There was no violation of the Commerce Clause because the cities imposed their B&O wholesaling taxes equally upon interstate and intrastate business and made no distinction between them.


OUTCOME: The judgments were affirmed.

 

252 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Kirkman


No. 76833-1, No. 77596-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 918; 155 P.3d 125; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 210


February 7, 2006, Argued 
April 5, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
State v. Candia, 128 Wn. App. 1053, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 2524 (2005)
State v. Kirkman, 126 Wn. App. 97, 107 P.3d 133, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 313 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State challenged orders of the Court of Appeals of Washington, reversing jury convictions of defendants for sex crimes against children. The cases raised the issue of whether a trial court allowed testimony of an investigating officer or examining doctor as supporting the victims' credibility; and, if so, whether the error was "manifest" constitutional error that could be raised for the first time on appeal under Wash. R. App. P. 2.5(a)(3).


OVERVIEW: Defendants alleged that their trials involved testimony opining on credibility, raising alleged errors of constitutional dimension (i.e., the right to a jury trial). However, opinion testimony relating indirectly to a victim's credibility, not objected to, did not give rise to such error. A doctor's statement that a child's account was "clear and consistent" did not constitute an opinion on credibility. Likewise, by testifying as to interview protocol, a detective provided context enabling the jury to assess the reasonableness of the child's responses. Detectives often used such protocol, regardless of whether they believed the child, and it did not carry a "special aura of reliability" beyond that conferred when the judge swore the child, pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 5.28.020, to tell the truth. Also, where a doctor was asked to reconcile his negative findings with the victim's statement, he did not testify as to any ultimate fact; his testimony that the girl had good language skills was content neutral. Moreover, it appeared that counsel in both cases chose not to object for tactical reasons, and each jury received proper instructions as to their role as the trier of fact.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed, affirming the convictions of defendants.

 

253 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Watson


No. 78052-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


160 Wn.2d 1; 154 P.3d 909; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 211


November 14, 2006, Argued 
April 5, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Watson, 130 Wn. App. 376, 122 P.3d 939, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 2901 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Washington Court of Appeals, which upheld his conviction for failing to register as a sex offender under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.130.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of a sex offense, and he served time in prison. Upon his release, he registered as a sex offender. A few months later, he was put back in jail for 60 days based on his failure to comply with the conditions of community custody. After he was released, he returned to the same address, but failed to reregister as a sex offender. Due to this omission, he was convicted under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.130. He appealed, arguing that the statute was unconstitutionally vague under the Fourteenth Amendment. The court of appeals affirmed this conviction, and the supreme court granted review. In affirming the court of appeals, the supreme court determined that § 9A.44.130, when combined with the existing case law available to citizens, was sufficiently clear to provide the notice required under the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. Defendant did not overcome the presumption of constitutionality. The supreme court noted that a statute was not void for vagueness merely because it could have been drafted with greater precision.


OUTCOME: The conviction was affirmed.

 

254 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Silverstreak, Inc. v. Dep't of Labor & Indus.


No. 76695-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 868; 154 P.3d 891; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 207; 154 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P60,386



March 29, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Silverstreak, Inc. v. Wash. State Dep't of Labor & Indus., 155 Wn.2d 1001, 122 P.3d 185, 2005 Wash. LEXIS 725 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent fill material suppliers challenged petitioner Washington State Department of Labor and Industries' decision that their end-dump truck drivers were entitled to prevailing wages under Wash. Rev. Code § 39.12.020. The Court of Appeals of Washington, Division One, held that the drivers' activities did not involve participation in the incorporation of the delivered materials into the construction project. The Department appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether the end-dump truck drivers on the first phase of construction of an airport runway were entitled to be paid prevailing wages. The lower court erred in applying the canon of ejusdem generis to Wash. Admin. Code § 296-127-018(2)(a) to limit the scope of the coverage of the prevailing wage act, Wash. Rev. Code ch. 39.12, to only those activities similar to spreading, leveling, or rolling. The lower court's application of the canon did not advance the legislature's intent in passing Wash. Rev. Code § 39.12.020, produced the type of wage decrease the prevailing wage act was designed to prevent, and did not accord proper weight to the Department's interpretation of its regulation. Thus, the Department's broader construction of the governing regulation was correct and the drivers had participated in the incorporation of fill material into the project. However, because the Department's present position on the applicability of the prevailing wage act to the drivers' activities was inconsistent with the position it adopted in its 1992 policy memorandum and with subsequent representations it made to the suppliers, the Department was estopped from enforcing its order.


OUTCOME: The judgment that the drivers were not entitled to prevailing wages was affirmed, albeit on different grounds. Because the Department was substantially justified in its actions, the denial of attorney fees to the suppliers was affirmed.

 

255 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Conte


No. 78995-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 797; 154 P.3d 194; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 200


November 28, 2006 
March 15, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Conte v. Washington, 552 U.S. 992, 128 S. Ct. 512, 169 L. Ed. 2d 342, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 11812 (U.S., Oct. 29, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State charged four defendants with nine counts of violating Wash. Rev. Code § 40.16.030. A Washington trial court dismissed the charges against defendants, holding, as a matter of law, that the Washington Public Disclosure Act (PDA), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 42.17, provided the exclusive penalties for the conduct alleged by the State. The State appealed the dismissal of the charges.


OVERVIEW: The State alleged that campaign contributions made by some individuals to three city council members during their reelection campaigns were reimbursed in cash by defendants, and that as a result of this scheme, the incumbents filed false campaign disclosure reports because they did not identify the true source of the contributions. The instant court concluded that the PDA did not bar prosecution of defendants under Wash. Rev. Code § 40.16.030. In dismissing the charges, the trial court erred in applying the "general-specific" rule of statutory interpretation. Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.390 expressly stated that the civil remedies and sanctions in the PDA were in addition to any other remedies provided by law. Thus, the PDA expressly provided that any remedy available under any other statute could be imposed. The express acknowledgement in § 42.17.390 that the remedies listed did not preclude "any other remedies provided by law" showed that the PDA was not intended to preclude a criminal action under Wash. Rev. Code § 40.16.030. There was no conflict between § 40.16.030 and the PDA. Therefore, both could be given effect and harmonized.


OUTCOME: The instant court reversed the trial court's decision, reinstated the charges against defendants, and remanded the case for further proceedings.

 

256 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Bostain v. Food Express, Inc.


No. 77201-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 700; 153 P.3d 846; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 131; 12 Wage & Hour Cas. 2d (BNA) 600


May 18, 2006, Argued 
March 1, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Food Express, Inc. v. Bostain, 128 S. Ct. 661, 169 L. Ed. 2d 512, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 12359 (U.S., Nov. 26, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Bostain v. Food Express, Inc., 127 Wn. App. 499, 111 P.3d 906, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 1018 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a truck driver and his wife, sought review of a decision from a Washington Court of Appeals, which held that the truck driver was not entitled to overtime pay for work as an interstate truck driver because he did not work more than 40 hours a week within the state of Washington. The truck driver contended that such overtime pay was mandated by Washington's Minimum Wage Act (MWA), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 49.46.130(1).


OVERVIEW: The truck driver had prevailed before a trial court on his overtime claim, but that decision was reversed by a state appellate court, which held that the MWA did not count hours worked outside the state of Washington. On review, the court reversed because it found that the plain language of the statute made no distinction between the hours spent driving in state and those spent driving outside Washington, and that its holding was consistent with other related statutes. To the extent that the appellate court had relied on certain regulations for its ruling, the court noted that the regulations could not supersede the plain language of the statute. That is, the court gave no weight to rules defining hours for purposes of overtime provisions as hours worked within Washington's borders because those rules were not consistent with the plain language of the statutes being implemented, nor with the stated purposes of the MWA, nor with the principles that applied to interpretation of remedial legislation governing payment of wages in Washington state. The court also remanded for a new analysis of attorneys' fees, given its ruling, and an analysis of whether a multiplier was appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment below and remanded to the trial court with directions to determine, in reference to attorneys' fees for the truck driver, whether a multiplier was appropriate, in light of the court's conclusion that the factors previously relied on did not support a reduction in the lodestar amount.

 

257 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Sleasman v. City of Lacey


No. 77590-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 639; 151 P.3d 990; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 126


September 12, 2006, Argued 
February 8, 2007, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Sleasman v. City of Lacey, 128 Wn. App. 617, 116 P.3d 446, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 1879 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a husband and wife (couple), in their suit against respondent city, sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Washington), which affirmed a decision of a trial court that the couple violated Lacey, Wash., Code ch. 14.32 by removing trees on their lot without a permit.


OVERVIEW: The state appellate court held that the couple's property was "partially developed," and that former Lacey, Wash., Code § 14.32.030 was not unconstitutionally vague. It held that § 14.32.030 was clear and unambiguous. The state supreme court agreed but found it inapplicable. Under the term's plain meaning, the couple's property was "developed" as it was a lawful building site that was ready for sale or use. The city confused "developed" with "improved." After land was developed, it could be improved. The city asserted that land was only developed when one could no longer improve it. But one could not build on or improve upon a lot unless it was developed. The couple could add to their improvements only because their lot was developed. The land was developed as no part was raw land needing further development to be suitable for sale or improvement; the city and the state appellate court read "partially developed" so broadly that it included nearly every piece of property. And, finally, even if the ordinance was ambiguous, the city's interpretation would not be entitled to deference as it failed to meet its burden that the interpretation was a matter of preexisting policy.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court reversed and awarded costs to the couple.

 

258 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Pillatos


No. 75984-7, No. 75989-8, No. 76081-1, No. 76077-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 459; 150 P.3d 1130; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 62


October 25, 2005, Argued 
January 25, 2007, Filed


NOTICE:

As corrected by 166 Wn.2d errata.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Metcalf, 150 Wn. App. 1063, 2009 Wash. App. LEXIS 1688 (2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Monschke, 133 Wn. App. 313, 135 P.3d 966, 2006 Wash. App. LEXIS 1104 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In four consolidated cases, review was issued to decide whether the Superior Courts of Pierce County, King County, and Spokane County, Washington, had the inherent authority to empanel a jury to determine whether an exceptional sentence should be imposed, and whether 2005 Wash. Laws ch. 68 applied to any of these cases.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court originally took review of these cases to decide whether juries in Washington could be asked to find facts that might justify an upwards departure from standard-range sentences. Shortly after the initial oral argument, the Washington Legislature enacted 2005 Wash. Laws ch. 68, amending the Sentencing Reform Act, Wash. Rev. Code ch. 9-94A, to explicitly give juries this responsibility. The supreme court found that 2005 Wash. Laws ch. 68 applied to all sentencing proceedings held since it was signed into law on April 15, 2005. Thus, the State could seek exceptional sentences against the third and fourth defendants, whether they ultimately plead guilty or went to trial, as they had neither pled guilty nor begun their trials prior to the signing of 2005 Wash. Laws ch. 68. However, since the first two defendant had each pled guilty prior to the signing of 2005 Wash. Laws ch. 68, the procedures set forth in the new enactment did not apply to them. Additionally, the supreme court found that it lacked the power to fashion a remedy to empanel juries, thus the State could not seek exceptional sentences against the first two defendants.


OUTCOME: The State could seek exceptional sentences against the third and fourth defendants, the State could not seek exceptional sentences against the first two defendants, and Washington courts lacked inherent power to empanel sentencing juries outside of the new enactment.

 

259 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Okeson v. City of Seattle


No. 77888-4


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 436; 150 P.3d 556; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 56


May 23, 2006, Argued 
January 18, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Costs and fees proceeding at, Remanded by City of Seattle v. Okeson, 137 Wn. App. 1051, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 1434 (2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Okeson v. City of Seattle, 130 Wn. App. 814, 125 P.3d 172, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 3190 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff ratepayers brought a class action against defendant city, challenging the legality of the municipal utility's greenhouse-gas offset contracts. The King County Superior Court (Washington) granted summary judgment to the city. The ratepayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether the municipal utility was allowed to mitigate the effects of its greenhouse gas emissions by paying public and private entities to reduce those entities' emissions. Combating global warming was a general government purpose, albeit a meritorious one, and not a proprietary utility purpose because, the offset contracts were not part of the services for which individual customers were billed. Rather, the offset contracts were charged to municipal utility customers regardless of how much electricity they used. The contracts also lacked the required nexus to the purpose of supplying electricity because although they were designed to clean up the utility's own emissions, they were also designed to benefit the public as a whole. Thus, the utility lacked authority under Wash. Rev. Code § 35.92.050 to use ratepayer money for the offset contracts because they were neither proprietary in nature nor sufficiently related to the purpose of supplying electricity. Therefore, such mitigation expenses had to be borne by general taxpayers rather than utility ratepayers.


OUTCOME: The order granting the city summary judgment was reversed. The case was remanded for entry of judgment consistent with the appellate court's opinion.

In re Det. of Stout


NO. 77369-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 357; 150 P.3d 86; 2007 Wash. LEXIS 1


June 8, 2006, Argued 
January 4, 2007, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by In re Det. of Stout v. Stout, 2015 Wash. App. LEXIS 1272 (Wash. Ct. App., June 15, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
State v. Stout, 127 Wn. App. 1014, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 1748 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner detainee challenged a decision from the Washington Court of Appeals, which affirmed a trial court's decision in favor of respondent State committing him as a sexually violent predator (SVP).


OVERVIEW: The detainee was convicted of burglary due to an incident where he made unwelcome sexual advances to a victim in her residence. While he was serving this sentence, the State sought an involuntary SVP commitment. The State presented evidence of the burglary and other sexually motivated crimes and incidents. After a commitment order was entered, the court of appeals affirmed. This appeal followed. In affirming, the supreme court determined that, because sexual motivation was established under Wash. Rev. Code § 71.09.020(15)(c), the State was not required to reprove the underlying facts comprising the burglary conviction. The Sixth Amendment right to confrontation did not apply because the detainee was not a criminal defendant. Given the myriad procedural safeguards surrounding such a proceeding, the detainee did not have a due process right to confront witnesses at his commitment trial or at depositions. In addition, the detainee's counsel did not render ineffective assistance at his commitment trial. Finally, a commitment court was not required to reach a specific conclusion of law regarding the detainee's ability to control his behavior.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

 

261 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Brockob


No. 78571-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 311; 150 P.3d 59; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 981


June 8, 2006, Argued 
December 28, 2006, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court January 26, 2007.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Three different defendants' appeals were transferred from the Washington Court of Appeals, Division Two and consolidated to determine two issues common to all three defendants. First, whether there was independent evidence sufficient to corroborate defendants' incriminating statements to law enforcement officers under the corpus delicti rule. Second, whether there was sufficient evidence to support defendants' convictions.


OVERVIEW: The first defendant argued that the State produced no independent evidence to corroborate his statement to an officer that he was stealing the pseudoephedrine pills to sell to someone else who planned to manufacture methamphetamine. The supreme court agreed. The State presented evidence that petitioner stole somewhere between 15 and 30 packages of pills. That evidence was sufficient only to support the logical and reasonable inference that defendant intended to steal the pills. The mere assertion that the pills were known to be used to manufacture methamphetamine did not necessarily lead to the logical inference that defendant intended to do so, without more. Defendant told the officer that he was stealing the pills for someone else who was going to make methamphetamine, but the State had no independent evidence to support that statement other than the officer's bare assertion that the pills were used to manufacture methamphetamine. Absent defendant's statement that he intended to sell the pills to another person who was going to manufacture methamphetamine, the State's evidence only supported the inference that defendant intended to steal the pills.


OUTCOME: The first defendant's conviction was reversed. The second defendant's conviction was affirmed but his case was remanded to strike a $100 fee. The third defendant's conviction was affirmed.

 

262 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Jones


No. 76900-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 231; 149 P.3d 636; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 982


February 7, 2006, Argued 
December 28, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Thomas v. Washington, 127 S. Ct. 2066, 167 L. Ed. 2d 790, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 4175 (U.S., Apr. 16, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Jones, 126 Wn. App. 136, 107 P.3d 755, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 339 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals, Division One (Washington), that reversed defendants' sentences on the grounds that their status on community placement at the time of the current offense was a fact that had to, under decisions of Apprendi and Blakely, be found by a jury beyond a reasonable doubt.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were on community placement at the time they committed their current offenses. Defendants argued that the sentencing court violated their Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial when the judges, rather than a jury, determined that they were on community placement at the time of the present crimes and used that determination to increase their standard range sentence. The supreme court found that unlike the criminal defendants in Blakely and Apprendi, neither defendant improperly received an exceptional sentence based on an aggravating fact relating to their current offense. Rather, under Washington law, a condition of a prior conviction and sentence served to increase their standard range sentence in the same way as the fact of their prior conviction did. The community placement status determination under former Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.525(17) (amended) related only to defendants' status as a recidivist and was readily established as a matter of law by a review of criminal history that incorporated constitutional safeguards. Therefore, the sentencing court's determination properly fell within the Almendarez-Torres prior conviction exception.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the court of appeals and upheld the sentences imposed on defendants.

 

263 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Weber


No. 77395-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 252; 149 P.3d 646; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 983


March 23, 2006, Argued 
December 28, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Weber v. Washington, 551 U.S. 1137, 127 S. Ct. 2986, 168 L. Ed. 2d 714, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 7828 (2007)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at In re Pers. Restraint of Weber, 175 Wn.2d 247, 284 P.3d 734, 2012 Wash. LEXIS 592 (2012)
Magistrate's recommendation at, Habeas corpus proceeding at Weber v. Sinclair, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 187395 (W.D. Wash., Aug. 30, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
State v. Weber, 127 Wn. App. 879, 112 P.3d 1287, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 1314 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury convicted petitioner of first degree assault and second degree attempted murder. The trial court found that petitioner's convictions for attempted murder and assault constituted double jeopardy and vacated the assault conviction as the lesser offense. The Washington Court of Appeals vacated the attempted murder conviction as the lesser offense for double jeopardy purposes and reinstated the assault conviction. Petitioner sought review.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner argued that (1) the inclusion of his prior juvenile adjudications in his offender score during sentencing violated his due process rights and his right to a jury trial, (2) the intermediate appellate court erred by vacating his attempted murder conviction rather than his assault conviction as the lesser offense for double jeopardy purposes, and (3) the prosecuting attorney committed three instances of misconduct requiring reversal of his convictions. The instant court concluded that the inclusion of petitioner's juvenile adjudications in his offender score fell under the prior conviction exception in Apprendi v. New Jersey and did not violate Blakely v. Washington. Petitioner's due process and jury trial rights were not violated by including his prior juvenile adjudications in his offender score. The lesser offense for double jeopardy purposes in this case was second degree attempted murder, not assault, because the attempted murder conviction was the offense that carried the lesser sentence. Thus, the intermediate appellate court properly vacated petitioner's attempted murder conviction. The prosecutor did not commit misconduct that constituted reversible error.


OUTCOME: The intermediate appellate court's judgment was affirmed, and the case was remanded to the trial court for resentencing.

 

264 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



1000 Friends of Wash. v. McFarland


No. 76581-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 165; 149 P.3d 616; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 979


January 26, 2006, Argued 
December 21, 2006, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court January 8, 2007.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court was asked by petitioner to decide whether county ordinances enacted under detailed procedures established by the state Growth Management Act (GMA), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 36.70A, to designate and protect critical areas were subject to a veto by a local vote. Respondents, an advocacy group and a county, opposed allowing referenda to give local entities veto power over the GMA.


OVERVIEW: In a previous case, the court concluded that GMA ordinances, at least those relating to critical areas, were not subject to referenda. The court reached that decision due to the fact that the county was required by the state to designate and protect critical areas and that the state had established elaborate procedures for public participation that were inconsistent with local up and down votes. In the instant proceeding, the court had to determine whether the legislature intended to create broad state policy to be implemented by counties when it enacted the GMA or whether it simply intended to authorize municipalities to take some action. The court held that it was for the legislature to establish any additional role county voters should play in the GMA process or for the voters of the state to amend the GMA via a statewide process under Wash. Const. art. II, § 1. Viewed as a whole, the GMA contemplated that public participation would be channeled through its systems. If ordinances adopted pursuant to these state mandates were to be subject to local referenda, the state legislature had to include the procedure in the underlying statutory schema; it did not do so with the GMA.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the trial judge. Attorney fees were denied, and the county's motion to strike petitioner's statement of additional authorities was denied.

 

265 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Pierce County v. State


No. 76534-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


159 Wn.2d 16; 148 P.3d 1002; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 895


January 24, 2006 
December 7, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
Pierce County v. State, 150 Wn.2d 422, 78 P.3d 640, 2003 Wash. LEXIS 732 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent local governments and others sued appellant State, challenging an initiative that reduced motor vehicle excise taxes. On remand, a trial court granted respondents summary judgment, finding that the initiative impaired the contract between bondholders and intervenor-respondent transit district. Intervenor-appellants, a homeowners association, an association member, and an initiative sponsor, appealed the grant of summary judgment.


OVERVIEW: The initiative violated Wash. Const. art. I, § 23, as the bonds constituted a contract between the district and the bondholders. The initiative substantially impaired that contract by expressly withdrawing the district's ability to levy and collect the motor vehicle excise taxes, thereby reducing the bondholders' security without providing alternative or additional security. Moreover, the initiative was not justified as a reasonable and necessary exercise of the State's sovereign power as a change in tax policy was not a sufficient justification. The bond contract was valid as the district had been properly formed pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 81.112.030(8), that statute was properly enacted by the legislature, and delegation of taxing authority to the district was constitutional. In addition, the district's pledge of the tax revenues did not exceed its statutory authority under Wash. Rev. Code § 81.104.180. The intervenor's request to retire the bonds early was rejected as unconstitutional. The trial court did not err in refusing to award interest on the refund of license fees as the refund of fees was not a judgment and thus, Wash. Rev. Code § 4.56.110 did not apply.


OUTCOME: The trial court's grant of summary judgment and final order on the refund of vehicle license fees were affirmed.

 

266 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Gregory


No. 71155-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


158 Wn.2d 759; 147 P.3d 1201; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 890


March 22, 2005.  
November 30, 2006, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court December 22, 2006 changing the dissenting opinion.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion granted by, Motion to strike denied by State v. Gregory, 2015 Wash. LEXIS 74 (Wash., Jan. 8, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was tried in a Washington State trial court and convicted of first degree rape. In a separate Washington state trial court proceeding, he was convicted of aggravated first degree murder committed in the course of a rape and robbery. The jury in the murder case sentenced defendant to death. He appealed the convictions and sentences in both cases.


OVERVIEW: Although the trial court did not err in its evidentiary rulings in the rape case, the conviction was reversed because of an abuse of discretion by the trial court when it declined to review in camera the dependency files of the victim's child for evidence relevant to defendant's consent defense. The verdict in the rape case boiled down to whose version of the facts was more credible--the victim's or defendant's. The dependency files revealed information that could have led to admissible evidence that the victim engaged in prostitution activity similar to that claimed by defendant close to the time of the alleged sexual assault. Because the evidence was material to the defense, its nondisclosure was not harmless. There was no basis for reversal of the aggravated first degree murder conviction. However, the prosecutor engaged in misconduct during closing arguments in the penalty phase of the murder trial, painting a rosy picture of prison life, including images of defendant watching television and lifting weights, that, when juxtaposed against the images of the crime scene, could not have been cured by an instruction and thus provided a basis for reversal of the death sentence.


OUTCOME: The rape conviction was reversed. The aggravated first degree murder conviction was affirmed, but the death sentence was reversed. The murder case was remanded to the trial court for resentencing.

 

267 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Nelson


No. 77629-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


158 Wn.2d 699; 147 P.3d 553; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 884



November 22, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Nelson, 156 Wn.2d 1016, 135 P.3d 478, 2006 Wash. LEXIS 183 (2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's driver's license was suspended when he was stopped for driving under the influence and refused to take a breath test. While defendant was in custody at a rehabilitation facility, the Department of Licensing sent an order of revocation to his address of record. Defendant was subsequently arrested and convicted for driving with a revoked driver's license. The Washington Court of Appeals, Division One, denied review. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant argued that the Department committed reversible error when it revoked his driver's license without providing constitutionally sufficient notice. The State argued that it satisfied due process requirements because it followed the requirements for providing notice under Wash. Rev. Code § 46.65.065(1). The supreme court found that the State did not know that the notice sent to defendant's address of record was ineffective until after the revocation became operative. Moreover, because the rehabilitation facility was only a temporary detainment facility, the inquiry defendant sent asking for information about how he could have his license reinstated did not put the Department on notice that defendant would still be at the facility when it sent the order of revocation. In addition, by the time the State learned that defendant had not received the notice, defendant had already been released from the facility. Under these circumstances, the Department's failure to take additional steps to put defendant on notice was reasonable. The notice was reasonably calculated to provide defendant with notice of the pendency of his driver's license revocation.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

268 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Gourley v. Gourley


No. 76270-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


158 Wn.2d 460; 145 P.3d 1185; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 828


September 15, 2005.  
October 26, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Gourley, 129 Wn. App. 1040, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 3131 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner father appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Washington upholding a domestic violence protection order under Wash. Rev. Code ch. 26.50 prohibiting contact between the father, respondent mother, and the parties' three children.


OVERVIEW: The mother obtained a protection order against the father after one of parties' children accused the father of sexually abusing her. On appeal, the court held that the father's argument that the commissioner improperly relied on hearsay evidence failed because Wash. R. Evid. 1101(c)(4) allowed courts to consider hearsay in a Wash. Rev. Code ch. 26.50 protection order proceeding. The father's due process rights were not violated when the commissioner refused to allow testimony from and cross-examination of the complaining child at the hearing because cross-examination was unnecessary, as the commissioner had ample evidence with which to make his determination, including the father's admission that he rubbed aloe vera on his child's naked body. In addition, the procedures outlined in ch. 26.50, which protected the father's due process requirements of being heard at a meaningful time and in a meaningful manner, were followed.


OUTCOME: The judgment upholding the protection order was affirmed.

 

269 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Mehlhaff


No. 76776-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


158 Wn.2d 363; 143 P.3d 824; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 735


November 15, 2005.  
October 12, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Mehlhaff, 125 Wn. App. 1044, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 911 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed defendant's conviction for first degree rape of a child, holding that he did not make a showing that the plea bargains were divisible from the stipulations. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that defendant entered into his plea agreement with the understanding that Washington's sentencing scheme permitted a trial judge to enact in fact finding, and if the judge found substantial and compelling reasons, he or she could impose an exceptional sentence beyond the standard range but not exceeding the maximum allowed by the statute. Defendant argued that he need not challenge the entire plea agreement to challenge his exceptional sentence. The supreme court found that Blakely did not apply to indeterminate minimum sentences under former Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.712 that did not exceed the maximum sentence imposed. The statutory maximum sentence was life in prison. Blakely did not apply to defendant's exceptional minimum sentence of 200 months for each offense because both sentences were less than the statutory maximum sentence.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

270 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Hagar


No. 77138-3


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


158 Wn.2d 369; 144 P.3d 298; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 736


November 15, 2005.  
October 12, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Hagar, 126 Wn. App. 320, 105 P.3d 65, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 135 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed defendant's conviction for theft in the second degree and theft in the first degree, holding that he did not make a showing that the plea bargains were divisible from the stipulations. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that the court of appeals found defendant's stipulation to the real facts was an integral part of the plea agreement and was not shown to be divisible. However, whether the stipulation was divisible was irrelevant because defendant did not need to challenge his stipulation in order to establish that a Blakely violation occurred. Even assuming defendant's stipulation was valid, the trial court still engaged in improper Blakely fact finding when it found the crimes constituted a major economic offense. The trial court imposed an exceptional sentence of 30 months, well outside the standard range of three to nine months, based on its finding that defendant had committed a major economic offense. Defendant's sentence was in violation of Blakely because the exceptional sentence was predicated on an unstipulated fact that was not found by a jury beyond a reasonable doubt.


OUTCOME: Defendant's exceptional sentence was reversed and the matter was remanded for resentencing within the standard range.

 

271 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Suleiman


NO. 76807-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


158 Wn.2d 280; 143 P.3d 795; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 732



October 5, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Suleiman, 121 Wn. App. 1067, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 3554 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to three counts of vehicular assault. He stipulated that the facts set forth in the certification for determination of probable cause and the prosecutor's summary were real and material facts for the purposes of sentencing. He did not agree that those facts formed a legal basis for an exceptional sentence. For the most severely injured victim, the trial court sentenced him to a 48-month exceptional sentence.


OVERVIEW: The Court of Appeals (Washington) affirmed the exceptional sentence and concluded that the trial court properly found that the one victim who had been rendered paraplegic was particularly vulnerable. As her vulnerability was sufficient to support the exceptional sentence, the court of appeals did not address the sufficiency of the other reasons. Blakely was subsequently decided, and defendant moved for reconsideration. The court of appeals denied reconsideration. Defendant filed a petition for review and challenged the exceptional sentence, arguing that it violated the Sixth Amendment pursuant to Blakely because the jury did not find the necessary facts beyond a reasonable doubt. The Supreme Court of Washington recognized defendant's behavior was reprehensible. Yet even if the supreme court assumed for the sake of argument that defendant's stipulation was valid, the trial court had to make additional factual findings above and beyond the admitted facts in order to have supported an exceptional sentence based on victim vulnerability. Because those facts were not found by a jury beyond a reasonable doubt, the exceptional sentence violated the Sixth Amendment under Blakely.


OUTCOME: The case was remanded to the court of appeals for determination of whether the Blakely error was harmless.

 

272 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



City of Fircrest v. Jensen


No. 76738-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


158 Wn.2d 384; 143 P.3d 776; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 734



October 5, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Jensen v. Fircrest, 549 U.S. 1254, 127 S. Ct. 1382, 167 L. Ed. 2d 162, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 2701 (U.S., Feb. 26, 2007)



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the validity of legislation, Wash. SHB 3055, relating to admissibility of breath or blood alcohol concentration tests in drunk driving cases. A Washington municipal court rejected all challenges to the legislation. Appellant, who was convicted of being a driver under 21 consuming alcohol, petitioned for direct review of the judgment pursuant to Wash. R. App. P. 4.3.


OVERVIEW: Appellant argued the legislation violated either the United States Constitution or the Washington Constitution by: (1) including more than one subject in its title (2) not including the subject of the bill in its title (3) violating the doctrine of separation of powers, and or (4) violating due process by creating a mandatory rebuttable presumption. The court concluded that the legislation's title stated explicitly that it was amending Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.506 and reenacting and amending Wash. Rev. Code §§ 46.20.308 and 46.20.3101. The title adequately announced it was amending the Washington Motor Vehicle Act, and the subject matter of the legislation was within the Act's purview. Therefore, the legislation did not violate the single-subject rule or the subject-in-title rule of Wash. Const. art. II, § 19. The legislation did not violate the separation of powers doctrine because the legislature was neither invading the prerogative of the courts nor was it threatening judicial independence. Regarding appellant's due process challenge, the court concluded that appellant did not show an impermissible or unconstitutional shifting of the evidentiary burden.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Amunrud v. Bd. of Appeals


No. 76590-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


158 Wn.2d 208; 143 P.3d 571; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 725; 30 A.L.R.6th 775


November 9, 2005.  
September 21, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Amunrud v. Bd. of Appeals, 127 S. Ct. 1844, 167 L. Ed. 2d 324, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 3082 (U.S., Mar. 19, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: Amunrud v. DSHS, 124 Wn. App. 884, 103 P.3d 257, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 3083 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed an order from respondent Department of Social and Health Services (DSHS) suspending appellant father's commercial driver's license for failing to pay child support for his son. The father appealed.


OVERVIEW: The father claimed that he was denied procedural due process prior to the suspension because he was not given a meaningful hearing and that he had a fundamental economic right to pursue an occupation as a taxi driver and that suspension of his commercial drivers' license under Wash. Rev. Code § 74.20A.320 did not survive strict scrutiny. The supreme court found that § 74.20A.320 provided a person with the opportunity for an administrative hearing in order to challenge the suspension. He could obtain a release of his suspension by signing a repayment agreement under Wash. Admin. Code § 388-14A-4520. The father could have appealed the order that raised his child support payment and file a motion to modify support with the court. There was a rational relationship between professional license suspension and the State's interest in enforcing child support orders. The statute promoted the State's interest in encouraging legally responsible persons to financially support their children.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. French


No. 78569-4


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


157 Wn.2d 593; 141 P.3d 54; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 615


June 13, 2006  
August 17, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. French, 88 Wn. App. 586, 945 P.2d 752, 1997 Wash. App. LEXIS 1781 (1997)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in 1995 of six counts: one count of first degree child molestation, two counts of first degree rape of a child, and three counts of second degree rape of a child, for raping and molesting his stepdaughter. Before the sentencing hearing, defendant fled to Mexico. He was apprehended in Mexico in 2005 and sentenced to 192 months by the trial court, Washington. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant raised the following issues, inter alia, on appeal: (1) Whether the fugitive disentitlement doctrine precluded defendant's right to appeal when defendant absconded from the jurisdiction after conviction but before sentencing? (2) Whether the trial court's denial of public funds for the translation of Mexican extradition documents violated Wash. Super. Ct. Crim. R. 3.1 and the due process and fair trial guaranties of the Washington and United States Constitutions? and (3) Whether child molestation was a lesser included offense of rape and whether the defendant's convictions violated his double jeopardy rights or constitute the "same criminal conduct"? The state supreme court held that (1) the fugitive disentitlement doctrine generally did not apply to defendant who absconded after conviction but before sentencing, (2) the trial court's denial of the use of public funds to translate the Mexican extradition documents was not an abuse of discretion because defendant did not prove why it was necessary and defendant was being sentenced within the standard range, and (3) defendant's convictions did not violate his double jeopardy rights.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentence were affirmed.

State v. Korum


No. 75491-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


157 Wn.2d 614; 141 P.3d 13; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 614



August 17, 2006, Decided  
August 17, 2006, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court September 13, 2006.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal after remand at, Remanded by State v. Korum, 141 Wn. App. 1005, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 2824 (Wash. Ct. App., Oct. 9, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Korum, 120 Wn. App. 686, 86 P.3d 166, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 383 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State appealed a decision of the Court of Appeals (Washington), which dismissed charges added after defendant withdrew his guilty plea. The case was also remanded for a determination of whether the trial court should dismiss any of the remaining charges as a deterrent to prosecutorial vindictiveness under Wash. Super. Ct. Crim. R. 8.3(b). Defendant cross-appealed his convictions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with 16 counts of burglary, robbery, kidnapping, and assault. He pled guilty to one count of first degree kidnapping while armed with a deadly weapon and one count of second degree possession of a firearm. Defendant later withdrew his plea, and the State filed an amended information containing 15 additional counts. The Court of Appeals held that 10 of the kidnapping charges were incidental to the robberies as a matter of law. The State did not raise the merger issue under Wash. R. App. P. 13.7, 13.4(c)(5). The court reversed in part, and held that all of the charges, with the exception of unlawful possession of a firearm, were crimes against persons under former Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.440(2). The court also held that the additional charges related to crimes where defendant personally entered the invaded homes and hence was identifiable by nonparticipants in the crime. In the incident related to the original charges, defendant did not enter the homes. Also, the increased number and the consequent severity of the collective charges caused the discrepancy in the sentences, not prosecutorial vindictiveness.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the 10 kidnapping counts that the court of appeals held were incidental to the robberies. The court reversed the dismissal of the counts related to prosecutorial vindictiveness, and upheld defendant's other convictions.

State v. Osman


No. 76948-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


157 Wn.2d 474; 139 P.3d 334; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 606


February 16, 2006  
August 3, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Osman, 126 Wn. App. 575, 108 P.3d 1287, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 519 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Washington Court of Appeals upheld the trial court's imposition of a standard range sentence. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that the trial court violated the Sentencing Reform Act of 1981 (SRA), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 9.94A, the equal protection clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, and Wash. Const. art. I, § 12 by denying his request for a sentence under the special sex offender sentencing alternative (SSOSA), Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.670(4), based on his possible deportation. The supreme court found that the trial court did not violate the SRA when it considered the effect that defendant's possible deportation had on his treatment and/or punishment. Defendant, an illegal alien, did not show how his treatment differed from treatment of others. He did not indicate whether his claim was based on different treatment as compared to a citizen or as compared to another alien, subject to deportation or not subject to deportation. The denial of defendant's request for a sentencing alternative out of concern that he would not be able to comply with the requirements of a SSOSA was rationally related to the SRA's goal of ensuring standardized sentencing and did not violate equal protection. Defendant did not meet his burden of showing that the trial court intentionally discriminated against him.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Fowler


No. 77250-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


157 Wn.2d 387; 139 P.3d 342; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 607


March 21, 2006  
August 3, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Fowler, 127 Wn. App. 676, 111 P.3d 1264, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 1176 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a Washington trial court of two counts of incest in the first degree, two counts of incest in the second degree, and one count of rape in the second degree, all stemming from sexual misconduct with his stepdaughter. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the trial court erred by admitting into evidence recordings of two telephone conversations he had with the victim. The conversations were recorded in Oregon with the consent of and by the victim acting at the request of the Oregon police. Defendant was in Washington when he spoke on the phone to the victim, and he did not consent to the recordings. Defendant claimed that the recordings of the conversations in Oregon were unlawful under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.73.030 and were therefore inadmissible in court under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.73.050. The supreme court found that the two calls were recorded in Oregon with the consent of one of the parties to the call. It was lawful under Oregon law to record a telephone conversation with one party's consent. Defendant did not have an expectation of privacy related to his behavior in Oregon and the resulting criminal investigation by Oregon police regarding his sexual misconduct while in Oregon. No Washington State law enforcement officer or official requested or in any way encouraged the making or recording of the calls. They were not unlawful under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.73.030 and were not barred by Wash. Rev. Code § 9.73.050.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.
