Andersen v. King County


No. 75934-1, No. 75956-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


158 Wn.2d 1; 138 P.3d 963; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 598



July 26, 2006, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, the State and a county, challenged decisions of the King County Superior Court and the Thurston County Superior Court (Washington), which held that the provisions of Washington's 1998 Defense of Marriage Act (DOMA), 1998 Wash. Laws ch. 1, that prohibited same-sex marriages were facially unconstitutional under the privileges and immunities and due process clauses of the Washington State Constitution.


OVERVIEW: Respondent couples were denied marriage licenses because they sought to marry a person of the same sex. They sought a declaratory judgment that Wash. Rev. Code §§ 26.04.010 and 26.04.020(1)(c) were unconstitutional. The trial court applied heightened scrutiny. The court reversed and held (1) an independent state analysis was not appropriate unless the challenged law was a grant of positive favoritism to a minority class; (2) gay and lesbian persons did not constitute an inherently suspect class for purposes of Wash. Const. art. I, § 12, and there was not a fundamental right to marry a person of the same sex; (3) the appropriate standard of review was rational basis review; (4) encouraging procreation between opposite-sex individuals within the framework of marriage was a legitimate government interest furthered by limiting marriage to opposite-sex couples; (5) as there was no history of same-sex marriage in Washington, the citizens did not hold a privacy interest in marriage that included a right to marry a person of the same sex; and (6) DOMA did not draw classifications based on sex, and thus it did not restrict rights of one sex under the Equal Rights Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decisions of the trial courts.

State v. Walker


No. 76743-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


157 Wn.2d 307; 138 P.3d 113; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 524


January 24, 2006  
July 13, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1]
State v. Walker, 125 Wn. App. 1039, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 892 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress methamphetamine evidence on the ground that the underlying arrest was unlawful. The Court of Appeals of Washington reversed. Defendant petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested without a warrant for a misdemeanor involving the use of cannabis not occurring in the presence of an officer. The trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress methamphetamine evidence that was subsequently found in her purse, holding that the arrest was unlawful because the crime for which defendant was charged, Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.412(1), was not specific to cannibus and therefore the cannibus exception of Wash. Rev. Code § 10.31.100(1), which permitted warrantless misdemeanor arrests that were not committed in an officer's presence, did not apply. The appellate court reversed, holding that § 10.31.100(1) did apply and that sufficient probable cause supported defendant's arrest. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that § 10.31.100(1) did not violate Wash. Const. art. 1, § 7 and was constitutional given the legislative history in Washington, the original purpose of the requirement that the crime be committed in an officer's presence, the overwhelming practice of other jurisdictions allowing for limited warrantless misdemeanor arrests not committed in the presence of the arresting officer, and § 10.31.100(1)'s probable cause requirement.


OUTCOME: The order reversing the grant of defendant's motion to suppress was affirmed.

City of Sequim v. Malkasian


No. 74987-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


157 Wn.2d 251; 138 P.3d 943; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 536


January 13, 2005  
July 13, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
City of Sequim v. Malkasian, 119 Wn. App. 654, 79 P.3d 24, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 2675 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner individual challenged the judgment of the Court of Appeals, Washington, declining to decide the validity of an initiative, and finding that respondent City of Sequim, Washington, had standing postelection to bring an action under Wash. Rev. Code ch. 7.24 to challenge the initiative, and declining to award the individual attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: Prior to an election, the City sought a declaratory judgment that a proposed initiative was beyond the scope of initiative power of the residents of the City. The proposed initiative imposed additional requirements on revenue bonds issued by the City. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the individual and ordered the initiative placed on the ballot. The appellate court declined to stay the trial court's ruling and the election went forward. As a result, eight years later, no appellate court had reviewed the merits of the trial court's ruling. The City argued that the narrow issue of whether the initiative was beyond the scope of the initiative power granted to the residents of the City, an action it brought before the election was held, was not moot. The reviewing court held that the question of whether the initiative was beyond the scope of the initiative power was not mooted by the election because the election did not alter or expand the scope of the initiative power. Also, the initiative was outside of the initiative power, contravening the authority over bonds given to the City under Wash. Rev. Code ch. 35.41.


OUTCOME: The portion of the court of appeals' ruling dismissing the appeal was reversed, as well as the trial court's summary judgment in favor of the individual, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

McClarty v. Totem Elec.


No. 75024-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


157 Wn.2d 214; 137 P.3d 844; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 504; 18 Am. Disabilities Cas. (BNA) 217; 12 Accom. Disabilities Dec. (CCH) P12-148


January 19, 2005  
July 6, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1]
McClarty v. Totem Elec., 119 Wn. App. 453, 81 P.3d 901, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 3052 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Washington Court of Appeals reversed the summary judgment dismissal of a disparate treatment discrimination claim brought by respondent, a former employee. Petitioner employer sought review.


OVERVIEW: The Washington Law Against Discrimination (WLAD), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 49.60, forbade an employer from discharging an employee because of the presence of any sensory, mental, or physical disability, Wash. Rev. Code § 49.60.180(2). The supreme court determined that a plaintiff bringing suit under the WLAD established that he had a disability if he had a physical or mental impairment that substantially limited one or more of his major life activities, a record of such an impairment, or was regarded as having such an impairment. This was consistent with the term "disability" as utilized by the legislature and the history underlying the WLAD and accorded closely with the definition of the same term "disability" in the federal Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990, 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 12101-12209. The proposed definition would ensure that scarce judicial resources were available to those most in need of the WLAD's protections, rather than persons with receding hairlines. The trial court had to apply this definition to the employee's disparate treatment claim. Both parties prevailed on some issues. Neither party substantially prevailed, and the parties had to bear their own costs.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded for the trial court to apply the definition stated to the facts in the case.

In re Custody of Shields


No. 75263-0


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


157 Wn.2d 126; 136 P.3d 117; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 495


November 15, 2005.  
June 8, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1]
In re Custody of Shields, 120 Wn. App. 108, 84 P.3d 905, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 215 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother appealed the judgment of the Washington Supreme Court that upheld the award of custody of the mother's child to appellee stepmother.


OVERVIEW: The mother argued, inter alia, that the trial court impermissibly placed the evidentiary burden on her to prove that custody with her would be in the child's best interests. The supreme court agreed. Although the trial court referred to the actual detriment standard, the record reflected that it applied the "best interests of the child" standard. As a result, the trial court failed to accord the mother the benefit of the presumption that placement of the child with her, a fit parent, would be in the child's best interests and failed to place a heightened burden of proof upon the stepmother, a nonparent. Under the actual detriment standard, the trial court should have been focusing primarily on the effects on the child's long-term growth and development, should he be placed with his mother, and the burden should have been squarely placed on the stepmother. The test was not a balancing of all the aspects of each household and on the child's wishes; it was a focused test looking at actual detriment to the child if placed with an otherwise fit parent. Therefore, the trial court erred.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

283 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Cobra Roofing v. Labor & Indus.


No. 76064-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


157 Wn.2d 90; 135 P.3d 913; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 437; 21 OSHC (BNA) 1548


October 18, 2005, Oral Argument  
June 1, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court. Honorable William D Acey.
Cobra Roofing Serv. v. Dep't of Labor & Indus., 122 Wn. App. 402, 97 P.3d 17, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 1415 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Washington Court of Appeals granted judgment in favor of respondent Department of Labor and Industries (Department) by finding a violation of the Washington Industrial Safety and Health Act of 1973 (WISHA), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 49.17, to be a repeat violation under Wash. Rev. Code § 49.17.180(1) subject to a greater penalty, and denying attorney fees to petitioner corporation. The corporation appealed.


OVERVIEW: The corporation petitioned for review to determine whether the fall protection violation was a repeat and whether the Equal Access to Justice Act (EAJA), Wash. Rev. Code § 4.84.340, .350, and .360, applied to Board of Industrial Insurance Appeals (Board) proceedings. The Department petitioned for review on the question of whether the EAJA applied to court review of WISHA decisions. The supreme court found that the corporation did not present evidence to show that the violations involved different types of hazards. The Department properly determined that the employees were repeatedly exposed to the same type of hazard and thus subject to enhanced penalties. Because the statute specifically limited its applicability to judicial review and judicial review was generally limited to court review, the EAJA did not apply to administrative review before the Board. The attorney fees authorized by the EAJA did not apply to judicial review of agency decisions not authorized by the Administrative Procedures Act.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed as to the repeat penalty and on the basis that the EAJA did not authorize attorney fees for administrative review before the Board; the application of the EAJA to judicial review of WISHA decisions was reversed and attorney fees were denied.

 

284 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Luther


No. 76849-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


157 Wn.2d 63; 134 P.3d 205; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 428


February 7, 2006.  
May 18, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Luther v. Washington, 127 S. Ct. 440, 166 L. Ed. 2d 312, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 7849 (U.S., Oct. 16, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Luther, 125 Wn. App. 176, 105 P.3d 56, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 30 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following defendant's waiver of the right to a jury trial, a trial court convicted defendant of one count of attempted possession of depictions of minors engaged in sexually explicit conduct, in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 9.68A.070. The Court of Appeals (Washington) affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court first concluded that Wash. Rev. Code § 9.68A.070 was unquestionably constitutional as it contained a "knowingly" scienter requirement and did not sweep within the First Amendment's protected speech. The critical focus was on defendant's criminal intent and not on the fact that no minors were actually subjected to sexual exploitation or abuse. As for defendant's contention that the trial court impermissibly based its guilty verdict on computer images that defendant had a constitutional right to possess, the supreme court concluded that such fact did not render the conviction unconstitutional. That the images defendant actually possessed may not have been minors did not mean that defendant could not be convicted of attempted possession of depictions of minors engaged in sexually explicit conduct. Finally, the supreme court held that the evidence was sufficient to sustain the conviction. Among other things, defendant's cautioning an individual to be careful because the individual was "underage" and defendant's requests for pictures of the individual's genitals were evidence of defendant's attempt to obtain pictures of a minor engaged in sexually explicit conduct.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed.

 

285 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Hosier


No. 76785-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


157 Wn.2d 1; 133 P.3d 936; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 424


January 24, 2006.  
May 11, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Hosier, 124 Wn. App. 696, 103 P.3d 217, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 3047 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Washington affirming his conviction of two counts of communication with a minor for immoral purposes under former Wash. Rev. Code § 9.68A.090.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for writing sexually explicit messages on a pair of young girl's underpants and on two notes and leaving the items where they were found by young children and a young girl's father. On appeal, the court held that there was sufficient evidence to support defendant's conviction for leaving the two notes on the young girl's lawn because although the girl did not read the notes, the trial court correctly found that her father was a conduit for defendant's communication with the girl. The father testified that he told the girl that he found two sexually threatening notes in the front yard that described her. Defendant did not dispute that he wrote the notes with the requisite predatory purpose of promoting a minor's exposure and involvement in sexual misconduct or that he left the notes in a location that the girl was likely to find them. The evidence was also sufficient to support defendant's conviction for writing a message on the underpants, even though the children who found them were too young to read the message, because defendant cited no authority suggesting that a victim must understand the sexual nature of the communication.


OUTCOME: The judgment affirming defendant's conviction was affirmed.

 

286 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Potter


No. 77822-1 (consolidated with No. 77849-3)


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


156 Wn.2d 835; 132 P.3d 1089; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 351


March 21, 2006.  
April 27, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Potter, 129 Wn. App. 494, 119 P.3d 877, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 2347 (2005)
State v. Holmes, 129 Wn. App. 24, 117 P.3d 360, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 1955 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated case, police officers arrested defendants for the offense of driving while license suspended (DWLS). During searches incident to their arrests, officers found evidence of controlled substances. The State charged defendants with unlawful possession of controlled substances. The trial courts granted defendants' motions to suppress. The Washington Court of Appeals reversed. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the evidence of controlled substances should be suppressed because the searches incident to their arrests were invalid under Wash. Const. art. I, § 7. The State argued that the arrests were valid because the police had probable cause to believe defendants had committed the crime of DWLS based on the Department of Licensing (DOL) records indicating their licenses were suspended. The supreme court noted that police needed probable cause to believe defendants had committed the crime of DWLS. Information from the DOL records provided officers with reasonably trustworthy information sufficient to establish probable cause to believe defendants' licenses were suspended. The subsequent invalidation of some of the license suspension procedures did not void the probable cause that existed to arrest petitioners for the crime of DWLS.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed and remanded with instructions to admit the evidence obtained during the searches incident to defendants' valid arrests.

State v. Cantu


No. 76198-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


156 Wn.2d 819; 132 P.3d 725; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 350


October 25, 2005, Oral Argument  
April 20, 2006, Filed


NOTICE:

 [***1] As amended by order of the Supreme Court May 26, 2006.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court. Honorable Ken L Jorgensen.
State v. Cantu, 123 Wn. App. 404, 98 P.3d 106, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 2165 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant juvenile was convicted of residential burglary following allegations he entered his mother's home, broke into her dead bolt-locked bedroom door, and took some of her possessions. The Court of Appeals of Washington affirmed the conviction, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's mother lived with her boyfriend and daughter. One morning, the daughter called the police and told the police that defendant had just left after breaking into his mother's bedroom by kicking in the dead bolt locked door. The daughter told the police that defendant had taken items out of the mother's bedroom. The mother testified that defendant was not living with her when he broke into her room and did not have permission to go into her room. Defendant testified that he went into his mother's house to pick up clothes and accidentally broke the lock on the mother's bedroom door. The appellate court reversed the court of appeals and vacated defendant's conviction because it appeared to the appellate court that the trial judge applied a mandatory presumption under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.52.040 to find defendant's intent was criminal because the trial judge stated that under § 9A.53.040 defendant's unlawful presence in the mother's bedroom allowed him to infer that defendant acted with criminal intent and that defendant had not rebutted that inference.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed, the conviction was vacated, and the matter was remanded for further proceedings.

Trummel v. Mitchell


No. 75977-4


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


156 Wn.2d 653; 131 P.3d 305; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 266


June 23, 2005, Oral Argument  
March 30, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
Trummel v. Mitchell (In re Trummel), 121 Wn. App. 1078, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 1966 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant resident challenged the decision of the Court of Appeals (Washington) upholding the trial court's antiharassment orders against the resident filed by respondent, housing project administrator on behalf of the project, which resulted from the resident placing newsletters on apartment doors, disruptive behavior and name-calling at resident meetings, and allegations of the resident listening at other residents' doors.


OVERVIEW: The trial judge identified a number of the declarations of the staff and other residents alleging that the resident had yelled at the staff and residents, spied on them and frightened them. The order effectively evicted the resident from the project. The court held that the record contained substantial evidence relating to the resident's conduct, which had little or no free speech protections. His behavior in forcing his newsletter onto the residents was not constitutionally protected. However, while the antiharassment order was properly based on conduct and not on constitutionally protected activity, the court held that the restriction prohibiting the resident from contacting other project residents in any way and at any location was in excess of the trial court's authority. Also, the trial court abused its discretion under Wash. Rev. Code § 10.14.080 in making the modification to enforce the prior order restraining the resident from posting to the internet any personal identifying information of any current, former, or future staff member or resident because the term "surveillance" was not broad enough to encompass the conduct in the modification.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed as to the first antiharassment order providing a no conduct zone around the administrator and the project, but reversed as to restraining the resident from contacting nonparties off premises and in adding conte restrictions to the original antiharassment order. The court also reversed as to the finding of contempt.

 

289 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Larson v. Monorail Auth.


No. 77073-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


156 Wn.2d 752; 131 P.3d 892; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 267


September 27, 2005, Oral Argument  
March 30, 2006, Filed


NOTICE:

 [***1] As amended by order of the Supreme Court May 24, 2006.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 04-2-34399-2. Date filed: 04/14/2005. Judge signing: Hon. William L Downing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant vehicle owners challenged a decision from the Superior Court of King County (Washington), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondents, a monorail authority, a state agency, and its director, on claims arising from the imposition of a motor vehicle excise tax (MVET).


OVERVIEW: The owners argued that there was an impermissible delegation of local taxing authority under Wash. Const. art. XI, § 12 and Wash. Const. art. VII, § 9 because some of the authority's board members were appointed. They sought to enjoin the application of the MVET. After summary judgment was granted, the owners sought review. In affirming, the appellate court determined that, under the plain language of the Washington Constitution, the Washington Legislature was not limited in its ability to delegate taxing authority. The owners' reliance on Amalgamated Transit Union Local 587 was misplaced. Delegation of taxing power to nonrepresentative entities was permitted since the Legislature clearly defined the purpose of the delegation, and there were adequate procedural safeguards established. The appellate court rejected the arguments that the MVET was an unlawful property tax and that the authority lacked jurisdiction to impose the MVET because it was not imposed on a proper taxing event in its taxing district. Finally, the MVET did not substantially depart from the petition approved by the voters.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

State v. Cross


No. 71267-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


156 Wn.2d 580; 132 P.3d 80; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 268



March 30, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Cross v. Washington, 549 U.S. 1022, 127 S. Ct. 559, 166 L. Ed. 2d 415, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 8433 (2006)
Post-conviction relief denied at, in part In re Pers. Restraint of Cross, 178 Wn.2d 519, 309 P.3d 1186, 2013 Wash. LEXIS 766 (2013)
Post-conviction relief denied at In re Pers. Restraint of Cross, 180 Wn.2d 664, 327 P.3d 660, 2014 Wash. LEXIS 481 (2014)
Habeas corpus proceeding at, Stay denied by Cross v. Holbrook, 2016 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 52355 (W.D. Wash., Apr. 18, 2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of King County (Washington) that imposed the jury's sentence of death upon defendant's conviction for three counts of aggravated first-degree murder and one count of kidnapping for the stabbing death of his wife and two of his three daughters. Defendant had entered an Alford plea specifically denying that he premeditated the murders after he withdrew his not guilty plea by reason of insanity.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the constitutionality of the death penalty, and the sufficiency of the evidence to support his death sentence under Wash. Rev. Code § 10.95.030. Defendant also raised claims of error regarding the exclusion of jurors, the lack of a premeditation instruction, conflicts with his counsel regarding his defense strategy. The court held that sufficient evidence supported defendant's death sentence because he had a history of domestic violence and brutally killed his wife and two daughters as part of a common scheme. Also the sentence was proportional and defendant failed to show the death penalty was unconstitutional. The court found that the trial court properly excluded jurors for their inability to follow the law and not their views on the death penalty. Also defendant failed to establish that he was denied the benefit of his Alford plea by the lack of an unsought premeditation instruction; that he was denied the right to counsel by the conflict with his counsel as to his defense strategy. The court held that evidence about defendant's mental health was properly presented to the jury, as well as, the challenged crime scene photographs and custodial statements.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's death sentence.

 

291 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Ermels


No. 76665-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


156 Wn.2d 528; 131 P.3d 299; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 262


November 15, 2005.  
March 16, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Ermels, 125 Wn. App. 195, 104 P.3d 67, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 32 (2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Washington Court of Appeals, which upheld a trial court's imposition of an exceptional sentence under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.535.


OVERVIEW: Defendant entered a guilty plea to manslaughter in the second degree. As part of the agreement, defendant stipulated to facts supporting an exceptional sentence based on victim vulnerability, and he stipulated that there was a legal basis for an exceptional sentence. He also waived the right to appeal the basis for and propriety of the sentence. Defendant later sought review, and the court of appeals affirmed. On appeal, the reviewing court determined that the exceptional sentence did not depend on improper Blakely fact finding. The trial court was not required to find other aggravating factors supporting the sentence given defendant's stipulations. Therefore, the case only presented an issue as to whether the plea was knowing, intelligent, and voluntary. Because the portions of the plea agreement stipulating to the facts supporting the exceptional sentence and the legal basis for the exceptional sentence were indivisible from the rest of defendant's plea agreement, he was unable to challenge the exceptional sentence without challenging the entire plea. Likewise, defendant was unable to challenge the validity of his appeal waiver without challenging his entire plea.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

 

292 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State ex rel. P.D.C. v. W.E.A.


No. 74268-5, No. 74316-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


156 Wn.2d 543; 130 P.3d 352; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 260; 179 L.R.R.M. 2518; 153 Lab. L. Rep. (CCH) 60276


May 27, 2004.  
March 16, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari granted by Davenport v. Wash. Educ. Ass'n, 548 U.S. 942, 127 S. Ct. 35, 165 L. Ed. 2d 1014, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 5417 (2006)
US Supreme Court certiorari granted by Washington v. Wash. Educ. Ass'n, 548 U.S. 942, 127 S. Ct. 35, 165 L. Ed. 2d 1014, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 5418 (2006)
Vacated by, Remanded by Davenport v. Wash. Educ. Ass'n, 551 U.S. 177, 127 S. Ct. 2372, 168 L. Ed. 2d 71, 2007 U.S. LEXIS 7722 (U.S., 2007)
Appeal after remand at, Remanded by Davenport v. Wash. Educ. Ass'n, 147 Wn. App. 704, 197 P.3d 686, 2008 Wash. App. LEXIS 2915 (Wash. Ct. App., Dec. 12, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: State ex rel. PDC v. WEA, 117 Wn. App. 625, 71 P.3d 244, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 1225 (2003)
Davenport v. Wash. State Educ. Ass'n, 117 Wn. App. 1035, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 1947 (2003)



DISPOSITION:  The court affirmed the decisions of the court of appeals.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two consolidated cases, the State and plaintiff employees appealed the decision of the Court of Appeals (Washington) holding that Wash. Rev. Code § 42.17.760 was unconstitutional. The statute governed a labor union's ability to use agency shop fees, the fees paid by educational employees who were not union members. The cases stemmed from a complaint filed against defendant education union with the Washington Public Disclosure Commission (PDC).


OVERVIEW: The union was the exclusive bargaining agent for state educational employees. Both members and nonmembers had to contribute to the union for the costs related to collective bargaining. The State claimed that the union violated § 42.17.760 because it failed to get the affirmative authorization of nonmembers before using nonmembers' fees for political purposes. The court affirmed and held that § 42.17.760 did not require written authorization, and the court presumed that written authorization was not what was intended. The union was prevented from spending any portion of a nonmember's agency fees for political causes without the affirmative authorization of the nonmember. A presumption of dissent violated the First Amendment rights of both members and nonmembers. Written permission was not required. Because there was no compelled support if the union utilized the Hudson procedures, there was no governmental interference with First Amendment rights of nonmembers for § 42.17.760 to protect against. The union engaged in expressive activity and § 42.17.760's opt-in requirement significantly burdened the union's association with agency fee payers with regard to its political speech.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decisions of the court of appeals.

State v. Shafer


No. 75605-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


156 Wn.2d 381; 128 P.3d 87; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 178


June 30, 2005.  
February 9, 2006, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Shafer v. Washington, 127 S. Ct. 553, 166 L. Ed. 2d 409, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 8385 (U.S., Nov. 6, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Pierce County, Washington, convicting him of rape of a child in the first degree.


OVERVIEW: At trial, certain statements that the alleged child victim made to her mother and a family friend were admitted pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.120, notwithstanding the fact that the child did not testify at the trial and defendant had no prior opportunity to cross-examine the child. Defendant appealed, arguing that Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.120 facially or as applied, runs afoul of the constitutions of the United States and the State of Washington. The reviewing court ruled that the statements were properly admitted. Where non-testimonial hearsay statements of a child were at issue, the statements were admissible if there was compliance with Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.120 and the Ryan reliability factors. Defendant had not challenged any of the trial court's findings of fact in support of its conclusion that the child was unavailable and that her statements were reliable. The requirements for admission under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.120 comported with the general approach utilized to test hearsay against confrontation guaranties under U.S. Const. amend. VI and Wash. Const. art. I, § 22.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

294 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Johnston


No. 76544-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


156 Wn.2d 355; 127 P.3d 707; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 114


October 11, 2005, Oral Argument  
January 26, 2006, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Johnston, 123 Wn. App. 1044, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 3245 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of threats to bomb or injure property in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 9.61.160, for threatening to bomb an airport. The Court of Appeals, Washington, affirmed defendant's conviction, and defendant sought discretionary review, which was granted.


OVERVIEW: Over defense counsel's objection, the jury was instructed that "threat" meant to communicate, directly or indirectly, the intent to wrongfully cause physical damage to the property of a person other than the actor, and defendant's proposed instruction defining a "true threat" was denied. The court of appeals found that Wash. Rev. Code § 9.61.160(1) was neither overbroad nor limited to unprotected speech, and the jury was properly instructed. The supreme court ruled that Wash. Rev. Code § 9.61.160 should be construed to apply only to true threats, and the jury was improperly instructed. The jury instructions given at trial were insufficient to ensure a constitutional verdict, and the instructional error could not be deemed to be harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. The jury must be instructed that a conviction under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.61.160 required a true threat and must be instructed on the meaning of a true threat. A "true threat" was a statement wherein a reasonable person would foresee that the statement would be interpreted as a serious expression of an intention to inflict bodily harm upon or to take the life of another individual.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed and the case was remanded for a new trial under proper jury instructions.

In re Discipline of Haley


No. 200,153-0


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


156 Wn.2d 324; 126 P.3d 1262; 2006 Wash. LEXIS 115


May 10, 2005.  
January 26, 2006, File


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Related proceeding at In re Discipline of Haley, 157 Wn.2d 398, 138 P.3d 1044, 2006 Wash. LEXIS 601 (Wash., July 27, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney challenged the recommendation of appellee Disciplinary Board of the Washington State Bar Association (Board), that he serve two six-month suspensions concurrently for allegedly violating Wash. R. Prof. Conduct 4.2(a) (count 2) and Wash. R. Prof. Conduct 1.7 (count 3).


OVERVIEW: Counts 2 and 3 of the complaint against the attorney arose out of separate sets of events that occurred in 1996-1997 and 1988-1991, respectively. Count 2 alleged that the attorney had improper contact with a represented party. The issue was whether a lawyer who was representing himself or herself was, in the words of Wash. R. Prof. Conduct 4.2(a), "representing a client." The supreme court ruled that under Wash. R. Prof. Conduct 4.2(a), a lawyer acting pro se was prohibited from contacting a party represented by counsel in the matter. Wash. R. Prof. Conduct 4.2(a), was impermissibly vague as to its applicability to pro se attorneys, the supreme court's interpretation of the rule would be applied prospectively only, and the alleged violation in count 2 would be dismissed. As to count 3, the attorney conceded that conflicts of interest arose out of the series of events. The supreme court ruled that although the presumptive sanction for knowing violating Wash. R. Prof. Conduct 1.7 was suspension, the mitigating factors, in particular the extensive delay in both reporting and prosecution, demanded a more lenient sanction.


OUTCOME: The alleged violation of count 2 was dismissed, and the sanction for the violation of count 3 was a reprimand.

Sewer Dist. v. Home Bldg.


No. 76062-4


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 858; 123 P.3d 823; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 923


September 13, 2005.  
November 23, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
Holmes Harbor Sewer Dist. v. Frontier Bank, 123 Wn. App. 45, 96 P.3d 442, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 1915 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The trial court held that monthly fees imposed by respondent sewer district on unimproved lots were authorized by Wash. Rev. Code § 57.08.081(1), but were also property taxes and thus unconstitutional because they lacked proportionality. The Court of Appeals of Washington affirmed the statutory issue, but reversed the trial court and concluded the charges were permissible regulatory fees. Petitioner landowner sought review.


OVERVIEW: The district formed a utility local improvement district (ULID) to finance the sewer system through bonds and special assessments levied against property within the ULID. The district hired an appraiser to determine the specific benefit each parcel would receive from sewer service, and the district charged a special assessment to the property owners. Following completion of the sewer system, the district adopted a resolution that governed the use of the system. Under the resolution, the district could compel property owners to connect to the sewer when a dwelling or other structure used by humans was situated on any lot within the district. Under the resolution, no guaranteed right to connect to the sewer system was created. The landowner had not developed any of the lots it owned. The state supreme court reversed the court of appeals and held that given that the lots at issue were not improved, were not connected to the sewer system, and had no guaranteed right to connect upon improvement, sewer service was not available to the lots under Wash. Rev. Code § 57.08.081(1). The matter was remanded to the trial court to enter summary judgment for the landowner.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed, and the matter was remanded to the trial court to enter summary judgment for the landowner.

State v. Elmore


No. 75637-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 758; 123 P.3d 72; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 915


May 17, 2005.  
November 10, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: On remand at State v. Elmore, 2010 Wash. App. LEXIS 479 (Wash. Ct. App., Mar. 9, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Elmore, 121 Wn. App. 747, 90 P.3d 1110, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 1082 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State filed a petition for review of a decision of the Court of Appeals (Washington), which reversed defendant's convictions for first degree murder, first degree burglary, first degree kidnapping, second degree assault, and conspiracy to commit robbery in the second degree, all of which were based on her complicity in the invasion of the home of a severely disabled man and the shooting death of one of his caregivers.


OVERVIEW: Defendant enlisted friends to rob the residence of a quadriplegic man. The man's caregiver was shot during the robbery and died. On remand, during deliberations, the judge received two notes from jurors stating that one juror was refusing to follow the instructions. The judge questioned the presiding juror and the juror complained of, then dismissed the alleged uncooperative juror. The court affirmed the reversal, and held that the trial court departed from the general guidelines because it first should have reinstructed and allowed the jury to continue deliberations. Also, a more balanced investigation into the allegations should have been conducted. The court affirmed the court of appeals' adoption of the "any reasonable possibility" standard that a juror could not be dismissed where there was any reasonable possibility that his views stemmed from an evaluation of the sufficiency of the evidence. The trial court's finding recognized that the juror in question had expressed some valid disagreement with the other jurors' views of the evidence and when weighing the conflicting evidence, there was no indication that the trial court applied the heightened evidentiary standard adopted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals' reversal, and remanded for a new trial.

Sheehan v. Transit Auth.


No. 76036-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 790; 123 P.3d 88; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 919


May 19, 2005.  
November 10, 2005, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers sought review of a judgment of the King County Superior Court, Washington, which dismissed on summary judgment their claims challenging motor vehicle excise taxes levied and collected by respondent transportation authorities.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers argued, inter alia, that the taxes were unconstitutional under Wash. Const. art. VII, § 5. The instant court held that the taxes imposed were not constitutionally or statutorily prohibited. The plain language of Washington's tax authorization and collection statutes satisfied the constitutional requirement that the authorities' use of vehicle registration as the taxable event was done in pursuance of law. The authorities' vehicle taxes satisfied the requirement that excise taxes must be voluntary, as there was no requirement that residents of the taxing districts own or continue to own a motor vehicle. The instant court also concluded that the legislature authorized the excise taxes be collected annually. The taxes were not preempted by Wash. Rev. Code § 46.08.010. The authorities had not undertaken the issuance of vehicle licenses, as prohibited by § 46.08.010. Instead, the authorities implemented a duty authorized excise tax on the privilege of relicensing a vehicle for use on the public roadways. The instant court also concluded that the trial court did not err in denying the taxpayers' motion for class certification on the basis that the motion was moot.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in all respects.

 

300 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



In re Parentage of L.B.


No. 75626-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 679; 122 P.3d 161; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 861


February 15, 2005, Oral Argument  
November 3, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
U.S. Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion denied by Britain v. Carvin, 126 S. Ct. 2021, 164 L. Ed. 2d 806, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 3701 (U.S., May 15, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY: Carvin v. Britain (In re Parentage of L.B.), 121 Wn. App. 460, 89 P.3d 271, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 833 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent former lesbian partner filed a petition for the establishment of parentage of petitioner mother's child. The Washington Court of Appeals reversed the trial court's dismissal of the petition, recognizing a common law de facto parent status in Washington. The appellate court also reinstated the former partner's alternative petition for third party visitation pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 26.10.160(3). The mother petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The instant court concluded that the former partner, who was neither a biological nor adoptive parent of the child, had standing under Washington law to petition the courts for a determination of coparentage with regard to the child. It noted that the equitable power of the courts to adjudicate relationships between children and families was well recognized. It also observed that the legislature had evinced no intent to preclude the application of an equitable remedy in circumstances such as these. The instant court held that Washington's common law recognized the status of de facto parents and granted them standing to petition for a determination of the rights and responsibilities that accompanied legal parentage in the state. The former partner was given the opportunity to present evidence to the trial court sufficient to establish her status as a de facto parent of the child and if successful to obtain the rights and responsibilities attendant to parentage. However, the former partner did not have standing to assert rights to visitation with the child under § 26.10.160(3) because that statute was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: Finding no constitutional infirmities in recognizing de facto parents, the instant court affirmed the appellate court on this issue and remanded to the trial court for a determination of whether the former partner was the child's de facto parent. However, the instant court reversed the appellate court's alternative holding that the former partner could petition for visitation.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mayor appealed a decision by the Superior Court of Spokane County, Washington, that authorized a recall effort to proceed against him by finding the charge in the recall petition to be legally sufficient.


OVERVIEW: A citizen filed a recall petition and charged, inter alia, that the mayor had committed recallable misfeasance by offering an opportunity to obtain an internship with his office as part of an effort to pursue a sexual relationship with a young person he met while on-line in an internet chat room. In re Seattle Monorail Auth.


No. 76462-0


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 612; 121 P.3d 1166; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 859


March 17, 2005, Oral Argument  
October 20, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at, Remanded by, in part In re Petition of Seattle Popular Monorail Auth., 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 2105 (Wash. Ct. App., July 23, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner challenged an order of a Superior Court (Washington) adjudicating public use and necessity that authorized respondent Seattle Monorail Project (SMP) to condemn its property to build a monorail station.


OVERVIEW: The parties stipulated that the proposed use for the property was a public use. The trial court ruled that because eminent domain procedures of Wash. Rev. Code ch. 8.12 governed condemnation actions, SMP had statutory authority to condemn property. The court affirmed and held that powers reasonably necessary to carry out a grant of eminent domain power could be inferred from the authorizing statute or other statutes and that by implication, ch. 8.12 applied to the SMP. Further the SMP properly followed the condemnation method prescribed for cities in ch. 8.12. The owner pointed to no authority that required a condemning authority to have a public use planned for property forever. Moreover, providing that decisions as to the type of property interest to be acquired were reviewed under the deferential legislative standard, the SMP's determination to condemn a fee interest was a legislative question. The SMP's contentions that it needed all of the property for a substantial period of time to build and construct a monorail station was supported by the record. Given the absence of fraud, the determination to condemn a fee interest in the entire property was necessary to the public use.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.

 

303 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Cubias


No. 75109-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 549; 120 P.3d 929; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 800


February 10, 2005, Oral Argument  
October 6, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Cubias, 119 Wn. App. 1018, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 3337 (2003).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of three counts of attempted murder in the first degree in the Superior Court of King County, Washington. The trial judge concluded that the offenses arose from "separate and distinct criminal conduct" and, consequently, imposed consecutive sentences on all counts. Defendant appealed, asserting that the imposition of consecutive sentences was unconstitutional in light of Blakely v. Washington and Apprendi v. New Jersey.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that because the imposition of consecutive sentences had the effect of increasing his total sentence beyond the statutory maximum sentence for any one of the counts, he was denied his constitutional right to have a jury determine all of the facts necessary to support consecutive sentencing. However, under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.589(1)(b), sentencing courts were required to impose consecutive sentences when a defendant was convicted of two or more serious violent offenses that arose from separate and distinct criminal conduct. The appellate court held that consecutive sentences were not prohibited where a sentencing court's determination that two crimes were separate offenses under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.589(1)(b) did not operate to elevate either crime to the equivalent of a greater offense merely because it resulted in two standard range sentences running consecutively. A trial court's imposition of consecutive sentences under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.589(1)(b) that did not increase the penalty for any single underlying offense beyond the statutory maximum provided for that offense did not run afoul of the decisions of the United States Supreme Court.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed.

 

304 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Louis


No. 75151-0


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 563; 120 P.3d 936; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 803


February 10, 2005, Oral Argument  
October 6, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Louis, 119 Wn. App. 1080, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 527 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, King County (Washington) convicted defendant of first degree robbery and first degree kidnapping. Defendant appealed. On appeal, defendant questioned whether his convictions violated principles of double jeopardy and, alternatively, whether his kidnapping convictions merged into his robbery convictions. He also questioned whether his consecutive sentences for kidnapping were unconstitutional in light of Blakely.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was part of a trio that robbed a jewelry store. Defendant was convicted of kidnapping and robbing the two jewelry store owners, and the jury returned a special verdict that defendant was armed with a firearm during the commission of each kidnapping and robbery. Defendant's total sentence was 228 months in prison. Review was limited to determining whether the legislature intended to allow multiple punishments for criminal conduct that violated both the robbery and kidnapping statutes. Applying the same evidence test, the court concluded that the robbery and kidnapping charges against defendant were not the same in fact or law and that double jeopardy principles did not preclude separate convictions for robbery and kidnapping. Defendant could be punished separately for robbery and kidnapping. Apprendi and Blakely had no application to consecutive sentencing decisions so long as each individual sentence remained within the statutory maximum for that particular offense, as was the case here.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

305 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Habitat Watch v. Skagit County


No. 74073-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 397; 120 P.3d 56; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 795



September 22, 2005, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizen's group appealed a decision of the trial court (Washington), which found against the group as a matter of law in the group's Land Use Petition Act (LUPA), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 36.70C action for declaratory and injunctive relief challenging a grading permit issued by a county to respondent tribe and the validity of special use permit extensions pertaining to a golf course project.


OVERVIEW: The group participated in the initial public hearings on the project, but the county failed to provide notice or a public hearing for the permit extensions and the group did not learn of the continued existence of the project until seven years after the last public hearing. The court held that under Wash. Rev. Code § 36.70C.040(2), the trial court was correct to dismiss the group's challenges to the permit extensions because the LUPA petition was filed over 21 days after the extensions were made available to the group. Further, in challenging the grading permit, the group challenged the validity of the special use permit and its extensions, and because appeal of the special use permit and its extensions were time barred, the group could not collaterally attack them through its challenge to the grading permit. Also, the trial court correctly found the grading permit was valid, and the group had not challenged the factual findings; the tribe had submitted an extensive application for the permit, retained a surveyor to work on the property, obtained a forest practice conversion permit, put into place an excavator, chain saws, and logging equipment, and removed trees from the property.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court.

State v. Kinneman


No. 76051-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 272; 119 P.3d 350; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 719


June 30, 2005.  
September 8, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Kinneman, 122 Wn. App. 850, 95 P.3d 1277, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 1884 (2004).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the Washington Court of Appeals, which partially affirmed a trial court's decision regarding restitution in a theft case.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of several theft counts relating to the personal use of escrow funds involved in a real estate transaction. After restitution was ordered, both defendant and the State sought review. The court of appeals held that the State was entitled to appeal, the trial court abused its discretion by ordering restitution to one victim in the total amount stolen, and the order of restitution to another victim was not an abuse of discretion. Defendant then sought review. In affirming the court of appeals, the court determined that the State was entitled to appeal the restitution order, asserting that the trial court abused its discretion. Further, Apprendi and Blakely did not apply to restitution under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.753 because the decision to order restitution was discretionary. Finally, the court of appeals properly remanded the case for an evidentiary hearing on one victim's losses and to determine whether restitution should be imposed for attorney fees and costs incurred by all victims allegedly as a direct result of the thefts. There was evidence that one victim had recovered some of the amount stolen, and the escrow funds were not his property at any rate.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals' decision was affirmed.

 

307 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Coppernoll v. Reed


No. 76818-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 290; 119 P.3d 318; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 717


June 30, 2005, Oral Argument  
September 8, 2005, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court, King County (Washington), dismissed petitioner families' action to enjoin the secretary of state from placing three sections of Initiative 330 (I-330) on the general election ballot. The families sought discretionary review, seeking reversal of the order.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied the families' motion for summary judgment and granted the intervenor medical groups' cross-motion to dismiss. The families argued that certain sections of the initiative would be unconstitutional if enacted, and thus exceeded the scope of the legislative power under Wash. Const. art. II, § 1. The families cast their unconstitutionality claim as a challenge to whether I-330 was validly within the legislative power, whereas respondent secretary of state and intervenor medical groups labeled the claim as a challenge to the substantive validity of I-330. Preelection review of an initiative was appropriate only when it was clear that an initiative exceeded the scope of the broad legislative power under art. II, § 1. The families' claim as to the scope of the legislative power was a pretext for a challenge to the possible constitutionality of several sections of I-330. Because the families offered no theory under which I-330 exceeded the legislative power, other than this allegation of some sections unconstitutionality, the families' claims were not justiciable. I-330 did not exceed the scope of the legislative power.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

308 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Tiffany Family Trust v. City of Kent


No. 74526-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 225; 119 P.3d 325; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 718


September 28, 2004.  
September 8, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
Tiffany Family Trust Corp. v. City of Kent, 118 Wn. App. 1003, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 2405 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed a trial court order dismissing plaintiff property owner's suit claiming that a local improvement district (LID) assessment by respondent city was an unconstitutional taking and a violation of due process. The instant court granted the property owner's petition for discretionary review.


OVERVIEW: The instant court held that the trial court properly dismissed each of the property owner's five causes of action because the LID assessment was conclusively correct once the time period for challenging it had passed. The property owner did not challenge the validity or amount of the LID assessment until over one year after the assessment roll was confirmed. The property owner was required to use the prescribed statutory procedure set forth in Wash. Rev. Code § 35.44.190 for challenging the assessment but failed to do so. Accordingly, the assessment was conclusively correct and could not in any manner be contested or questioned by any person. Because the trial court lacked jurisdiction to collaterally review the propriety of the LID assessment and because the property owner's state and federal constitutional claims were premised on its assertion that the assessment was in excess of special benefits and as such unreasonable, arbitrary, capricious, and unduly oppressive, its claims were without substance. The trial court lacked jurisdiction to grant the relief the property owner sought - a redetermination of the assessment amount.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment was affirmed in all respects.

Brown v. State


No. 75878-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 254; 119 P.3d 341; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 720


June 28, 2005.  
September 8, 2005, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the State of Washington and school districts, appealed the judgment from the Superior Court of King County (Washington), which, in a suit brought by plaintiff teacher, on behalf of teachers and taxpayers, ruled that the State violated Initiative 732 and Wash. Const. art. IX, §§ 1-2 when it decided to fund a maximum of two learning improvement days for the 2002-03 school year instead of the usual three.


OVERVIEW: The State eliminated one of three funded learning improvement days. A teacher filed a successful suit contending that the decision violated the state constitution and Initiative 732, which provided base salaries for teachers. On appeal, the court reversed, finding that it had never held that the Basic Education Act, Wash. Rev. Code §§ 28A.150.200 through 28A.150.510, defined the scope of the State's constitutional duty to provide education. The court then considered the subject appropriation bills and concluded that they did not contain an explicit declaration that the legislature had found learning improvement days to be a part of basic education. Furthermore, the court found that the headers and the disclaimer in the appropriation bills were insufficient to make the program part of basic education. Lastly, the court held that the teacher had not shown beyond a reasonable doubt that the legislature violated the constitution by reducing the number of days it was willing to fund learning improvement days. Accordingly, the court held that the teacher had failed to prove that such days were part of the basic education the State was constitutionally required to provide to all children.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment.

 

310 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Willman v. WUTC


No. 75821-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


154 Wn.2d 801; 117 P.3d 343; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 670



August 11, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
Willman v. Wash. Utils. & Transp. Comm'n, 122 Wn. App. 194, 93 P.3d 909, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 1308 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Yakima County Superior Court, Washington, dismissed petitioner residents' petition against respondents, the Washington Utilities and Transportation Commission (WUTC) and utility corporations, claiming that the WUTC improperly allowed utilities to take costs imposed upon them by the Yakima Indian Nation (Nation) and pass them on to the bills of all customers living within the reservation. The court of appeals affirmed. The residents appealed.


OVERVIEW: The residents argued that the WUTC unlawfully allowed the utilities to pass the Nation's tax on to their utility bills. The appellate court found that it had to primarily apply Washington state law, thereby requiring utilities to pay only prudent expenses. The WUTC did not act in an arbitrary and capricious manner in determining the tax was valid and thus a prudent expense. For the purposes of Indian law taxation of utilities, a tax that was not "clearly invalid" was a prudent expense. The WUTC treated a franchise fee differently from a tax. A utility could pass a tax on to the bills of taxpayers within the taxing jurisdiction. A franchise fee was considered a cost of doing business and could be distributed only as an expense to all ratepayers served, systemwide. Wash. Rev. Code § 80.280.090 was designed to ensure that all ratepayers in the same area paid the same rate and were not unfairly discriminated against in that manner. In the reservation, all residents were receiving the same utility services for the same price. Since none of the residents' claims were valid, the utilities' argument that the residents failed to join the Nation did not need to be addressed.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

311 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



City of Redmond v. Bagby


No. 73249-3


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 59; 117 P.3d 1126; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 664


March 17, 2005, Oral Argument  
August 11, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at City of Bremerton v. Hawkins, 155 Wn.2d 107, 117 P.3d 1132, 2005 Wash. LEXIS 665 (Wash., Aug. 11, 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent drivers were charged in the King County, Washington, District Court with driving while license suspended. They moved to dismiss the charges, arguing that they were unconstitutionally denied a pre-suspension or post-suspension hearing. The county court granted the motion, holding that the drivers' due process rights were violated, and the Superior Court of King County, Washington, affirmed. Petitioner city appealed.


OVERVIEW: The drivers had been convicted for reckless driving, vehicular homicide, eluding police, and multiple driving under the influence convictions. Under the statutes for those crimes, the Washington Department of Licensing was required to revoke the drivers' licenses for varying lengths of time. The drivers had successfully challenged the revocations on the basis that they were denied sufficient due process. While the city acknowledged that a person had a right to due process prior to the suspension of his or her driver's license, it argued that sufficient due process had been afforded the drivers under the statutes at issue. The supreme court noted that sufficient due process requirements existed for those who had their driver's license suspended based on a criminal conviction. The supreme court held that statutes that required a mandatory suspension based on a conviction provided a heightened government interest in highway safety and a decreased likelihood of erroneous depravation. The supreme court concluded that the statutes were constitutional as applied to the drivers.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed and remanded.

City of Bremerton v. Hawkins


No. 76134-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 107; 117 P.3d 1132; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 665


March 17, 2005, Oral Argument  
August 11, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: City of Redmond v. Bagby, 155 Wn.2d 59, 117 P.3d 1126, 2005 Wash. LEXIS 664 (Wash., Aug. 11, 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant driver, a minor, had been convicted in the Bremerton Municipal Court, Washington, trial court for possession of marijuana, and his driver's license was revoked. He was later convicted on five counts of driving while license suspended. Thereafter, he sought to have his five convictions vacated, which the trial court denied. The driver appealed.


OVERVIEW: The driver was convicted of being a minor in possession of drugs, and his sentence included a mandatory revocation of his driver's license under Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.265. No separate pre-suspension hearing was provided. He was then convicted on five counts of driving while license suspended. He then filed motions to vacate his driving while license suspended convictions, which were denied. The appellate court noted that defendants who had their license revoked under either Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.270 or Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.265 did not have a constitutional right to a hearing by the Washington Department of Licensing prior to suspension. The appellate court held that although a driver's license was a substantial private interest, erroneous deprivation was unlikely because a defendant personally appeared before a judge for imposition of the suspension and there was a significant government interest in the license revocation of convicted criminals. The appellate court concluded that a conviction that resulted in a mandatory license suspension for a minor, under Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.265, satisfied constitutional due process requirements without a separate hearing.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Born v. Thompson


No. 74126-3


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


154 Wn.2d 749; 117 P.3d 1098; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 660


June 30, 2004, Oral Argument  
August 4, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court, Honorable Glenna S Hall.
Born v. Thompson, 117 Wn. App. 57, 69 P.3d 343, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 968 (2003).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the district court determined that defendant was incompetent and that the charge pending against him alleged a violent act, it committed him for mental health treatment under Wash. Rev. Code § 10.77.090(1)(d)(i). He filed a habeas corpus petition, which the superior court denied. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant sought further review.


OVERVIEW: When a bus driver told defendant to exit the bus, he refused, raised his fist, and cocked it back as if to hit the driver. He was charged with unlawful bus conduct for exhibiting harassing behavior, a misdemeanor. Based solely on the incident report, the district court committed defendant on the basis that he was incompetent and that the pending charge alleged a violent act. Defendant argued that the State failed to prove by clear and convincing evidence that the charge alleged a violent act. The court of appeals held that the standard was a preponderance of the evidence and that the State met its burden. The supreme court disagreed. Due process required that the standard of proof under § 10.77.090(1)(d)(i) was clear and convincing evidence. Though defendant was no longer in custody, the matter was not moot since the finding that he was charged with a violent act had potential collateral consequences sufficient to constitute restraint under Wash. Rev. Code § 7.36.010. The incident report did not contain clear and convincing evidence that defendant's threatened behavior, if carried out, would have resulted in physical pain or injury, illness, or an impairment of physical condition.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded to the superior court with directions to grant the petition for habeas corpus to the extent of vacating its finding of a violent act.

 

314 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Bosteder v. City of Renton


No. 74934-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


155 Wn.2d 18; 117 P.3d 316; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 655



July 28, 2005, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of King County (Washington) that granted summary judgment to defendants, city and city code enforcement team members, and dismissed his action alleging trespass and violations of 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 based on his claim that a noncriminal administrative search warrant for building or housing code violations issued by a municipal judge was invalid under the Fourth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found the warrant was valid under the Fourth Amendment. On appeal, the court held that the warrant was invalid under the Fourth Amendment because the municipal judge lacked the authority to issue an administrative search warrant for noncriminal building or housing code violations absent authorization by statute or court rule. Therefore the court reversed the dismissal of the § 1983 claim against the city and remanded for a determination of whether an official city policy or custom caused the team members to violate the owner's Fourth Amendment rights. However, the court affirmed the dismissal of the owner's claim against the individual team members on the ground that they were qualifiedly immune under § 1983. As to the owner's trespass claim, the court held that he failed to comply with the 60-day claim filing requirement of Wash. Rev. Code § 4.96.020(4) and that § 4.96.020(4) applied to suits against individual municipal employees for acts committed within the scope of their employment, but that the team members were not completely relieved of personal liability, because they could still be answerable for trespass for acts outside of the scope of their employment.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the dismissal of the property owner's § 1983 claim against the city but affirmed the dismissal of his § 1983 claim against the team members because they were qualifiedly immune. The court affirmed the dismissal of the trespass claim against the city and the team members for acts within the scope of their employment but reversed as to the members' alleged acts outside the scope of their employment. The case was remanded.

 

315 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



United States v. Hoffman


No. 76629-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


154 Wn.2d 730; 116 P.3d 999; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 657


May 19, 2005, Oral Argument  
July 28, 2005, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court August 25, 2005.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Answer conformed to United States v. Manning, 434 F. Supp. 2d 988, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 38835 (E.D. Wash., 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Five questions of law related to the Washington Cleanup Priority Act (CPA), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 70.105E, were certified to the supreme court by the United States District Court for the Eastern District of Washington after defendant, State of Washington, sought and obtained a temporary restraining order against plaintiff United States in the district court.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that for a material to qualify as "mixed waste" under the CPA definition, it had to have both a nonradioactive component and a radioactive component. There was no requirement that the materials defined as "hazardous substances" under Wash. Rev. Code § 70.105D.020(7) be released or pose a threat of release. The CPA was also devoid of any such a requirement. The CPA included some materials that did not "designate" as dangerous waste. The definition of "mixed waste" encompassed materials that were not "solid wastes" under the Resource Conservation and Recovery Act of 1976 (RCRA), 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 6901-6992k, to the extent that a hazardous substance, as defined in Wash. Rev. Code § 70.105D.020(7)(b)-(d), failed to qualify as a solid waste for lack of being discarded or otherwise abandoned, recycled, or inherently waste-like. The operation of the CPA did not prevent the intrasite transfer of waste. The naval exemption was limited to those materials specifically listed, but did not extend to other materials that qualified as hazardous substances. Physical inspection of each and every material disposed was not required.


OUTCOME: The questions of law were certified to the supreme court.

 

316 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



City of Walla Walla v. Greene


No. 75108-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


154 Wn.2d 722; 116 P.3d 1008; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 656



July 28, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Greene v. Walla Walla, 126 S. Ct. 1339, 164 L. Ed. 2d 54, 2006 U.S. LEXIS 1198 (U.S., Feb. 21, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In 2000, defendant was charged with driving under the influence (DUI), to which she pled guilty to an amended charge of first degree negligent driving. In 2004, she was charged and convicted of DUI. The State sought enhanced punishment pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.5055(12)(a)(v) due to her prior offense. The Walla Walla District Court, Washington, held the statute violated requirements of due process. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The district court relied on State v. Shaffer, 55 P.3d 668 (2002) to hold that it was unconstitutional to conclude that a prior DUI offense occurred where no proof existed. Accordingly, the district court had sentenced defendant as a DUI first time offender. However, the supreme court held that in Shaffer, the court of appeals erred in concluding that the due process protections articulated in In re Winship rendered Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.5055(12)(a)(v) unconstitutional. Winship held that every element of a crime had to be proven beyond a reasonable doubt. However, the fact that a defendant was previously convicted of negligent driving in the first degree (Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.5249), reckless driving (Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.500), or reckless endangerment (Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.36.050), was sufficient to satisfy a defendant's due process protections where all elements of the offense were established by virtue of the conviction itself, and where the prosecution could establish that intoxicating liquor or drugs were involved in that prior offense. Accordingly, Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.5055(12)(a)(v) survived constitutional challenge and Shaffer was overruled.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was reversed and the matter was remanded.
