Farm Bureau Fed'n v. Reed


No. 77010-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


154 Wn.2d 668; 115 P.3d 301; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 558


June 30, 2005, Oral Argument  
July 14, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Date first filed at Supreme Court: 05/03/2005.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner business associations sought a writ of mandamus in the Washington Supreme Court directing respondent secretary of state to accept referendum measure 60 for processing.


OVERVIEW: The associations argued that because they only had until July 23, 2005, to gather approximately 100,000 signatures on their petition for referendum, a writ of mandamus was the only proper remedy. The associations argued that the rights provided by Wash. Const. art. II, § 1 should be liberally construed. The supreme court noted that the constitutional grant of power to the people was not without limitations. The legislature could suspend the right of the people to order a referendum on a bill where the bill was necessary for the immediate preservation of the public peace, health or in support of state government and its existing public institutions. Given the substantial deference to the legislature in a legislative declaration of emergency, the emergency clause in Section 7 of the Washington Substitute Senate Bill 6078 covering sections 1 and 2 of that bill was valid and was not obviously false or a palpable attempt at dissimulation.


OUTCOME: The petition for writ of mandamus was denied.

 

318 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Evans


No. 74851-9, No. 75766-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


154 Wn.2d 438; 114 P.3d 627; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 541



June 16, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Evans v. Wash., 126 S. Ct. 560, 163 L. Ed. 2d 472, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 7963 (U.S., Oct. 31, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Evans, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 520 (Wash., July 8, 2004)
State v. Swenson, 104 Wn. App. 744, 9 P.3d 933, 2000 Wash. App. LEXIS 1899 (2000)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In separate cases, defendant one's first degree rape conviction and defendant two's first degree felony murder conviction were affirmed on appeal. After the Blakely and Apprendi decisions, defendants sought resentencing. The Washington Court of Appeals moved to transfer for a decision as to whether Blakely and/or Apprendi applied retroactively.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendants argued for retroactive application of Blakely based on state law; defendant two also challenged the use of erroneous accomplice liability instruction. The supreme court found that defendants did not make a compelling case that there were reasons for retroactive application that were sufficient under State law, and concluded that neither Apprendi nor Blakely applied retroactively to cases already final on direct review. The supreme court also found in defendant two's case that the prosecution largely based its case on the theory that the jury could convict defendant two of first degree felony murder if it found that he was either the principle or an accomplice in the underlying crime of robbery, contending that the jury should convict defendant of robbery based on nothing more than his admission that he intended to commit theft against the victim. Thus, defendant two's ability to present his defense was fatally compromised; he showed actual and substantial prejudice in that the State repeatedly told the jury that it could convict defendant two of murder without first finding that he intended to do anything but steal certain items.


OUTCOME: Neither defendant was entitled to resentencing; however, defendant two's felony murder conviction was reversed and his case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

319 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Pulfrey


No. 75301-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


154 Wn.2d 517; 111 P.3d 1162; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 471


January 20, 2005, Oral Argument  
May 19, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Pulfrey, 120 Wn. App. 270, 86 P.3d 790, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 311 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of possession of methamphetamine. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the arresting officer failed to exercise discretion by always arresting suspects for driving with a suspended license. The supreme court disagreed. A police officer may arrest a person for driving while license suspended and take a reasonable amount of time to investigate; he was not bound to cite and release in short order. Read together, Wash. Rev. Code §§ 10.31.100 and 46.64.015, and Wash. Crim. R. Ct. Ltd. Juris.2.1 allowed officers to arrest a person for driving while license suspended and then exercising discretion to retain the person in custody or cite and release. The officer arrested people for driving while license suspended, as he was authorized to do, and then later discussed with them the possibility of release with a citation and promise to appear in court. In defendant's case, the process was truncated by the discovery of methamphetamine, possession of which was a felony. That discovery eliminated the possibility of release.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

320 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



In re Pers. Restraint of Lavery


No. 75340-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


154 Wn.2d 249; 111 P.3d 837; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 370



May 5, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Lavery, 100 Wn. App. 1068, 2000 Wash. App. LEXIS 3038 (2000).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner was convicted of second degree robbery in 1998 and sentenced to life in prison under the Washington Persistent Offender Accountability Act (POAA), former Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.120 (1998). Later, the Washington Court of Appeals, held his federal robbery conviction was a strike under the POAA. The instant court accepted his second personal restraint petition (PRP), wherein he argued there had been a significant change in the law.


OVERVIEW: On review, the Washington Supreme Court noted that to determine if a foreign crime was comparable to a Washington offense, for purposes of the POAA, the elements of the out of state crime had to be compared to the elements of a Washington criminal statute in effect when the foreign crime was committed. The supreme court concluded that the elements of federal bank robbery (petitioner's foreign conviction), and second degree robbery under Washington's criminal statutes, were not factually and legally comparable at the time petitioner was sentenced in Washington, and that the foreign conviction was not a strike under the POAA. That was because the federal bank robbery was a general intent crime, while the offense of robbery in Washington was a specific intent crime. Secondly, until State v. Freeburg (in 2004), synthesized both State v. Bunting and Carter v. United States, and declared that the crimes were not necessarily comparable, the rule in Washington was that federal bank robbery and second degree robbery were comparable as a matter of law. Because Freeburg represented a significant change in the law that was material to petitioner's sentence, his PRP was not time barred.


OUTCOME: The supreme court vacated petitioner's sentence and ordered that he be resentenced for the crime of second degree robbery.

 

321 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Hughes


No. 74147-6, No. 75053-0, No. 75063-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


154 Wn.2d 118; 110 P.3d 192; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 362


November 9, 2004, Oral Argument  
April 14, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Anderson, 152 Wn.2d 1011, 99 P.3d 895, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 616 (2004).
State v. Selvidge, 152 Wn.2d 1011, 99 P.3d 896, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 576 (2004).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, defendants sought review of judgments of the Superior Court of Grays Harbor County (Washington) that imposed exceptional sentences pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.535 of the Sentencing Reform Act of 1981 (SRA). One defendant challenged his conviction and the fine imposed for first-degree theft for cutting down old growth cedar trees.


OVERVIEW: Defendants sought review claiming that the exceptional sentence provisions of the SRA, Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.535, violated their Sixth Amendment rights to a jury trial in light of the decision of the United States Supreme Court in Blakely v. Washington. The court held that the exceptional sentence provisions of the SRA were still facially constitutional following the Blakely decision but that the specific exceptional sentences imposed upon defendants violated their Sixth Amendment rights. The court found that in each of the cases, the trial judge imposed exceptional sentences based on facts found by the trial court and not supported by jury findings. The court also held that Blakely Sixth Amendment violations were not harmless error because judges, rather than juries, found the facts used to increase sentences; therefore, there was no jury finding beyond a reasonable doubt of aggravating factors warranting an enhanced sentence. Also the court held there was no procedure established for empanelling juries on remand for re-sentencing; therefore, the court remanded the cases for imposition of standard range sentences.


OUTCOME: The court held that the exceptional sentence provisions of the SRA were facially constitutional but that the exceptional sentences imposed upon the three defendants were unconstitutional. The court held that the harmless error analysis was inapplicable to the violations and remanded the cases for sentencing within the standard range. The court affirmed the theft conviction and fine imposed against defendant who challenged his conviction.

 

322 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Mills


No. 73894-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


154 Wn.2d 1; 109 P.3d 415; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 355



April 7, 2005, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at State v. Davis, 154 Wn.2d 291, 111 P.3d 844, 2005 Wash. LEXIS 462 (Wash., May 12, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Mills, 116 Wn. App. 106, 64 P.3d 1253, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 372 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate was convicted by special verdict of telephone harassment - felony domestic violence, as charged in count I. The inmate appealed her conviction to Division One of the Court of Appeals (Washington). The appellate court rejected the arguments that the bifurcated structure used by the trial court was constitutionally defective. The supreme court granted the inmate's petition for review.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court held that where the legislature had established a statutory framework which defined a base crime which was elevated to a greater crime if a certain fact was present, a trial court could, consistent with the guaranties of due process, U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1 and Wash. Const. art. I, § 3, and a trial by jury, U.S. Const. art. III, § 2, U.S. Const. amend. VI, and Wash. Const. art. I, §§ 21 and 22, bifurcate the elevating fact into a special verdict form. So long as the jury was instructed it had to unanimously agree beyond a reasonable doubt before it could affirmatively answer the special verdict, the constitution was not offended. The supreme court found that the jury was not instructed that it had to find that the victim was placed in reasonable fear that the threat to kill her would be carried out. Also, the State's attempted gap-filling in for the jury instructions did not meet the requirement that all elements of the offense be clearly set forth. Thus, under the instructions in the inmate's case, the supreme court held that the jury was not instructed on all the elements required to convict her of felony harassment under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.46.020.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the inmate's conviction and remanded the case to the trial court for a new trial.

In re Parentage of C.A.M.A.


No. 75262-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


154 Wn.2d 52; 109 P.3d 405; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 356


November 16, 2004, Oral Argument  
April 7, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
Appel v. Norton (In re C.A.M.A.), 120 Wn. App. 199, 84 P.3d 1253, 2004 Wash. App. LEXIS 230 (2004).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Snohomish County Superior Court, Washington, granted petitioner father's motion to dismiss respondent grandmother's motion to intervene after the father filed a parentage action, holding that the child's parents were fit, and that no constitutional third party visitation statute existed. The court of appeals reversed, finding Wash. Rev. Code § 26.09.240 unconstitutional. The father petitioned for discretionary review.


OVERVIEW: The grandmother argued that a prior case was no longer good law; however, the appellate court found that it remained binding precedent. The father's challenge stemmed from the language of Wash. Rev. Code § 26.09.240 itself. The grandmother argued that the requirement that a visitation petitioner establish a "substantial relationship" with the child preserved the statute's constitutionality. Establishing a substantial relationship was a necessary, though not sufficient, component of a visitation statute that comported with due process. The statutes considered in out-of-state cases did not contain the constitutionally flawed presumption in favor of grandparent visitation present in Washington's statute, and those courts had not held that a showing of harm to the child was required to overcome the presumption that a fit parent acted in the best interest of the child. The unconstitutional portions of the statute, combined with the constitutionally required presumptions not contained in the text, rendered the entire statute unconstitutional. The trial court had to weigh the financial resource of the parties and award fees to the father under Wash. Rev. Code § 26.09.140 if appropriate.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals was reversed, and the judgment of the trial court was reinstated; the case was remanded to the trial court to weigh the financial resources of the parties and determine if it would award fees to the parent.

 

324 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Robinson


No. 74611-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


153 Wn.2d 689; 107 P.3d 90; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 194


September 14, 2004, Oral Argument  
February 24, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court. Honorable Steven J Mura.
State v. Robinson, 117 Wn. App. 1073, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 2177 (2003).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Superior Court (Washington), which after he entered two separate Alford pleas to kidnapping and delivery of cocaine, convicted defendant. Defendant alleged in part breach of the plea agreement.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that he was entitled to counsel at state expense under Wash. Super. Ct. Crim. R. 3.1(b)(2) when he moved to withdraw his guilty pleas after sentencing. The court held that it was apparent that Rule 3.1(b)(2) referred to the right to counsel that was either already constitutionally guaranteed or provided elsewhere in the rules. Further, the court interpreted Wash. Super. Ct. Crim. R. 7.8 to provide counsel after an initial determination had been made that the motion was not frivolous. By not holding a hearing the trial court effectively determined that defendant did not establish grounds for relief and thus would not have been entitled to counsel. The court held that any denial of the right to counsel was harmless because the court of appeals reversed the denial of the motion to withdraw the guilty plea under which defendant may have been entitled to counsel. The court of appeals determined the trial court was incorrect in denying one of his motions to withdraw guilty plea. Because the court of appeals determined a ground for relief, the superior court was incorrect in denying counsel, but this was harmless in light of the subsequent reversal.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals.

State v. Roggenkamp


No. 73839-4, No. 74231-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


153 Wn.2d 614; 106 P.3d 196; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 180


January 15, 2004, Oral Argument

State v. Clark, 117 Wn. App. 281, 71 P.3d 224, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 1198 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant one was convicted in the King County Juvenile Court (Washington) with one count of vehicular homicide and two counts of vehicular assault. The Court of Appeals, Division One, Washington, affirmed. Defendant two was convicted of three counts of vehicular assault by the Clark County Superior Court (Washington). The Court of Appeals affirmed the conviction. Both defendants petitioned for review. Their appeals were consolidated.


OVERVIEW: The primary question presented to the Supreme Court of Washington in this consolidated review was whether the term "in a reckless manner," which appeared in the vehicular homicide and vehicular assault statutes, Wash. Rev. Code §§ 46.61.520(1)(b), 46.61.522(1)(a), was defined by the "willful or wanton disregard for the safety of persons or property" language of the reckless driving statute, Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.500(1). The court answered "no" to that question, holding to the well-established definition of the term as "driving in a rash or heedless manner, indifferent to the consequences."


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in both of the cases.

 

326 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Luby v. DaSilva (In re Brown)


No. 74699-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


153 Wn.2d 646; 105 P.3d 991; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 185


October 19, 2004.  
February 10, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court, Honorable Mary Yu.
Luby v. Da Silva (In re S.H.B.), 118 Wn. App. 71, 74 P.3d 674, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 1823 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a case involving a dispute between two nonparents over custody of a child, the trial court concluded that it was in the child's best interest to award custody to respondent maternal grandmother because of petitioner paternal grandmother's marijuana growing operation and the use of alcohol in her home. The Washington Court of Appeals, Division One, affirmed the trial court's judgment. The paternal grandmother sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The paternal grandmother did not challenge the findings of fact entered by the trial court, but argued that the trial court erred in applying the best interests of the child standard in making its custody determination. The paternal grandmother contended that since the child was living with her at the time the petitions for custody were filed, she should have been afforded the rights of a parent under the common law doctrine of in loco parentis. She also argued that the trial court's use of a parenting evaluator and guardians ad litem violated her right to due process. The instant court held that the trial court properly applied the best interests of the child standard under Wash. Rev. Code § 26.10.100 when making its nonparental custody determination. The paternal grandmother did not establish any basis on which she should have been afforded the fundamental right to make child rearing decisions on behalf of the child. The paternal grandmother's right to due process was not violated by the trial court's use of the parenting evaluation or the guardian ad litem recommendation. The paternal grandmother's request for attorney fees pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 26.10.080 was denied.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment was affirmed.

 

327 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Kitsap County v. Mattress Outlet


No. 73913-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


153 Wn.2d 506; 104 P.3d 1280; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 80; 33 Media L. Rep. 1339


February 19, 2004, Oral Argument  
January 27, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court of Kitsap County. Docket No: 02-2-03369-7. Date filed: 03/27/2003. Judge signing: Hon. Jay B Roof.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent county cited petitioner business for using raincoat-clad workers as an offsite sign without a permit in violation local ordinances. The violations were dismissed upon findings that the sign ordinances were unconstitutionally vague and overbroad and an unconstitutional restriction of free speech as applied to the raincoats used by the business. The Superior Court of Kitsap County, Washington, reversed. The business appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the business contended, inter alia, that under the plain language of the sign code, its raincoats were not signs and, therefore, were not subject to the sign ordinance. The county contended that the raincoats were signs, as described by the nonexclusive list of portable signs regulated by the sign ordinance. The supreme court found that the raincoats were designed to function as retail signs; mounting the advertising message on the raincoats rather than cardboard or other material did not obscure the raincoats' character as signs. Therefore, the business's raincoats were signs subject to the county sign ordinance. However, the supreme court also held that Kitsap County, Wash., County Code § 17.445.070(C) was an unconstitutional restriction of commercial speech as applied to the business under the Central Hudson test, as prohibiting persons from wearing signage provided minimal, if any, benefit in aesthetics and safety, and the total ban of offsite advertising did not reflect any method of narrowly tailoring the restriction to meet the specific goals of increased safety and aesthetics.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the trial court's dismissal of the citations issued to the business was affirmed.

State Grange v. Locke


No. 75384-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


153 Wn.2d 475; 105 P.3d 9; 2005 Wash. LEXIS 78


June 10, 2004, Oral Argument  
January 20, 2005, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Date first filed at Supeme Court: 04/22/2004.
Democratic Party of Wash. State v. Reed, 388 F.3d 1281, 2004 U.S. App. LEXIS 23951 (9th Cir., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, voters, legislators, and the State Grange, filed an action challenging the validity of parts of E.S.B. 6453, 58th Leg., Reg. Sess. (Wash. 2004), which allowed for a "Montana style" primary election system and the governor's veto. Petitioners sought a writ of mandamus directing the secretary of state and the governor to conduct the 2004 primary election in accordance with E.S.B. 6453 as passed by the legislature.


OVERVIEW: After Washington's blanket primary election system was declared unconstitutional, the legislature enacted E.S.B. 6453, which created a "Louisiana style" or "top two" primary system, but also allowed for a "Montana style" system should the Louisiana system be declared invalid. After the governor's veto, the resulting legislation, 2004 Wash. Laws 271, created only a Montana style primary system. Petitioners claimed that the enactment process violated the governor's veto powers provided in Wash. Const. art. III, § 12; the subject in title rules in Wash. Const. art. II, § 19; and the limitation on amendments in Wash. Const. art. II, § 38. The court held that the governor did not act outside his veto power because he had vetoed entire sections of the bill, which was clearly allowed under Wash. Const. art. III, § 12. The court held that there was no violation of the subject in title provision of Wash. Const. art. II, § 19 because the title was general and encompassed both primary election system styles. The court held that petitioners failed to show that the amendments to E.S.B. 6453 adopted during the legislative process violated Wash. Const. art. II, § 38.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioners' application for a writ of mandamus.

 

329 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Harner


No. 74460-2 (consolidated with No. 75337-7)


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


153 Wn.2d 228; 103 P.3d 738; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 933


September 14, 2004, Argued  
December 23, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court February 17, 2005.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by State v. Harner, 2005 Wash. LEXIS 195 (Wash., Feb. 17, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Harner, 151 Wn.2d 1010, 88 P.3d 964, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 253 (2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, both defendants were convicted of felony level offenses of possession of a controlled substance. Division Two of the Washington Court of Appeals held that one county's failure to offer a drug court did not violate that defendant's right to equal protection and due process. The second case was transferred to the instant court from Division Three of the Washington Court of Appeals. The instant court reviewed both cases.


OVERVIEW: The Washington State Legislature had enabled counties to promulgate drug courts under Wash. Rev. Code § 2.28.170. The court noted that defendants had not shown a fundamental right existed to participate in a drug court program, nor was there any support to find drug offenders were a suspect or semi-suspect class. Applying the rational basis test, the court held the absence of a drug court in a county where a defendant was charged did not violate a defendant's right to equal protection. As to due process, the court could not conclude that a county was arbitrary and capricious where the county failed to establish a program it was not required to establish by statute, and where the program was funded in part by local resources. The legislature had been explicit that while counties could establish drug courts, the legislature was not creating an entitlement for a criminal defendant to any specific sanction, alternative, sentence option, or substance abuse treatment. Thus, defendants' rights to equal protection and due process were not violated by the absence of a drug court in the counties where they were charged.


OUTCOME: Defendants' convictions were affirmed.

Republican Party v. Election Servs.


No. 76399-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


153 Wn.2d 220; 103 P.3d 725; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 926


December 22, 2004.  
December 22, 2004, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor Washington State Democratic Central Committee and petitioners, a county canvassing board and the Secretary of State, sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Pierce County (Washington), which granted injunctive relief to respondents, the Washington State Republican Party and two citizens, in their action to stop the board from recanvassing votes in the election for governor, pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 29A.60.210.


OVERVIEW: During a hand recount in the governor's election, the board discovered that 573 ballots were coded as having no signature on file, but the workers only checked one place for signatures. Thus, the board decided to recanvass the ballots under § 29A.60.210. Respondents petitioned the court for an injunction. The trial court granted the injunction, and the Democratic Committee, the board, and the Secretary appealed. The court held that the board could recanvass the ballots if there was an apparent discrepancy or an inconsistency in the returns, as provided by § 29A.60.210. The phrase "an apparent discrepancy or an inconsistency in the returns" did not mean only an arithmetic error disclosed on the face of the returns, as argued by respondents. Certain ballots were coded as having "no signature on file" without having been fully examined to properly place them in that category. In that sense they were never fully canvassed. That was just the sort of apparent discrepancy or inconsistency that the board could correct through recanvassing. Accordingly, the trial court erred in granting the injunction.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and vacated the judgment of the trial court. The court remanded the matter to the trial court for entry of an order of dismissal.

 

331 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



In re Pers. Restraint of Turay


No. 74556-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


153 Wn.2d 44; 101 P.3d 854; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 843


May 25, 2004, Oral Argument  
November 18, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Turay v. Washington, 544 U.S. 952, 125 S. Ct. 1704, 161 L. Ed. 2d 531, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 2849 (U.S., Mar. 28, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: In re Pers. Restraint of Turay, 150 Wn.2d 71, 74 P.3d 1194, 2003 Wash. LEXIS 561 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In this matter, a personal restraint petitioner sought release from civil commitment as a sexually violent predator, arguing that the King County (Washington) prosecutor was required, but failed, to plead and prove a recent overt act at his commitment trial.


OVERVIEW: While the petitioner was still incarcerated, the King County Prosecutor petitioned for his commitment as a sexually violent predator, but did not allege he had committed a "recent overt act," that either caused harm of a sexually violent nature or created a reasonable apprehension of such harm. The only direct bar to raising new issues in this court was the abuse of the writ doctrine. This was the petitioner's fourth personal restraint petition. The only reason the petitioner offered for failing to raise his "new issue" earlier was his argument that Albrecht and Henrickson qualified as pertinent intervening legal developments, but that argument had already been rejected. There was no basis for any exception comparable to the actual innocence exception under federal law. The petitioner was not confined pursuant to a criminal conviction, and there was no issue of innocence to consider. It was not enough for the petitioner to show that the State failed to plead and prove a recent overt act. Instead, to avoid dismissal of this petition on abuse of the writ grounds, he had to, at the least, show that, when the State confined him, he was not presently dangerous. He had not done so.


OUTCOME: The personal restraint petition was dismissed as an abuse of the writ.

In re Pers. Restraint of Orange


No. 72485-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


152 Wn.2d 795; 100 P.3d 291; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 827; 32 Media L. Rep. 2569



November 10, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court January 20, 2005.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Reconsideration denied by In re Pers. Restraint of Orange, 2005 Wash. LEXIS 88 (Wash., Jan. 20, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court, County.
In re Pers. Restraint of Orange, 110 Wn. App. 1086, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 1667 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner prisoner appealed from a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Washington), which denied his personal restraint petition.


OVERVIEW: The prisoner drove into a gasoline station and fired at least 11 shots, killing one victim, wounding another, and piercing the clothing of a third victim. The court held that the trial court's closure of the courtroom during voir dire violated the prisoner's constitutional right to a public trial under Wash. Const. art. I, § 22 because: (1) the trial court did not identify a compelling interest that the closure was essential to protect; (2) even if the trial court's desire to keep the large jury pool intact or to protect the safety of the trial participants were compelling interests, the trial court's ruling was not narrowly tailored in scope or time to preserve those aims; and (3) the trial court gave little more than passing consideration to alternatives to full closure. The remedy was remand for a new trial. Defendant's convictions for first degree attempted murder and first degree assault violated his constitutional protection against double jeopardy. The two crimes were based on the same shot directed at the same victim, and the evidence required to support the conviction for first degree attempted murder was sufficient to convict the prisoner of first degree assault.


OUTCOME: The court granted the personal restraint petition and remanded for a new trial.

 

333 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



In re Pers. Restraint of Davis


No. 70834-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


152 Wn.2d 647; 101 P.3d 1; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 752



November 4, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Post-conviction relief denied at State v. Davis, 146 Wn. App. 1037, 2008 Wash. App. LEXIS 2160 (Aug. 19, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Davis, 141 Wn.2d 798, 10 P.3d 977, 2000 Wash. LEXIS 758 (Sept. 28, 2000)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate filed a personal restraint petition. The inmate had been convicted of premeditated murder in the first degree and sentenced to death based on a jury's finding of aggravating circumstances of rape, robbery, and burglary. The jury had recommended that leniency not be granted. The court had affirmed the inmate's conviction and death sentence on direct appeal.


OVERVIEW: The inmate claimed ineffective assistance of trial counsel and that the State's death penalty scheme was cruel and unusual punishment. The court found that the inmate could not establish prejudice from counsel's decision not to object to his being shackled during the guilt phase. Because of the overwhelming evidence of the inmate's guilt, he could not show that there was a reasonable probability that, but for his counsel's deficient performance in failing to object, the outcome of the guilt phase would have been different. Testimony established the inmate's opportunity and motive to commit the crime, and forensic evidence connected him to the crime. However, the court held that the prejudice to the inmate during a special sentencing proceeding could not necessarily be overcome by objective and overwhelming evidence. Although jurors' opportunity to observe the inmate in shackles was partial and fleeting, the balance had to tip in the inmate's favor in the penalty phase given the difference in the nature of the inquiry. Thus, a new trial in the penalty phase was required. The inmate failed to show how the sentencing scheme relating to the death penalty violated the Eighth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The court granted the inmate's petition and remanded for a new penalty phase.

 

334 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Eckblad


No. 74109-3


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


152 Wn.2d 515; 98 P.3d 1184; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 742



October 14, 2004, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: With certain exceptions (such as for postal carriers), Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.688 required all drivers to wear seatbelts. Respondent citizen was stopped for failing to wear a seat belt, the officer smelled marijuana, and the result was that the citizen was arrested for criminal offenses. However, the Washington trial court suppressed the evidence, finding that § 46.61.688, the basis for the stop, was unconstitutionally vague. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The citizen argued that Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.688 was vague because it incorporated federal seat belt safety standards that were difficult to find. However, the Washington Supreme Court noted that an ordinary, reasonable reader would read the entire statute and would recognize that the legislature intended that Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.688(3) and (4) were the heart of the statute. Those subsections plainly required every person driving or riding in a motor vehicle be seat-belted or in a child safety seat. Moreover, while seat belt laws had not been challenged across the nation as unconstitutionally vague, the closely related regime of motorcycle helmet laws had been challenged in many sister states. Those statutes, with similar type restrictions, had not been found to be unconstitutionally vague in most of the subject states. The supreme court held that Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.688 was not vague facially because an ordinary citizen reading the statute was put on notice that there was a general obligation to wear a seat belt. Finally, a future due process challenge to the statute was possible, but that issue was not before the supreme court in the instant case.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower court was reversed, and the matter was remanded for further proceedings consistent with the supreme court's opinion.

 

335 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Bradshaw


No. 74410-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


152 Wn.2d 528; 98 P.3d 1190; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 743



October 14, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Bradshaw v. Washington, 544 U.S. 922, 125 S. Ct. 1662, 161 L. Ed. 2d 480, 2005 U.S. LEXIS 2486 (U.S., Mar. 21, 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Bradshaw, 117 Wn. App. 1019, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 3165 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged a decision from the Washington Court of Appeals, which affirmed their convictions for the unlawful possession of a controlled substance.


OVERVIEW: In unrelated cases, defendants were caught trying to bring drugs over the Canadian border. They were convicted of the unlawful possession of a controlled substance. After their convictions were upheld by the court of appeals, defendant sought further review. Specifically, they asked the court to overrule case law finding that Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.401(d) did not have a mens rea element. In affirming the court of appeals, the court declined to make such a ruling. The case law in question properly looked to the language of the mere possession statute, and, because the statute did not have an explicit mens rea element, the ruling court looked to the legislative history. The legislative history for the mere possession statute supported the conclusion that no mens rea element should have been implied. In the 22 years since the case in question was decided, the legislature did not added a mens rea element. Section 69.50.401(d) did not improperly shift the burden of proof. Further, Wash. Rev. Code 69.50.603 did not require an implied mens rea based on uniformity. Finally, the court declined to address the vagueness arguments proffered by defendants.


OUTCOME: The convictions were affirmed.

 

336 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Simmons


No. 74383-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


152 Wn.2d 450; 98 P.3d 789; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 660



October 7, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court. Honorable Frederick B Hayes, Judge.
State v. Simmons, 117 Wn. App. 682, 73 P.3d 380, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 1511 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate was convicted of second-degree robbery. He was committed to the custody of the Washington Department of Corrections (DOC), where he committed over 46 violations of disciplinary rules. The trial court found the inmate guilty of persistent prison misbehavior under Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94.070, which delayed his release eligibility. The Washington Court of Appeals, Division Two, affirmed. The inmate appealed.


OVERVIEW: Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94.070 made it a felony for a prisoner to commit a serious prison infraction after losing all potential earned early release credits. That was the situation in the instant case. The issue on appeal was whether § 9.94.070 unconstitutionally delegated legislative authority to the DOC or violated equal protection principles. The Washington Supreme Court noted that, where a criminal prosecution arose under the statute, an inmate was entitled to all of the procedural safeguards that were afforded a defendant in a criminal prosecution. Thus, the supreme court held that § 9.94.070 provided adequate procedural safeguards against arbitrary administrative action and abuse of discretion and did not unconstitutionally delegate legislative authority to the DOC. Secondly, § 9.94.070 did not create an arbitrary classification between inmates. In fact, no case law existed that distinguished between classes of inmates based on the length of sentence being served for purposes of an equal protection analysis. Consequently, the supreme court held that § 9.94.070 satisfied the State's objective of maintaining prison security and did not violate principles of equal protection.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower court was affirmed.

 

337 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



City of Seattle v. Mighty Movers, Inc.


No. 73005-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


152 Wn.2d 343; 96 P.3d 979; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 555


June 12, 2003, Oral Argument  
September 9, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court September 14, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] 
City of Seattle v. Mighty Movers, Inc., 112 Wn. App. 904, 51 P.3d 152, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 1842 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Washington) that reversed the trial court decision and held that city utility poles were a traditional public forum and that the city's ordinance codified at Seattle, Wash., Mun. Code §§ 15.48.100 through 15.48.130, prohibiting sign posting on utility poles was unconstitutional. The city had filed the action to recover the cost of removing signs posted by appellee moving company.


OVERVIEW: The court of appeals reversed the grant of summary judgment for the city and held that the city ordinance restricting posting on utility poles was violated Wash. Const. art. I, § 5, in that it was not narrowly tailored to serve a compelling governmental interest and adequate alternative means of communication were not available. The court of appeals also held that the ordinance was overbroad because it swept within its prohibitions constitutionally protected speech. On appeal, the court concluded that the ordinance did not prevent the public from exercising free speech rights on public streets and other public places and that there were alternate channels of communication sufficient under the standard applicable to non-public forums. The court agreed with the trial court and held that the ordinance was enacted to achieve legitimate governmental purposes, it was reasonable in light of the purposes served by utility poles, and it was content and viewpoint neutral. The court held that utility poles were not a public forum because they were an essential part of the city's power system and they had not been a traditional public forum or historically held open to the general public.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals judgment and reinstated the trial court's summary judgment in favor of the city. The court concluded that the city was entitled to attorney fees and directed that an award of reasonable fees be made both for the appeal and for the court's discretionary review.

339 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Saddle Mountain Minerals v. Joshi


No. 73899-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


152 Wn.2d 242; 95 P.3d 1236; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 547; 162 Oil & Gas Rep. 980


February 26, 2004, Oral Argument  
August 12, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court. Honorable Craig J Matheson.
Saddle Mt. Minerals, L.L.C. v. Joshi, 116 Wn. App. 198, 65 P.3d 366, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 445 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Washington Court of Appeals reversed a trial court's summary judgment in favor of appellant landowners' case involving trespass to land and conversion. Appellant mineral estate owners alleged that the landowners exported sand and gravel from the property without permission from or payment to the estate owners. The trial court ruled that the estate owners were barred from recovering damages. The landowners appealed.


OVERVIEW: The landowners argued that the estate owners were barred from recovering damages because the estate owners' mineral rights were rendered worthless by the local zoning code prohibiting mining and the landowners' right to subjacent support. The appellate court noted that the warranty deed provided that the estate owners owned any kind of minerals in and under the site including sand and gravel. The estate owners never applied for a variance or waiver from the mining prohibition under the ordinance. There was no final governmental decision regarding the application of the regulations to the minerals on and in the land. The zoning ordinance did not bar the estate owners from recovering damages from the landowners. The estate owners could extract sand and gravel in a manner that did not raise the issue of subjacent support. The landowners were entitled to utilize the surface soil in developing the site. However, the landowners had to compensate the estate owners if they exported sand and grave from the site.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed.

In re Marriage of Suggs


No. 73892-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


152 Wn.2d 74; 93 P.3d 161; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 459


February 12, 2004, Oral Argument  
July 8, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court November 2, 2004.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] 
Reconsideration denied by In re Marriage of Suggs & Hamilton, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 823 (Wash., Nov. 2, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court, County. Honorable David Russell Draper.
Suggs v. Hamilton, 2003 Wash. App. LEXIS 440 (Wash. Ct. App., Mar. 18, 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Five years after their acrimonious dissolution, respondent ex-husband filed a petition for an order for protection based on unlawful harassment against petitioner ex-wife. The trial court issued the order. The trial court denied the ex-wife's motion for reconsideration. The Court of Appeals (Washington) affirmed. The ex-wife petitioned for review.


OVERVIEW: The trial court issued an order permanently restraining the ex-wife from "knowingly and willfully making invalid and unsubstantiated allegations or complaints to third parties which were designed for the purpose of annoying, harassing, vexing, or otherwise harming her ex-husband for no lawful purpose." The Supreme Court of Washington found that the antiharassment order was an unconstitutional prior restraint on speech. The order's "invalid and unsubstantiated" language was problematic because what could appear valid and substantiated to the ex-wife could ultimately be found invalid and unsubstantiated by a court. The ex-wife would have been hesitant to assert any allegations, including those she deemed truthful. As a result, even her protected speech would have been chilled.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed. The order was vacated.

 

341 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



City of Spokane v. Neff


No. 74872-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


152 Wn.2d 85; 93 P.3d 158; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 460


May 13, 2004, Oral Argument  
July 8, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court, County. Honorable Ellen Kalama Clark.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The City charged defendant with loitering for the purposes of prostitution, in violation of Spokane, Wash., Mun. Code § 10.06.030. The municipal court ruled that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague because it did not define the term "known prostitute." The Superior Court of Spokane County (Washington) affirmed, and also held that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in excluding certain evidence. The City appealed.


OVERVIEW: The ordinance sought to define situations in which a person displayed intent to engage in prostitution-related activity. One situation highlighted by the ordinance, and the language at issue, was when the suspect was a "known prostitute." Defendant argued that the undefined term "known prostitute" did not provide an ascertainable standard for determining whether a person was loitering with intent to commit a prostitution-related act. Undefined, the term allowed an arresting officer to identify a "known prostitute" as someone with prior convictions or arrests, or merely prior acts of loitering in an area where prostitution occurred. It did not limit police discretion in any way when it came to determination of who was a "known prostitute," and it provided no standards for locating the line between who was a "known prostitute" and who was not. The unqualified term "known prostitute" could have included anyone from a person with a recent conviction for prostitution to a person who was simply loitering on a street where prostitution occurred. To the extent the ordinance contained the undefined term "known prostitute" the Supreme Court of Washington held it was unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Dreiling v. Jain


No. 73756-8.


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


151 Wn.2d 900; 93 P.3d 861; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 447


September 2, 2003, Oral Argument  
June 24, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
Dreiling v. Jain, 281 F. Supp. 2d 1234, 2003 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 20652 (W.D. Wash., 2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner/Intervenor newspaper sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of King County (Washington) that denied its motion to unseal records and directed respondent corporation's special litigation committee (SLC) to file a log as to the documents attached to the SLC's report and motion to terminate a shareholder derivative suit.


OVERVIEW: The court granted interlocutory review of the limited question of whether the trial court applied the correct legal standard when it sealed material and briefing filed in support of the SLC's motion to terminate a shareholder derivative action. The corporation claimed that a motion to terminate was like an internal corporate matter or a voluntary settlement agreement and asked that the standards underlying Wash. Super. Ct. Civ. R. 26(c), which allowed a court to limit discovery and its use, be applied. The newspaper argued that a motion to terminate was the functional equivalent to a summary judgment motion and that the trial court erred in not applying more stringent factors before sealing the documentation. The court held that motions to terminate derivative actions were analogous to motions for dispositive judgment, therefore any documents submitted in support of a motion to terminate were presumptively accessible to the public and could be sealed only in accordance with the analytical approach set out in Seattle Times Co. v. Ishikawa. The court noted that the trial court should determine if there was a waiver of attorney-client and work product privilege for any documents.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings and noted that the trial court should determine whether and to what extent the attorney-client and work product privileges had been waived by the SLC's filing with the court.

State v. Thompson


NO. 72997-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


151 Wn.2d 793; 92 P.3d 228; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 445


June 10, 2003, Oral Argument  
June 24, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court.
State v. Thompson, 112 Wn. App. 787, 51 P.3d 143, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 1831 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Superior Court Pierce County (Washington) convicted defendant for unlawful manufacture of a controlled substance. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Police went to defendant's trailer to arrest him on an outstanding civil warrant. They observed another individual in the trailer and ordered him to come out. Before leaving, he told police he needed his jacket from the trailer. Police entered to retrieve his jacket. Based on his experience and training, police detected the odor of methamphetamine. Defendant's parents gave consent to search an attached garage and boathouse. Defendant was charged with one count of unlawful manufacture of methamphetamine, Wash. Rev. Code § 69.50.401(a)(1)(ii) (1998). Defendant correctly argued police should not have forcibly entered a private dwelling to serve a civil arrest warrant. Police erred in forcibly opening the trailer door when executing it. The "knock and wait" statute did not encompass the execution of civil arrest warrants. The officer improperly used the community caretaking function in retrieving the jacket. Evidence obtained from entry into the trailer should have been suppressed. Evidence from the boathouse which should have been considered by the trial court was not admitted. There was no other untainted evidence which could be relied upon to affirm defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction was vacated, and the case was remanded for a new trial consistent with the opinion.

Police Guild v. City of Seattle


NO. 73202-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


151 Wn.2d 823; 92 P.3d 243; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 446


September 11, 2003, Argued  
June 24, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court. Honorable William L. Downing.
Seattle Police Officers Guild v. City of Seattle, 113 Wn. App. 431, 53 P.3d 1036, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 2232 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, police guild and officers, challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals (Washington), which held appellee City's practice of certifying five police officers with the highest civil service exam scores for promotional opportunities substantially accomplished the purpose of Wash. Rev. Code ch. 41.12 to provide for promotion on the basis of merit. Appellants claimed the City's "rule of five" gave the police chief too much discretion.


OVERVIEW: The City exercised its authority under Wash. Rev. Code § 41.12.010 and enacted an ordinance codified as Seattle, Wash., Mun. Code ch. 4.08, which permitted its civil service commission to certify the names of candidates in the top 25 percent of the eligible register, or the top five candidates, whichever number was larger. The court of appeals found that the City's "rule of five" substantially accomplished the purpose of chapter 41.12, but that the "rule of 25 percent" did not. The court of appeals struck the impermissible language from the ordinance and validated the remainder. On review, the court held that, because the legislature had enacted another statute that permitted the certification of six names and thus believed that certifying six names could accomplish the purpose of promotions based on merit, the court would adhere to the legislature's "benchmark." The court held that cities would substantially accomplish the purpose of chapter 41.12, so long as their system provided for appointment by certification of no more than six names. The court also held that the court of appeals appropriately severed the invalid ordinance language pertaining to the "rule of 25 percent."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals judgment that held the City's "rule of five" procedure for promoting police officers substantially accomplished the statutory purpose of promoting officers on the basis of merit.

 

345 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Goins


No. 73177-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


151 Wn.2d 728; 92 P.3d 181; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 436



June 10, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Goins, 113 Wn. App. 723, 54 P.3d 723, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 2412 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was tried by a jury and convicted of second degree assault with the intent to commit indecent liberties pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.36.021(1)(e). However, the jury also found by subsequent special verdict that he had not acted with sexual motivation, pursuant to former Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.127 (1999), recodified as Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.835. The Court of Appeals (Washington) affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defense counsel made no objection to the apparently inconsistent verdicts. Defendant argued the inconsistent verdicts violated his rights to due process and therefore his second-degree assault conviction should be reversed. In the alternative, he argued that former Wash. Rev. Code § 4.44.440 could be applied in criminal cases, so the finding of his special verdict should control, thereby dismissing his general conviction for second degree assault. The special verdict was separate and distinct from the underlying conviction, and posed only to determine whether defendant should be registered as a sex offender. The testimony of all three witnesses established sufficient evidence to support the jury's finding that defendant was guilty of assault with intent to commit indecent liberties. Because the general guilty verdict was supported by sufficient evidence, the court of appeals was affirmed, upholding defendant' conviction. A special verdict supplemented a general conviction in order to determine if sex offender registration was warranted. Former Wash. Rev. Code § 4.44.440 could not apply to criminal cases and therefore provided no basis for the dismissal of defendant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The ruling of the court of appeals was affirmed, upholding defendant's conviction of second-degree assault with intent to commit indecent liberties.

Pers. Restraint of Addleman


No. 73857-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


151 Wn.2d 769; 92 P.3d 221; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 433



June 10, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
In re Personal Restraint of Addleman, 139 Wn.2d 751, 991 P.2d 1123, 2000 Wash. LEXIS 75 (2000)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a Washington Superior Court of first-degree statutory rape of a friend's child in 1979. In 2002, after many appeals, the Indeterminate Sentence Review Board (ISRB) determined that defendant was not rehabilitated and extended his minimum sentence by more than 14 years. Defendant filed a personal restraint petition (PRP) against the ISRB.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that his restraint was unlawful, because his sentence was significantly longer than the standard range provided for in the Sentencing Reform Act of 1981 (SRA), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 9.94A. The appellate court concluded that, between a statutory requirement that a prisoner was not released until rehabilitation was complete and a duty to attempt consistency with the SRA, the statutory requirement trumped the duty to attempt. The ISRB had to make reasonable attempts to set its minimum sentences consistent with the SRA, but had no duty to parole an unrehabilitated prisoner. Defendant did not meet his initial burden of arguing under established jurisprudence that the consideration of his slang dictionary was impermissible. There was ample evidence supporting the ISRB's conclusion that defendant was not rehabilitated and evidence to the contrary was duly considered. Psychological screening and evaluation tests suggested that defendant presented a high risk of committing another violent offense within six months after release. The ISRB properly concluded that defendant presented too great a risk to be released to the community at this time.


OUTCOME: Defendant's personal restraint petition was denied.

City of Redmond v. Moore


No. 72614-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


151 Wn.2d 664; 91 P.3d 875; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 374


February 13, 2003, Oral Argument  
June 3, 2004, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The City sought review of the decision of the district court (Washington), which found in favor of the two defendants and held that the mandatory suspension of their licenses pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.289 violated procedural due process. Defendants had been charged with driving while license suspended, in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.342(1)(c).


OVERVIEW: The case consolidated direct review of two separate district court orders dismissing charges against two defendants for driving while license suspended. In both cases, the district court concluded mandatory suspension of their licenses pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.289 violated procedural due process, because defendants were not afforded an administrative hearing by the Department of Licensing before or after the effective date of the suspensions. By implication, the district court's orders also invalidated Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.324(1), which provided that a person should not be entitled to an administrative hearing when the license suspension or revocation was mandatory. The supreme court affirmed, holding that Wash. Rev. Code §§ 46.20.289 and 46.20.324(1) violated due process. The statutes did not provide adequate procedural safeguards to ensure against the erroneous deprivation of a driver's interest in the continued use and possession of his or her driver's license. As such, because a driver could not be convicted of the offense of driving while license suspended where the suspension violated due process, the trial court properly dismissed the charges.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Port of Seattle v. Pollution Control


No. 73419-4


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


151 Wn.2d 568; 90 P.3d 659; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 361



May 14, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court of County Date filed: 10/29/2002. Judge signing: Hon. Suzanne M Barnett.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Port of Seattle and cross-petitioners, a community coalition, a citizens' group, and the State of Washington Department of Ecology, appealed the State of Washington Pollution Control Hearings Board's (PCHB) decision affirming, with 16 new conditions, Ecology's issuance of a water quality certification under § 401 of the Clean Water Act, 33 U.S.C.S. § 1341 to the Port so that it could fill wetlands to build a third airport runway.


OVERVIEW: Ecology issued a § 401 certification to the Port finding that there was reasonable assurance that the Port's project to build a third runway would not violate applicable state water quality standards under 40 C.F.R. § 121.2(a)(3). The PCHB affirmed, but added 16 new conditions it deemed necessary for reasonable assurance that state water quality standards would be met. After accepting direct review of the matter, the court held that the record showed there was reasonable assurance that the third runway project would meet state water quality standards. However, a water right was not necessary for reasonable assurance because the Port's low flow offset plan involved management of storm water, rather than its use where the Port would merely maintain existing stream hydrology. While there was minimal risk that in the absence of a water right the Port's access to its storm water could someday be threatened, the risk was properly accounted for in the reasonable assurance determination. Thus, the PCHB erroneously interpreted and applied Washington law by requiring the Port to obtain a water right as a prerequisite to capturing and detaining storm water for summer release into the streams.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the PCHB's conclusion that there was reasonable assurance that the runway project would not violate state water quality standards, affirmed PCHB conditions 1-4, 9-10, the first two sentences of condition 11, and conditions 12-15. The court overturned conditions 5, 6, the last sentence of condition 11, and condition 16. The court overturned conditions 7 and 8.

 

349 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Wash. Water Jet v. Yarbrough


No. 70814-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


151 Wn.2d 470; 90 P.3d 42; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 360


October 28, 2003, Oral Argument  
May 13, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:

 [***1] As amended by order of the Supreme Court May 27, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court of King County. Docket No: 99-2-20202-3. Judge signing: Hon. James a Doerty.
Wash. Water Jet Workers Ass'n v. Yarbrough, 148 Wn.2d 403, 61 P.3d 309, 2003 Wash. LEXIS 60 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant private industries brought an action against respondents, the Washington State Department of Corrections, Division of Correctional Industries and a private industry, alleging that an inmate labor contract under Wash. Rev. Code § 72.09.100(1) violated Wash. Const. art. II, § 29. The Superior Court of King County (Washington) entered a judgment for respondents. The court affirmed but granted the industries' motion for reconsideration.


OVERVIEW: Pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 72.09.100(1), the Department of Corrections created a program of inmate labor under which it entered into a contract with respondent industry. The contract provided access to inmate labor that competitors did not enjoy, allowed the use of correctional facility space rent-free, and provided free or discounted utilities. Respondent industry interviewed, hired, and paid the inmates. Respondents argued that Wash. Const. art. II, § 29, which provided that the State of Washington could not let out by contract the labor of inmates to a private entity, only referred to the sale or lease of a commodity. The court held that Wash. Rev. Code § 72.09.100(1) directly conflicted with Wash. Const. art. II, § 29. The court held that Wash. Const. art. II, § 29 limited inmate labor to profit the State rather than private enterprise and sought to prevent direct competition between inmate and free laborers. The program unconstitutionally let out inmate labor by contract for the benefit of private enterprises. To the extent that it held otherwise, Washington Water Jet Workers Association v. Yarbrough, 148 Wash. 2d 403, 61 P.3d 309 (2003) was overruled.


OUTCOME: The court overruled its prior decision to the extent that it conflicted with its opinion and remanded the matter to the trial court for further proceedings.

Arborwood Idaho v. Kennewick


No. 73197-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


151 Wn.2d 359; 89 P.3d 217; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 291


October 30, 2003, Oral Argument  
May 6, 2004, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
Arborwood Idaho, L.L.C. v. City of Kennewick, 113 Wn. App. 1032, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 2796 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner apartment complex sought review of the decision of the Court of Appeals (Washington), which affirmed the trial court's granting of summary judgment in favor of respondent, the City of Kennewick (City), and held that a monthly ambulance service charge was a valid regulatory fee.


OVERVIEW: The apartment complex challenged a city ordinance that imposed a monthly ambulance service charge on each household, business, and industry within the City. The trial court granted summary judgment in favor of the City, holding that the charge was a valid regulatory fee; the appellate court affirmed. The supreme court reversed the appellate court's decision, stating that the City's charge exceeded the taxing authority granted to cities and towns under Wash. Rev. Code § 35.21.768 and the charge was not justified as a regulatory fee, benefit charge, or a charge for direct services. The legislature explicitly provided authority for an excise tax to fund the ambulance services authorized by Wash. Rev. Code § 35.21.766 and that section included no language expressly authorizing a municipality to set rates for an ambulance service or to regulate the ambulance service. Accordingly, the supreme court was unpersuaded that § 35.21.766 implied the power to impose ambulance utility rates on every house, business, and industry mentioned in Wash. Rev. Code § 35.21.768.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed the appellate court's decision.

 

351 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Philippides v. Bernard


No. 73239-6 (consolidated with Nos. 73603-1, 73736-3, 74098-4)


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


151 Wn.2d 376; 88 P.3d 939; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 284



April 22, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:

As amended by order of the Supreme Court May 4, 2004.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In four different cases, plaintiff parents of deceased adult children brought various claims under the wrongful death and survival statutes against various defendants in federal district court. The federal court certified a question as to whether a parent of an adult child had to have been financially dependant on that child in order to recover for loss of consortium under Wash. Rev. Code § 4.24.010.


OVERVIEW: The four cases were consolidated for review and the parents argued that the legislature's 1998 amendment to Wash. Rev. Code § 4.24.010 redefined who could bring a cause of action under that statute and that the amendment allowed the parent of an adult child to recover upon a showing of emotional support upon the child. The supreme court disagreed, holding that the language of the statute and its legislative history did not support the conclusion that the requirement of financial dependency on adult children was addressed or changed by the 1998 amendments. Rather, § 4.24.010 required that parents be financially dependent on an adult child in order to recover for that child's injury or death. Contrary to the parents' contention, the supreme court also found there was a reasonable basis for the statute's treating the parents of adult children differently from parents of minor children. The lines drawn by § 4.24.010 bore a rational relationship to the purpose of the statute. As such, the supreme court held that the statute did not violate the constitutional guaranty of equal protection.


OUTCOME: The certified question was answered in the affirmative.

 

352 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State ex rel. Citizens v. Murphy


No. 73745-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


151 Wn.2d 226; 88 P.3d 375; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 212


September 18, 2003, Oral Argument  
April 8, 2004, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff nonprofit organization was concerned about tolling as a result of a bridge project. The organization filed suit against defendant State of Washington and claimed, among other things, that the State violated existing state bidding laws. The Thurston County Superior Court (Washington) granted summary judgment in favor of the State. The organization appealed.


OVERVIEW: The principal issues were whether the State violated state bidding laws in executing a design-build agreement and personal service contracts relating to the project, whether the State violated Wash. Rev. Code ch. 47.05, the priority programming statute, and whether Engrossed H.B. 2723 (Wash. 2002) (EHB) violated Wash. Const. art. II, § 19. The court concluded that summary judgment was proper. The court held that the Public-Private Transportation Initiatives Act (PPI Act) created an exemption for projects under the PPI Act from the requirements of the bidding laws. Therefore, the original agreement that the State entered into was lawfully executed. Because the original agreement was lawfully executed, the amended agreement, the design-build agreement, and the personal service contracts were not subject to the bidding laws. The court saw no conflict between the priority programming statute and the alternative bidding process under the PPI Act. Both statutes aimed at achieving efficiencies in developing transportation improvement projects. Finally, the court held that EHB 2723 did not violate the single-subject rule or the subject-in-title prong of Wash. Const. art. II, § 19.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

353 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Kilburn


NO. 73301-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


151 Wn.2d 36; 84 P.3d 1215; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 126


October 21, 2003, Oral Argument  
February 12, 2004, Filed


NOTICE:

As corrected by 169 Wn.2d errata.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The Name of this Case has been Corrected by the Court February 17, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court, County Honorable Susan R Agid.
State v. M.K., 114 Wn. App. 1006, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 3234 (2002).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After a trial, a superior court (Washington) entered judgment convicting defendant, a juvenile, of felony harassment under Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.46.020. Defendant petitioned for discretionary review, claiming that the State failed to prove that he actually intended to carry out an alleged threat made to a classmate and because his statements were intended only as a joke.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of felony harassment based on his statement to a classmate that he planned to bring a gun to school and shoot everyone in the class. On appeal, defendant claimed that the State failed to prove that he intended to carry out his threat and that this statements were intended only as a joke. Addressing the intent issue, the court held that neither the First Amendment nor § 9A.46.020 required the State to show that defendant intended to carry out the threat in order for the statements to constitute a true threat. However, after applying the rule of independent review, the court determined that the evidence was insufficient to support the conviction under § 9A.46.020. Defendant and the classmate to whom he made the alleged threat had no negative history and had joked with each other on occasion. Others in the class had also laughed at defendant's jokes. According to the classmate, defendant giggled as he made the comments, as if he had been joking. Only later did she think his statement might have been a threat. On such facts, the court held that a reasonable person in defendant's position would not have foreseen that his comments would be interpreted seriously.


OUTCOME: The court granted defendant's petition for discretionary review and reversed his conviction.

 

354 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Grant County Fire Prot. Dist. No. 5 v. City of Moses Lake


NOS. 70090-7, 70499-6, 73589-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 791; 83 P.3d 419; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 63


March 25, 2003, Oral Argument  
January 29, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected January 29, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: Grant County Fire Prot. Dist. No. 5 v. City of Moses Lake, 145 Wn.2d 702, 42 P.3d 394, 2002 Wash. LEXIS 178 (2002).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The supreme court granted respondents', three cities, motions for reconsideration after a finding in favor of appellants, fire districts and property owners, that the petition method of annexation afforded an impermissible privilege to the owners of land and thus violated Wash. Const. art. I, § 12. The superior court had granted summary judgment in favor of the first two cities and against the third city.


OVERVIEW: The court held prior that the petition method of annexation afforded an impermissible privilege to owners of highly valued land, and therefore violated Wash. Const. art. I, § 12. The first two cities filed motions for reconsideration. The court granted the motions and consolidated the third city's case with the two original cases. The court held that the petition method utilized by the first two cities was not unconstitutional and that the petition method of annexation employed by the third city was constitutional. Further, the fire districts had neither personal standing nor representational standing in the cases. After considering the Gunwall factors, the court stated that art. I, § 12 provided a basis for constitutional challenge independent from U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1. The petition method of annexation statutes were constitutional because they did not implicate a fundamental right and did not afford any class a privilege or immunity. Because the federal provision did not protect the right to petition the government for annexation, the court concluded that no such protection existed under the state constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial courts' grants of summary judgment in the cases of the first two cities and reversed the grant of summary judgment in the case of the third city.

 

355 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Campbell v. Dep't of Soc. & Health Servs.


No. 74204-9, (consolidated with No. 74206-5)


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 881; 83 P.3d 999; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 65


November 19, 2003, Oral Argument  
January 29, 2004, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant child sought review of the decision of the superior court (Washington), which held that respondent Department of Social and Health Services, Division of Developmental Disabilities (Department) correctly found that she was not eligible for services. The department sought review of the decision finding that the second child's equal protection rights were violated.


OVERVIEW: The cases were consolidated and a number of challenges were made to the decisions of the Department that the two children were no longer eligible for Division of Developmental Disability services provided for persons with developmental disabilities. The child in each case was reevaluated upon reaching age six, as required by Wash. Admin. Code § 388-825-030, and the Department determined that the children's conditions were not developmental disabilities. Prior to age six, Department services were available to a child at risk of developmental disabilities as measured by developmental delays. The superior court upheld the determination for the first child, and did not for the second child. The supreme court held that the department correctly determined that both children no longer qualified for Department services and that § 388-825-030 and Wash. Rev. Code § 71A.10.020 were not unconstitutional. The children also failed to show that the State law definition of developmentally disabled persons was preempted by federal law or that State law violated equal protection as applied in the cases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the superior court with regard to the first child and reversed the decision in regard to the second child.

 

356 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Goodman


No. 73533-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 774; 83 P.3d 410; 2004 Wash. LEXIS 61



January 15, 2004, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected February 2, 2004.


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Goodman, 114 Wn. App. 602, 59 P.3d 696, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 3074 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction of possession of a controlled substance with intent to deliver, complaining that the controlled substance was not specifically identified in the State's amended information. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed, holding that the State did not have to identify the specific controlled substance in the information. Defendant sought review.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant challenged the amended information and contended the evidence was insufficient to prove intent to deliver. The amended information upon which defendant was convicted identified the controlled substance as "meth" rather than the full name "methamphetamine," while the original information did fully identify the specific controlled substance. The supreme court found that, while the amount of methamphetamine alone might not have been sufficient to convict defendant, evidence, which included baggies later found to contain methamphetamine, a scale, additional baggies, and vials, was sufficient to convict defendant. However, as the statutory maximum sentence increased depending on which controlled substance defendant possessed, the State was obligated to allege and prove the substance defendant possessed was methamphetamine. Thus, the appellate court erred in ruling otherwise. Nonetheless, the State's failure to specifically identify the controlled substance in the amended information did not require reversal, as defendant had actual notice of the identity of the controlled substance that formed the basis of the charge by the arrest warrant and the first information.


OUTCOME: To the extent the appellate courts' opinion excused the State from alleging the specific controlled substance in an information, it was disapproved. However, the appellate court's judgment was affirmed in all other respects.
