Braam v. State


No. 72598-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 689; 81 P.3d 851; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 894


November 19, 2002, Argued  
December 18, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State Department of Social and Health Services (DSHS) sought review of the decision of the Superior Court (Washington), which granted an injunction in favor of respondents, class members, in the class members' action to improve the lives of foster children in the State's care.


OVERVIEW: The class action suit was brought against the DSHS in an effort to improve the lives of foster children in the State's care. Among other things, the class members sought to force the DSHS through a variety of legal claims to drastically reduce the number of time foster children were moved while in the State's care. The trial court granted an injunction substantially governing the State's foster care system. The court vacated, holding that foster children had substantive due process rights to be free of unreasonable risk of harm, and a right to reasonable safety. Alleged violations of that right were to be measured on the professional judgment standard. The jury instructions did not correctly articulate the right and the standard, and that the error was not harmless. The trial court also erred by admitting the ombudsman's report and evidence of national aspirational standards. The trial court did not err in its judgment on the use of fiscal constraints or by admitting evidence of past DSHS conduct. Wash. Rev. Code §§ 74.14A.050(2) and (3), and 74.13.250, and 74.13.280 did not create a private cause of action. 42 U.S.C.S. §§ 671(a)(16), 675(1) were not privately enforceable.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the injunction and verdict and remanded for further proceedings.

 

358 of 2278 DOCUMENTS




State v. Cheatam


No. 73079-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 626; 81 P.3d 830; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 892


June 26, 2003, Argued  
December 11, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of habeas corpus denied Cheatam v. Waddington, 177 Fed. Appx. 716, 2006 U.S. App. LEXIS 11122 (9th Cir. Wash., Apr. 24, 2006)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court.
State v. Cheatam, 112 Wn. App. 778, 51 P.3d 138, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 1832 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant's rape conviction after his first trial was reversed on appeal, and his second trial resulted in a mistrial prior to verdict. After a third trial, defendant was convicted of first-degree rape. The Court of Appeals (Washington) affirmed. Defendant petitioned for discretionary review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of raping a 16-year-old girl. On appeal, he claimed that the trial court should have excluded shoes retrieved from his jail property bag after his arrest on an unrelated charge. The tread of the shoes matched a footprint found at the rape scene. The court found no violation of U.S. Const. amend. IV by the warrantless search and seizure. Moreover, the shoes had already been exposed to police view when the property was inventoried. The court also found no violation of Wash. Const. art. I, § 7, which afforded broader protection that the Fourth Amendment, because, after the inventory of the shoes he wore, defendant no longer had a valid privacy interest in the inventoried items. The court also found no error in exclusion of expert testimony on the reliability of eyewitness identification. On the day of the rape the victim gave a detailed description of her attacker to a sketch artist, having purposefully noted his face, and she later selected defendant from a photograph montage. The expert's proposed testimony about the effects of stress on the memory would not have been likely to aid the jury in assessing the victim's credibility.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the intermediate appellate court's judgment.


 

359 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. C.G.


No. 73310-4


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 604; 80 P.3d 594; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 890


October 21, 2003, Oral Argument  
December 11, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. C.G., 114 Wn. App. 101, 55 P.3d 1204, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 2527 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed juvenile defendant's conviction of felony harassment for threatening to kill her school's vice principal. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant maintained that her juvenile conviction had to be reversed, because the State did not prove that the victim of her threat was placed in reasonable fear that she would carry out her threat to kill. To obtain a misdemeanor conviction based upon one of the threats listed in Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.46.020, the State had to prove the threat made and the threat feared were the same. The appellate court found that, under the plain language of § 9A.46.020, supported by Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.46.010, the State had to prove that the victim was placed in reasonable fear that the threat made was the one that would be carried out. Defendant's conviction for felony harassment had to be reversed, because there was no evidence that the victim was placed in reasonable fear that she would kill him. The inquiry into the nature of a threat depended on the facts and circumstances, and could not be limited to a literal translation of the words spoken. The State could still charge one who threatened to kill with threatening to inflict bodily injury, in the nature of a lesser-included offense, thus enabling a misdemeanor charge if the victim was placed in fear of bodily injury.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and remanded.

 

360 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Wash. Educ. Ass'n v. Wash. State Pub. Disclosure Comm'n


No. 72877-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 612; 80 P.3d 608; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 891


June 24, 2003, Oral Argument  
December 11, 2003, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent public school union challenged the guidelines appellant Washington State Public Disclosure Commission (PDC) issued interpreting the laws and rules governing the use of public facilities in campaigns under Wash. Rev. Code ch. 42.17. The Superior Court of King County, Washington, declared that the guidelines were unconstitutional and constituted an arbitrary and capricious agency action. The PDC appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the PDC contended, inter alia, that, because the guidelines were the agency's opinion only and could not be violated or enforced, the union was seeking an advisory opinion on an abstract dispute over whether the PDC's opinion violated constitutional rights and/or the state's administrative law. The supreme court found that the PDC issued the guidelines as an aid to the public, interpreting the laws and rules concerning the use of public facilities in campaigns. The guidelines were advisory and did not purport to be anything more. Moreover, there was no evidence indicating that the PDC acted to enforce the guidelines or regulate the union in any manner. As the union challenged the PDC's opinion only, there was no "agency action" for the supreme court to review pursuant to the union's petition for judicial review. Whether the guidelines were a correct or incorrect interpretation of the law presented nothing more than an academic or hypothetical question. The guidelines had no legal or regulatory effect, and the PDC's issuance of the guidelines did not implicate actual or direct legal interests of the union. Thus, the trial court erred in reviewing the union's claims.


OUTCOME: The trial court's orders, including the permanent injunction and grant of attorney fees was reversed, and the case was dismissed.

Malted Mousse, Inc. v. Steinmetz


No. 73120-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 518; 79 P.3d 1154; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 812


September 9, 2003, Oral Argument  
November 13, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified by Malted Mousse, Inc. v. Steinmetz, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 200 (Wash., Mar. 11, 2004)
Reconsideration denied by Malted Mousse, Inc. v. Steinmetz, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 201 (Wash., Mar. 11, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
Malted Mousse, Inc. v. Steinmetz, 113 Wn. App. 157, 52 P.3d 555, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 1963 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff seller sued defendant buyer when the buyer put a stop-order on a check he gave the seller because he was dissatisfied with the equipment and service he purchased. The trial court transferred the case to arbitration, which resulted in a decision in the seller's favor. The buyer's motion for trial de novo/review of award of attorney fees was denied. The Washington Court of Appeals, Division Two, reversed. The seller appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the supreme court determined that the act of confirming and vacating an arbitration award pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 7.04.150 did not apply to mandatory arbitrations such as the one in the instant case; such a motion was applicable only to arbitration awards from Wash. Rev. Code ch. 7.04 arbitrations. The sole way to appeal an erroneous ruling from mandatory arbitration was the trial de novo. Further, the manifest procedural error standard did not apply to mandatory arbitration. As the legislature provided a meaningful avenue for relief through the trial de novo, the appellate court erred by utilizing a constitutional writ to reverse the arbitrator's award under Wash. Rev. Code ch. 7.06. As the buyer failed to properly file his request within 20 days after the arbitrator's award was filed, he could no longer seek review by trial de novo. The seller was not entitled to attorney fees.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment was reversed and the case was remanded to the trial court for entry of judgment in accordance with the original arbitration award. The seller's motion for attorney fees was denied, but it is awarded its costs on appeal.

Okeson v. City of Seattle


No. 73227-2, No. 73228-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 540; 78 P.3d 1279; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 813


May 20, 2003, Oral Argument  
November 13, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Subsequent appeal at Okeson v. City of Seattle, 130 Wn. App. 814, 125 P.3d 172, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 3190 (Wash. Ct. App., Dec. 19, 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant customers of a municipal electric company challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of King County (Washington), which was in favor of respondent city, in the city's motion for summary judgment on its declaratory action to validate an ordinance allowing a transfer of responsibility to pay for streetlights from the city to the customers.


OVERVIEW: The city passed an ordinance transferring responsibility to pay for streetlights from the city's general budget to its municipal electric company's customers. The city brought a declaratory action to validate the ordinance after questions regarding its legality were raised. After an initial ruling against the ordinance, Wash. Rev. Code § 35.92.050 was amended by 2002 Wash. Laws ch. 102 to allow municipalities to incorporate streetlight costs into a utility's general rate structure. The trial court reversed itself and granted the city summary judgment upholding the ordinance. The customers appealed, claiming the ordinance imposed an illegal tax. The court agreed. The court found that providing streetlights was a governmental function and the main purpose behind the city's ordinance was to shift the streetlight costs to free up revenue for the city, and not to pay for regulating streetlights. Therefore, the ordinance created a tax. Since the ordinance did not clearly state that it was creating a tax, as required by Wash. Const. art. VII, § 5, and because Wash. Rev. Code § 35.21.870(1) required taxpayer approval for any tax in excess of six percent, the tax was unlawful.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the summary judgment grant in favor of the city.

State v. Baldwin


No. 72710-4


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 448; 78 P.3d 1005; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 738


March 27, 2003, Argued  
November 6, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected November 26, 2003.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court.
State v. Baldwin, 111 Wn. App. 631, 45 P.3d 1093, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 782 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted on three counts of identity theft and two counts of forgery; concurrent exceptional sentences of 36 months on all counts were imposed. The Washington Court of Appeals affirmed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, defendant contended that her convictions violated double jeopardy prohibitions and that the statutory factors upon which her exceptional sentences were based were unconstitutionally vague. The supreme court disagreed, holding that defendant's convictions on forgery and theft of identity did not violate the double jeopardy provisions of the Washington and United States Constitutions, because they were not the same in law and fact. Further, because sentencing guideline statutes were not subject to a vagueness analysis, defendant's claim that the sentencing guideline statutes at issue were unconstitutionally vague was without merit.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Pierce County v. State


No. 73607-3


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 422; 78 P.3d 640; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 732


June 26, 2003, Oral Argument  
October 30, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Reconsideration denied by Pierce County v. State, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 198 (Wash., Mar. 9, 2004)
Modified by Pierce County v. State, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 197 (Wash., Mar. 9, 2004)
Appeal after remand at Pierce County v. State, 159 Wn.2d 16, 148 P.3d 1002, 2006 Wash. LEXIS 895 (Wash., Dec. 7, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, a county, transit authority, and others, filed a complaint seeking declaratory and injunctive relief on the ground that the newly-passed Initiative Measure 776 (I-776) was unconstitutional; appellants, the State, a homeowners association, and others, intervened and sought a declaration that I-776 was constitutional. The King County Superior Court, Washington, declared I-776 unconstitutional. The State and the association appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court declared I-776 unconstitutional on the grounds that it embraced more than a single subject and substantially impaired the county's contractual obligations to its bondholders. In challenging the trial court's holding, the State contended, inter alia, that the sole subject of I-776 was the placement of a ceiling on government fees that citizens had to pay to license their vehicles, and that the 11 sections of the initiative were rationally related to that subject. The supreme court agreed, holding that precatory language could not yield additional "subjects" for Wash. Const. art. II, § 19 purposes; thus, the county and the authority failed to show beyond a reasonable doubt that I-776 required the people to cast a single vote on two unrelated, proposed laws. Nor did the county and the authority meet their burden of showing that I-776's ballot title failed to notify members of the legislature and the public of the subject matter of the measure, that it substantially impaired the county's contractual obligation to its bondholders, that it violated constitutional precepts of local home rule, or that it deprived the voters of a vested property right.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the matter was remanded for further proceedings.

Hough v. Stockbridge


NO. 73365-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 234; 76 P.3d 216; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 656



September 11, 2003, Filed

Related proceeding at Hough v. Stockbridge, 129 Wn. App. 1037, 2005 Wash. App. LEXIS 3082 (2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Superior Court.
Hough v. Stockbridge, 113 Wn. App. 532, 54 P.3d 192, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 2366 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner neighbors appealed from an order of the Court of Appeals (Washington), which affirmed a district court's refusal to extend a protection order against respondent neighbors and vacated a protection order that had been initially entered against petitioners.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners had sought protection from harassment by respondents. After a hearing, the district court issued protection orders preventing either party from contacting the other, even though only petitioners had sought an order. Each order had a duration of one year. Just before the orders expired, petitioners filed a motion to extend the protection order against respondents and to have the order against them lifted. The district court, sitting in equity, had power to issue mutual protection orders on its own motion under Wash. Const. art. IV, § 6 and Wash. Rev. Code § 10.14.080(6), which specifically granted broad discretion to fashion relief.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the court of appeals, insofar as it vacated the restraining order against petitioners.

State v. Smith


NO. 73113-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 135; 75 P.3d 934; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 562


March 11, 2003, Oral Argument  
August 28, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Smith v. Wash., 541 U.S. 909, 158 L. Ed. 2d 256, 124 S. Ct. 1616, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 1955 (U.S., Mar. 8, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Smith, 113 Wn. App. 1018, 2002 Wash. App. LEXIS 2739 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following convictions for burglary in the first degree and criminal trespass in the first degree, the trial court found defendant to be a persistent offender under the Persistent Offender Accountability Act (POAA), Wash. Rev. Code § 9.94A.570, and sentenced him to life without the possibility of parole. Division One of the Court of Appeals (Washington) affirmed the sentence, and defendant sought review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the sentencing procedures of the POAA under both the Sixth Amendment and Wash. Const. art. I, §§ 21 and 22, claiming that he had a federal and state constitutional right to have a jury determine whether he committed prior offenses. The court disagreed. The court rejected defendant's claim under U.S. Const. amend. VI, holding that recidivism need not have been pleaded and proved to a jury beyond a reasonable doubt and that no case overruled current case law on that issue. The court agreed with defendant that, under the Gunwall analysis, the Washington Constitution appeared to provide greater protection of the jury trial right than did the federal Constitution, but held that defendant provided insufficient evidence that such broader protection included the right to a jury trial on the fact of prior convictions at sentencing. Most importantly, the court noted that no such right existed by statute or at common law at the time Wash. Const. art. I, §§ 21 and 22 were written; therefore, it was unlikely that the drafters contemplated such a right in §§ 21 and 22.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

In re Pers. Restraint of Turay


No. 73255-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


150 Wn.2d 71; 74 P.3d 1194; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 561


March 13, 2003, Oral Argument  
August 21, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Post-conviction relief denied at  In re Pers. Restraint of Turay, 153 Wn.2d 44, 101 P.3d 854, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 843 (Wash., Nov. 18, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY:  In re Detention of Turay, 139 Wn.2d 379, 986 P.2d 790, 1999 Wash. LEXIS 794 (1999)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a personal restraint petition challenging his civil commitment as a sexually violent predator. The personal restraint petition, his third, was filed in the Washington Court of Appeals well over one year from the time of his commitment, thus raising the issue of whether the Wash. Rev. Code § 10.73.090 one-year time limit for filing a personal restraint petition applied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was committed as a sexually violent predator pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code ch. 71.09. He had a history of convictions of sexually violent crimes. While he was incarcerated following a parole revocation, a prosecutor petitioned for his commitment as a sexually violent predator. The petition did not allege that defendant committed a "recent overt act," i.e., an act that either caused harm of a sexually violent nature or created a reasonable apprehension of such harm. The appellate court held that, as Albrecht did not constitute a significant change in the law, as defendant claimed, he had not established that the exception in Wash. Rev. Code § 10.73.090. The appellate court also rejected defendant's claim that there had been a significant change in the law bringing him within an exception to the one-year limitation. Finally, it concluded that defendant filed, at best, a "mixed petition" that had to be dismissed without consideration of other issues.


OUTCOME: The personal restraint petition was dismissed.

 

368 of 2278 DOCUMENTS




In re Det. of Thorell


No. 69574-1 (consolidated with Nos. 70488-1, 70967-0, 71059-7, 71315-4 & 70405-8)


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


149 Wn.2d 724; 72 P.3d 708; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 463


April 10, 2003, Argued  
July 10, 2003, Filed


NOTICE:

As corrected by 160 Wn.2d errata.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Thorell v. Washington, 541 U.S. 990, 124 S. Ct. 2015, 158 L. Ed. 2d 496, 2004 U.S. LEXIS 2802 (2004)
Related proceeding at In re Det. of Johnson, 2007 Wash. App. LEXIS 3088 (Wash. Ct. App., Nov. 19, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY: State v. Strauss (In re Strauss), 106 Wn. App. 1, 20 P.3d 1022, 2001 Wash. App. LEXIS 511 (2001)
State v. Gordon (In re Gordon), 102 Wn. App. 912, 10 P.3d 500, 2000 Wash. App. LEXIS 2018 (2000)
In re Detention of Johnson, 106 Wn. App. 1015, 2001 Wash. App. LEXIS 1722 (2001)
In re Detention of Bishop, 104 Wn. App. 1029, 2001 Wash. App. LEXIS 687 (2001)
In re Detention of Ross, 102 Wn. App. 108, 6 P.3d 625, 2000 Wash. App. LEXIS 1527 (2000)
In re Detention of Thorell, 99 Wn. App. 1041, 2000 Wash. App. LEXIS 2899 (2000)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In separate actions consolidated on appeal, petitioner inpatients sought review of whether the sexually violent predator act (SVPA), Wash. Rev. Code ch. 71.09, violated the Equal Protection and Due Process Clauses of the U.S. Constitution, whether actuarial evidence could be admitted to aid in predicting future dangerousness, and whether their respective commitment judgments were subject to reversal.


OVERVIEW: The inpatients were ordered to be confined under the SPVA. The court consolidated their respective appeals from such judgments because of common issues, including claims that the SPVA was unconstitutional. The court held first that the Due Process Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV did not require a separate finding that a sexually violent predator (SVP) had serious difficulty controlling dangerous, sexually predatory behavior but only that there was a connection between the mental illness and the inability to control one's behavior. The court also held the State did not need to prove that an inpatient was completely unable to control his or her behavior. Additionally, the court found that SPVA provision limiting consideration of less restrictive alternatives to confinement until after an inpatient's first annual review did not violate the Equal Protection Clause because the protection of the public from the danger of SVPs and the effective treatment of SVPs constituted rational bases for the restriction. The court also held that actuarial instruments admitted to aid in the prediction of future dangerousness were not novel scientific evidence; hence, no Frye hearing was required.


OUTCOME: The court held that the SVPA was not unconstitutional and affirmed the inpatients' respective commitment judgments.

In re Pers. Restraint Petition of Hankerson


No. 72844-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


149 Wn.2d 695; 72 P.3d 703; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 459


March 11, 2003, Oral Argument  
July 3, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1]
 State v. Mitchell, 117 Wn.2d 521, 817 P.2d 398, 1991 Wash. LEXIS 369 (1991)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought review of the decision of a Washington appellate court, which dismissed the inmate's personal restraint petition, as it was filed after the one-year time limit of Wash. Rev. Code § 10.73.090, and at least one of the claims it contained did not fall within an exception in Wash. Rev. Code § 10.73.100.


OVERVIEW: After the inmate was convicted of aggravated first-degree murder and sentenced to life in prison without the possibility of parole and the conviction was affirmed, he filed a personal restraint petition. The appellate court dismissed the petition, as it was filed after the one-year time limit of Wash. Rev. Code § 10.73.090, and at least one of the claims it contained did not fall within an exception in Wash. Rev. Code § 10.73.100. The court affirmed on appeal, stating that, if a personal restraint petition with multiple claims was filed after the one-year period expired and the court determined that at least one of the claims was time barred, the petition must be dismissed. It was obvious that the inmate filed a mixed petition that must be dismissed. His personal restraint petition listed four grounds for relief and the second was that the admission of court statements was erroneous under a United States Supreme Court case. However, the case did not represent significant new law material to the inmate's conviction. Accordingly, his personal restraint petition was correctly dismissed as a mixed petition.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

370 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Parents Involved in Cmty. Schs v. Seattle Sch. Dist. No. 1


No. 72712-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


149 Wn.2d 660; 72 P.3d 151; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 456


October 24, 2002, Oral Argument  
June 26, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
  Parents Involved in Cmty. Schs v. Order Seattle Sch. Dist., 294 F.3d 1085, 2002 U.S. App. LEXIS 11881 (9th Cir., 2002).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant nonprofit corporation, formed by parents whose children had been denied admission to their preferred high school, brought suit against appellee school district, challenging its "open choice" plan. The plan was found not to violate state or federal law and the district's summary judgment motion was granted. On review, the U.S. Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit certified a state law question regarding Wash. Rev. Code § 49.60.400.


OVERVIEW: The underlying action arose when the district adopted a plan that allowed students a measure of choice of schools while attempting to ensure that schools did not become segregated. Because some schools were oversubscribed and not all students had their first choice, the district applied a series of tie breakers, one of which was specifically designed to promote racial diversity. In answering the federal court's certified question in the negative, the supreme court determined that this "open choice" plan's use of a racially cognizant tie breaker did not violate Wash. Rev. Code § 49.60.400 as it did not discriminate against, or grant preferential treatment to, any individual or group on the basis of race, sex, color, ethnicity, or national origin. To the extent the tie breaker was race conscious, the supreme court determined that it furthered a core mission of public education; to make available an equal, uniform and enriching educational environment to all students within the district. Affirmative action programs which advanced a less qualified applicant over a more qualified applicant were held to be impermissible while programs which were racially neutral were lawful.


OUTCOME: The certified question was answered in the negative and the case was returned to the federal court for further proceedings.

City of Seattle v. Crispin


No. 72515-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


149 Wn.2d 896; 71 P.3d 208; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 453


March 13, 2003, Argued  
June 19, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by  City of Seattle v. Crispin, 2003 Wash. LEXIS 570 (Wash., Sept. 2, 2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
 City of Seattle v. Crispin, 108 Wn. App. 1027, 2001 Wn. App. LEXIS 3006 (2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent city brought a foreclosure action. Petitioner property owner, who counterclaimed for declaratory relief, sought reversal of an opinion of the Washington Court of Appeals, which affirmed a lower court decision that a tax lot he purchased was not created in compliance with Wash. Rev. Code ch. 58.17, the state statute on lot boundary adjustments, and the city's ordinance governing the division of land.


OVERVIEW: The primary issue in this case concerned whether the division of land that created the owner's tax lot qualified as a boundary line adjustment for purposes of the exemption from the subdivision statutes set forth in Wash. Rev. Code § 58.17.040(6). The owner asserted that, because the tax lot was a division made for the purpose of alteration by adjusting boundary lines and did not create additional lots, it was exempt from the requirements of Wash. Rev. Code ch. 58.17 governing land division, which the city's ordinance mirrored, and was a legal lot. The city claimed that the creation of the tax lot neither complied with applicable subdivision regulations nor qualified as an exemption under Wash. Rev. Code § 58.17.040(6). The instant court concluded that the tax lot was a legally created lot with the statutory exemption under § 58.17.040(6). The reconfiguration of boundaries resulted in no new lots. Three lots existed before the adjustment, and three lots existed after the adjustment. The fact that a buildable site was created did not prevent application of the statutory boundary line adjustment exemption.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed.

Citizens for Responsible Wildlife Mgmt. v. State


No. 72186-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


149 Wn.2d 622; 71 P.3d 644; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 452


November 19, 2002, Argued  
June 19, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Related proceeding at Citizens for Responsible Wildlife Mgmt. v. State, 124 Wn. App. 566, 103 P.3d 203, 2004 Wn. App. LEXIS 2960 (Wash. Ct. App., Dec. 7, 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wildlife management organization challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Thurston County (Washington), which granted summary judgment in favor of respondents, the State and initiative sponsors, and held that Initiative 713 (codified at Wash. Rev. Code §§ 77.15.192-.198) did not violate Wash. Const. art. II, §§ 19, 37.


OVERVIEW: Wash. Rev. Code §§ 77.15.192-.198 outlawed the trapping or killing of any mammal with body-gripping traps, sodium fluoroacetate, or sodium cyanide. The organization was opposed to this initiative and claimed that it violated Wash. Const. art. II, §§ 19, 37. The trial court disagreed and granted the State and sponsors summary judgment. The court affirmed on appeal. Wash. Const. art. II, § 19 required that bills only contain one subject and that subject be expressed in its title. The statutory provisions addressed methods of trapping and killing animals, and the initiative title banned certain traps and certain poisons. Because particular types of traps and poison used fell within the auspices of methods of trapping and killing animals, the single subject and subject expressed in the title rules had not been violated. There was also no violation of Wash. Const. art. II, § 37, as the scope of rights created or affected by the statutory provisions were able to be ascertained without referring to any other statute or enactment, and the statutory provisions did not render existing rights erroneous. The organization failed to show that the statutory provisions were unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

 

373 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



City of Redmond v. Arroyo-Murillo


NO. 72599-3


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


149 Wn.2d 607; 70 P.3d 947; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 448


August 22, 2002, Oral Argument  
June 12, 2003, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant City challenged a decision of the Superior Court of King County (Washington), which upheld the trial court's dismissal of license revocation charges against defendant and held that due process was not satisfied when the Department of Licensing (DOL) (Washington) mailed a license revocation notice to an address obtained from a traffic ticket rather than the address provided to the DOL by the license holder.


OVERVIEW: The DOL revoked defendant's license and notified him by sending him an order of revocation and a hearing request form. This notice was sent to defendant's address of record, which the DOL obtained from his latest traffic ticket. Thereafter, a police officer cited defendant for a traffic violation. When the City learned of this, it charged defendant with driving with a suspended license. Defendant argued that his due process rights were violated because the DOL did not send the notice of revocation to defendant's address on his identicard application. The superior court agreed and upheld the dismissal of the charges. The court reversed on appeal. Pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.205 and Wash. Admin. Code § 308-104-018, the DOL was authorized to update defendant's address based on the traffic ticket because it was written documentation that was signed by defendant and filed by a governmental agency. There was no due process violation because the DOL complied with statutory requirements when it mailed the notice to the updated address. The court reversed the superior court's holding that § 308-104-018 conflicted with Wash. Rev. Code § 46.20.205 as interpreted by case law.


OUTCOME: The court reversed.

 

374 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Pauling


No. 71794-0


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


149 Wn.2d 381; 69 P.3d 331; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 369


June 27, 2002, Oral Argument  
May 22, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Pauling v. Washington, 540 U.S. 986, 124 S. Ct. 470, 157 L. Ed. 2d 379, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 8076 (2003)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
State v. Pauling, 108 Wn. App. 445, 31 P.3d 47, 2001 Wash. App. LEXIS 2084 (2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state challenged the Court of Appeals for the State of Washington decision that the extortion statute, Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.56.130 (1975), lacked a requirement that the threat be wrongful, declared former unconstitutionally overbroad, as it prohibited threats that constituted protected speech, and reversed. Respondent individual's sentence was stayed, pending the present appeal.


OVERVIEW: The individual was convicted of extortion for threatening to disseminate and, in actuality, disseminating nude photos of a former girlfriend to collect a small claims court judgment he had against her. The individual appealed to the Court of Appeals on the grounds that the extortion statute under which he was convicted was unconstitutionally overbroad. The question before the instant court was whether Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.56.130 (1975) encroached upon a real and substantial amount of free speech. The trial court concluded that the individual knowingly and intentionally attempted to obtain the judgment from the girlfriend by threat and that such threat communicated to the girlfriend an intent to continue to send the nude photos to friends, family, and neighbors. Clearly, there was no nexus between the threat to send nude photos to family and friends and the collection of a lawful judgment. The present court construed the statute, and, using the nexus limiting construction, the individual's conduct constituted second-degree extortion. It was no defense for the individual to assert that his conduct was not extortionate solely because he was trying to collect a legal debt.


OUTCOME: The appellate court was reversed, and the judgment and sentence of the trial court was reinstated.

Wash. Ass'n of Neighborhood Stores v. State


No. 72456-3


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


149 Wn.2d 359; 70 P.3d 920; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 362


October 15, 2002, Argued  
May 8, 2003, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a store association and others, filed a declaratory judgment action against respondent State of Washington, challenging the constitutionality of the Tobacco Tax Initiative, 2002 Wash. Laws ch. 2, §§ 1-4 (I-773). On cross motions for summary judgment, the Thurston County Superior Court entered summary judgment in favor of respondents and dismissed appellants' complaint. Appellants sought review.


OVERVIEW: Intervenor organization became a part to appellants' action, which claimed that 2002 Wash. Laws ch. 2, § 2, of I-773, which imposed additional tax on cigarettes and provided that the revenues were to be used to fund certain existing and future healthcare and anti-tobacco programs, was unconstitutional. The court disagreed. The enactment did not violate the biennium bar of Wash. Const. art. VIII, § 4, as the plain language of 2002 Wash. Laws ch. 2, § 2(2)(b), (c) merely authorized appropriations for the several programs to be funded, but required further action by the legislature to cause the appropriations to be made. There was also no violation of Wash. Const. art. II, § 19. The title of I-773 expressed a general subject, additional tobacco taxes for low-income health care and other programs, the title was broad, and the provisions were rationally related; hence, there was no violation of the single subject requirement. The title also gave adequately notice as to the scope and purpose of the law. I-773 did not revise any law that was not written out in full in the enactment; hence, there was no violation of Wash. Const. art. II, § 37. I-773 was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

 

376 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Northwest Ecosystem Alliance v. Wash. Forest Practices Bd.


No. 70874-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


149 Wn.2d 67; 66 P.3d 614; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 282


September 19, 2002, Argued  
April 10, 2003, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
Northwest Ecosystem Alliance v. Dep't of Ecology, 104 Wn. App. 901, 17 P.3d 697, 2001 Wash. App. LEXIS 286 (2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Washington Court of Appeals, Division Two, reversed a trial court's dismissal of a claim by respondent conservation organizations that petitioner state agencies failed to promulgate rules that adequately protected certain natural resources. The court of appeals also reversed the dismissal of the organizations' challenge to the validity of several forest practice regulations. The instant court granted the agencies' petition for review.


OVERVIEW: The organizations asserted that the agencies failed to perform a duty to adopt rules that they were required by statute to promulgate. The organizations claimed that the Washington Administrative Procedure Act did not provide the exclusive means for obtaining judicial review and that the instant court had the power to review the case even though administrative remedies had not been exhausted. The instant court held that the court of appeals erred in reversing the trial court's dismissal of the organizations' failure-to-act claims. The organizations could judicially challenge the agencies' failure to promulgate rules, but before doing so, they had to petition the agencies for rule making. The organizations' failure to formally petition for rule making precluded their judicial action. The organizations were not excused from failing to petition for rule making. The court of appeals erred in holding that a petition for rule making was not a prerequisite to judicial review of the failure-to-act claims. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in concluding that primary jurisdiction resided with the agencies.


OUTCOME: The instant court affirmed the court of appeals only to the extent it held that the organizations could obtain judicial review of an agency's failure to adopt rules. The instant court reversed the court of appeals' ruling on the issue of exhaustion of administrative remedies. It also reversed the court of appeals' decision on the issue of primary jurisdiction.

Wash. Indep. Tel. Ass'n v. Wash. Utils. & Transp. Comm'n

January 16, 2003, Oral Argument  
March 20, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court, Thurston County; 002001620. Honorable Daniel J. Berschauer, Judge.
Wash. Indep. Tel. Ass'n v. Wash. Utils. & Transp. Comm'n, 110 Wn. App. 498, 41 P.3d 1212, 2002 Wn. App. LEXIS 390 (2002)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant telephone company trade association claimed that appellee utilities commission violated the association members' due process rights by designating, at an open public meeting, appellee telephone company an additional telecommunications provider in rural areas. The superior court denied the association's administrative appeal and affirmed the commission's orders, and the Court of Appeals (Washington) affirmed. The association appealed.


OVERVIEW: The association members argued that, as recipients of federal funding, they had a property interest in their designations as sole telecommunications providers and that they were therefore entitled to a full adjudicative hearing on the petition of the company. Alternatively, they argued that the commission's decision was arbitrary and capricious under the Washington Administrative Procedure Act (WAPA), Wash. Rev. Code § 34.05.001 et seq. The Supreme Court of Washington concluded first that the association members had not established that they had a constitutionally protected U.S. Const. amend. Second, the supreme court concluded that the association and its members, independent rural telephone companies, had not shown that the commission acted arbitrarily or capriciously in concluding that the telephone company met the statutory requirements for designation as an additional eligible telecommunications carrier in the association's area.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed.

State v. Mertens


No. 72081-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 820; 64 P.3d 633; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 141


October 17, 2002, Oral Argument  
March 6, 2003, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was found guilty of commercial fishing without a license in the first degree under Wash. Rev. Code § 77.15.500 and former Wash. Rev. Code § 77.15.110(1). The Washington Court of Appeals reversed, finding that the two statutes created an irrebuttable presumption that relieved the State from independently establishing defendant acted with commercial purposes. The State appealed.


OVERVIEW: The underlying action arose when department of fish and wildlife enforcement officers caught defendant with more than three times the personal bag limit of geoducks while he was harvesting geoducks without a license; defendant maintained that he had been diving in the area to feed his large family and that he did not intend to resell the geoducks. The appellate court held that the statutory scheme created an unconstitutional conclusive presumption. On review, the supreme court disagreed, finding that the language and definitional structure of former Wash. Rev. Code § 77.15.110(1)(c) indicated that no presumption as to defendant's state of mind at the time of the offense was required; the language equated particular conduct with the element "acting for commercial purposes" and listed the conduct that satisfied that element. The supreme court concluded that the legislature intended commercial fishing without a license to be a strict liability crime.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's decision was reversed and defendant's conviction was affirmed.

Wash. Indep. Tel. Ass'n v. Wash. Utils. & Transp. Comm'n

SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 887; 64 P.3d 606; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 147


November 19, 2002, Oral Argument  
March 6, 2003, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent telecommunication companies challenged, in the trial court, petitioner utilities commission's rule regulating a particular source of their revenue. The trial court upheld the rule, but the Court of Appeals (Washington) reversed the trial court's judgment. The commission sought further review.


OVERVIEW: The Washington Utilities and Transportation Commission (Commission) adopted Wash. Admin. Code § 480-120-540, limiting local telephone exchange carriers' charges to interexchange carriers for terminating long distance telephone calls. The supreme court held the regulation did not set rates but only described a method for setting rates, the Commission had authority under Wash. Rev. Code ch. 34.05. Applying the correct definition of "arbitrary and capricious," the rule was not willful, unreasoning, or taken without regard to the attending facts or circumstances due to its suggested alternatives to make up lost revenue, as the companies could seek adjustments of their rates, if they did not receive sufficient revenue, potential losses while setting new rates did not make the rule arbitrary or capricious, nor was reliance on unreliable universal service cost determinations mandated. Excluding loop costs from recoverable costs of terminating calls was a permissible policy decision.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was reversed, and the trial court's decision was reinstated.

State v. Acrey

SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 738; 64 P.3d 594; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 140

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a juvenile, sought discretionary review of a decision of the Court of Appeals, Division One, Washington, which affirmed his disposition in the King County Superior Court, Juvenile Division, Washington, for possession of cocaine and marijuana, and an order denying his motion to suppress evidence.


OVERVIEW: The 12-year-old juvenile was initially detained after midnight in a brief investigative stop, after police responded to an emergency telephone call reporting juveniles fighting on a city street in an isolated commercial area. The officers concluded he was not involved in criminal activity, but continued to detain him while they telephoned his mother. In response to her request to transport him home, the officers conducted a pat down search prior to placing him in a car and found two baggies of marijuana. A search incident to arrest further uncovered money, crack cocaine, and additional marijuana. However, he asserted that he was impermissibly detained during the time the police telephoned his mother. The court held that the appellate court correctly determined that the officers' intrusion in detaining the juvenile long enough to ask him for his name and telephone number and to telephone his mother was minimal, and that the officers acted reasonably under the circumstances.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals, holding that the police officers acted reasonably under their community caretaking function in detaining the juvenile while they telephoned his mother, was affirmed.

 

383 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Mt. Hood Bev. Co. v. Constellation Brands, Inc.


No. 72882-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


149 Wn.2d 98; 63 P.3d 779; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 136


November 21, 2002, Argued  
February 20, 2003, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As amended by order of the Supreme Court April 30, 2003.
Reconsideration denied by Mt. Hood Bev. Co. v. Constellation Brands, Inc., 2003 Wash. LEXIS 359 (Wash., Apr. 30, 2003).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In two separate cases, respondents, out-of-state suppliers of wine, alleged to have violated Wash. Rev. Code § 19.126, counterclaimed and moved for summary judgment on the grounds that § 19.126 was unconstitutional. The Superior Court, King County, Washington, granted the suppliers summary judgment. Appellant distributors appealed. Division One of the Court of Appeals, Washington, consolidated the cases and certified both for review.


OVERVIEW: On appeal the distributors argued that the stated purpose of Wash. Rev. Code ch. 19.126, which regulated the contractual rights and obligations of wine suppliers and distributors to one another, was to serve legitimate local concerns, that it was not enacted for protectionist purposes, and that it survived commerce clause scrutiny. The court disagreed. The court held that the express exemption of in-state wine suppliers in Wash. Rev. Code ch. 19.126 facially violated the dormant commerce clause because it protected in-state wine suppliers, thereby discriminating against out-of-state suppliers, that the legislation was not saved by U.S. Const. amend. XXI, because exempting in-state suppliers from Wash. Rev. Code ch. 19.126 did not fall within the core concerns of the amendment, and that the exemption of in-state suppliers could not be severed from the remainder of Wash. Rev. Code ch. 19.126 because to do so would impermissibly broaden the statute. The court also affirmed the award of attorney fees to the suppliers, holding that defending against an action based on a statute by successfully arguing the statute was unconstitutional allowed an award of attorney fees under that statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the superior court striking down the challenged statute, held that the supplier in the first case had not breached its oral contract with its Washington distributor, upheld the award of attorney fees and costs at trial, and awarded the suppliers fees and costs on appeal. The court also remanded for resolution of statutory claims that were not before it on appeal consistent with the court's opinion.

State v. O'Neill


No. 70945-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 564; 62 P.3d 489; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 71


November 29, 2001, Argued  
January 30, 2003, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with drug offenses. The court granted defendant's motion to suppress. The State appealed. The Washington Court of Appeals reversed. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State argued, inter alia, that the court of appeals properly reversed the suppression of evidence because the search of the car was a proper search incident to probable cause to arrest defendant for driving while his license was revoked or for drug paraphernalia in plain view. The court of appeals reasoned that a search incident to arrest could occur before an actual custodial arrest so long as probable cause existed to arrest at the time of the search. The supreme court disagreed. The officer did not arrest defendant for driving while his license was revoked, or for either possession or use of drug paraphernalia. Probable cause for a custodial arrest was not enough. There must have been an actual custodial arrest to provide the "authority" of law justifying a warrantless search incident to arrest under Wash. Const. art. I, § 7. Because a search could not occur without "authority of law," and the search incident exception to the warrant requirement was a narrow one, an actual custodial arrest was required before a search occurred. Otherwise, the search was in fact conducted without an arrest, and thus without authority of law existing at the time of the search.


OUTCOME: The judgment with respect to the pipe and cocaine discovered during the search of the vehicle was reversed. The judgment was otherwise affirmed.

Wash. Pub. Ports Ass'n v. Dep't of Revenue


NO. 71934-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 637; 62 P.3d 462; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 72


November 13, 2002, Oral Argument  
January 30, 2003, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a trade association and two public port districts, filed an action against defendant State of Washington Department of Revenue (DOR), seeking a declaratory judgment invalidating the leasehold excise tax (LET) provisions of Wash. Admin. Code § 458-29A-500 and a refund of assessed LET. The Superior Court of Thurston County (Washington) denied relief, and the trade association appealed.


OVERVIEW: Two public port districts and a trade association consisting of 69 public port districts in Washington State filed a petition for declaratory relief and a request for refund of assessed LET. The port districts and trade association claimed that, pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 82.29A.050, public port districts could be held liable for payment of LET only when a district collected LET from a lessee but did not remit the tax to the DOR, and that Wash. Admin. Code § 458-29A-500 (Rule 500) violated Wash. Const. art. VII, § 1 and Wash. Const. art. VIII, §§ 5 and 7 because it allowed the DOR to hold a port district liable for payment of LET when the district failed to notify DOR that its lessee had not paid the tax. The state supreme court held that (1) Wash. Rev. Code § 82.29A.050 gave the DOR wide discretionary power to hold public port districts accountable for uncollected LET; (2) DOR did not exceed its statutory authority when it promulgated Rule 500; and (3) Rule 500 did not violate Wash. Const. art. VII, § 1 or Wash. Const. art. VIII, §§ 5 and 7.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

Retired Pub. Emples. Council of Wash. v. Charles


71847-4


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 602; 62 P.3d 470; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 70


September 19, 2002, Argued  
January 30, 2003, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, public employees, teachers, and organizations representing public employees and teachers, sued defendant Director of the Washington Department of Retirement Systems, seeking a writ of mandamus compelling the Director to collect employer contributions to two state retirement systems at a former rate. The Superior Court of Thurston County (Washington) granted the Director's motion for summary judgment, and the plaintiffs appealed.


OVERVIEW: The State of Washington Legislature enacted Engrossed House Bill (EHB) 2487, 2000 Wash. Laws ch. 1 (2d. Sp. Sess.), lowering employer contribution rates to two state retirement systems: the Washington Public Employees' Retirement System (PERS) 1 and the Washington Teachers' Retirement System (TRS) 1, and active and retired public employees and teachers filed an action against the Director of the Washington Department of Retirement Systems, challenging the constitutionality of EHB 2487 and seeking a writ of mandamus requiring the Director to collect employer contributions to PERS 1 and TRS 1 at a former, higher rate. The state supreme court held that: (1) the employees and teachers had standing under Wash. Rev. Code § 41.45.050(3) (1998) to seek a writ of mandamus; (2) PERS 1 and TRS 1 were not trusts, and the Director was not a trustee of the funds who had a fiduciary duty to the employees or teachers; and (3) EHB 2487 did not violate Wash. Const. art. I, § 23 or Wash. Const. art. II, §§ 19 or 37.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.

 

387 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



HJS Dev. v. Pierce County


Number 71430-4


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 451; 61 P.3d 1141; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 63



January 23, 2003, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent developer challenged a decision of a hearing examiner for appellant county, which revoked a preliminary plat approval under local ordinances. The Superior Court of Thurston County (Washington) reversed the hearing examiner's decision revoking the preliminary plat. The county appealed.


OVERVIEW: The developer argued that the county hearing examiner lacked the authority to revoke a preliminary plat approval under former Pierce County, Wash. Code §§ 18.50.970 and 18.50.975 because state laws establishing procedures for approving subdivision plats preempted the local ordinances. The developer contended that the revocation powers under the county code conflicted with Wash. Rev. Code § 58.17.140, which provided that a final plat meeting all requirements shall be submitted for approval within five years of preliminary plat approval. The appellate court determined that the hearing examiner had authority to revoke the preliminary plat under former the ordinances, that state platting laws did not preempt the county from enacting the challenged revocation ordinances, and that the revocation ordinance did not conflict with state platting laws. The appellate court held that the hearing examiner correctly decided revocation was the only remedy because the developer knowingly violated conditions of preliminary plat approval, prohibitions against removal of irreplaceable trees and vegetation in specified areas, which could not be restored to their original state.


OUTCOME: The appellate court reversed the trial court's judgment and upheld the decision of the county hearing examiner that revoked the preliminary plat approval.

City of Sumner v. Walsh


No. 71451-7


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 490; 61 P.3d 1111; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 64



January 23, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected January 24, 2003.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent city charged appellant father with two infractions of the city's juvenile curfew ordinance, Sumner, Wash., Municipal Code, 9.28.020(A), by allowing his 14-year-old son to be in a public place during curfew hours. The father was fined $ 50 for each infraction. The Pierce County Superior Court (Washington) rejected the father's constitutional arguments on appeal and affirmed the convictions. The father appealed.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the father argued that the city's curfew ordinance was unconstitutional in that it was void for vagueness as well as violative of the constitutional right of minors to move freely in public places and of parents to rear their children. The appellate court held that the city ordinance was unconstitutionally vague and, therefore, was void. The court found that the problem with the ordinance was that it did not provide ascertainable standards for locating the line between innocent and unlawful behavior. The ordinance simply did not provide sufficient guidance. Specifically, the term "errand" in the ordinance was also vague in that it failed to provide sufficient notice of what conduct was prohibited or standards for police officers to enforce that conduct. Because the ordinance failed to define what constituted an "errand," a police officer would be furnished with little guidance as to whether the juvenile engaged in such an activity was in violation of the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

 

389 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Wash. Water Jet Workers Ass'n v. Yarbrough


No. 70814-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 403; 61 P.3d 309; 2003 Wash. LEXIS 60


January 31, 2002, Oral Argument  
January 16, 2003, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1]
Affirmed in part and reversed in part by, Remanded by, On rehearing at Wash. Water Jet Workers Ass'n v. Yarbrough, 151 Wn.2d 470, 90 P.3d 42, 2004 Wash. LEXIS 360 (Wash., May 13, 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs sued defendants, the Washington Department of Corrections, a water jet cutting business, and its owners, seeking to void a contract between the business and the department. The King County Superior Court, Washington, dismissed claims based on, inter alia, the Ashurst-Sumners Act of 1935 and all claims brought against the owners in their individual capacity. The instant court accepted plaintiffs' request for direct review.


OVERVIEW: The water jet cutting business employed prisoners to work in the water jet cutting industry. Plaintiffs were an informal association of businesses and individuals who did similar work. Plaintiffs, which voluntarily dismissed their remaining causes of action, sought direct review on only the claims based on Wash. Const. art. II, § 29, and the Ashurst-Sumners Act. The instant court concluded that Wash. Rev. Code § 72.09.100(1), which allowed prisoners to be gainfully employed by private businesses, was constitutional. Although the Washington Constitution barred the State from selling the involuntary labor of prisoners by use of the contract system of labor, the contract system of labor bore no resemblance to the program established by § 72.09.100(1). Allowing inmates who wished to be employed to work for Class I Free Venture industries benefited the State. Recidivism, violent incidents among prisoners, and the taxpayers' burden were reduced. The legislature had the power to establish this program. The trial court did not err in dismissing plaintiffs' claims based on the Ashurst-Sumners Act, which created no cause of action enforceable via a 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983 action.


OUTCOME: The instant court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiffs' claims.

Tenino Aerie v. Grand Aerie


No. 71786-9


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 224; 59 P.3d 655; 2002 Wash. LEXIS 795


June 27, 2002, Oral Argument  
December 19, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Grand Aerie, Fraternal Order of Eagles v. Tenino Aerie No. 564, Fraternal Order of Eagles, 538 U.S. 1057, 123 S. Ct. 2221, 155 L. Ed. 2d 1107, 2003 U.S. LEXIS 4094 (2003)


PRIOR HISTORY: Fraternal Order of Eagles, Tenino Aerie No. 564 v. Grand Aerie, Fraternal Order of Eagles, 108 Wn. App. 208, 27 P.3d 1254, 2001 Wash. App. LEXIS 1826 (2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, the local chapters and female members, appealed the judgment of the Court of Appeals, Division Two (Washington), which reversed a trial court's ruling that they were entitled to summary judgment on the claim that the membership policy of respondent fraternal order barring the admission of new female applicants violated the Washington Law Against Discrimination (WLAD), Wash. Rev. Code § 49.60.


OVERVIEW: The fraternal order barred its local chapters from admitting new female applicants on the ground that they would inhibit the secret rituals of the male membership. Several local chapters and female members filed a gender discrimination action. The court, on de novo review, held that the trial court correctly granted the petitioners' motion for summary judgment after concluding that the order was a public accommodation under the WLAD that could not discriminate and was required to admit women into membership. The court held that the appellate court erred in reversing the trial court after reading the ambiguous provisions of Wash. Rev. Code § 49.60.040(10) to automatically exempt the order from public accommodation status merely because it identified itself as a fraternal organization. The court held that the trial court properly determined that the fraternal order was not distinctly private or a selective social club after considering its size of more than a million members worldwide, complex operations, and involvement in community service. The court concluded that the petitioners were entitled to reasonable attorney fees to be determined by the court's clerk.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's decision against the local chapters and female members that the fraternal order's policy was not discriminatory.

 

391 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



McGowan v. State


No. 71947-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 278; 60 P.3d 67; 2002 Wash. LEXIS 802


May 7, 2002, Oral Argument  
December 19, 2002, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, voters and school employees, sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Thurston County (Washington), which granted the state's motion for summary judgment to the state in plaintiffs' action involving Initiative 732 (I-732), which provided for annual cost-of-living increases for school district employees.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs filed an action against the state contending that the state had to fully fund the school district employees' cost-of-living increases pursuant to I-732. The superior court issued an order granting summary judgment to the state, and further declared that under I-732 § 2(1)(d), the State was required to fund the cost-of-living increases only for school district employees who were funded by the state, but not for school district employees who were not funded by the state. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part, stating that § 2(1)(d) was unconstitutional because it treated every school district employee position as part of basic education solely because of certain funding for that position without regard to any education, educational service, or instruction paid for by the funding, and it impermissibly tied basic education dollars to levy dollars, which could result in lack of uniformity in basic education funding in different districts. However, the invalid portion of § 2(1)(d) was severable, and the remainder of section 2(1)(d) as well as the other sections of the initiative were unaffected by the unconstitutionally.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the superior court's decision that all school district employees were entitled to the cost-of-living increase, but it also reversed in part, stating that the initiative required the state to fund the increase for all those employees.

State v. Vickers


No. 71667-6


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 91; 59 P.3d 58; 2002 Wash. LEXIS 787


November 7, 2002, Argued  
December 12, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
 State v. Vickers, 107 Wn. App. 960, 29 P.3d 752, 2001 Wash. App. LEXIS 2007 (2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, petitioners sought review of a decision of the Court of Appeals, Division Two (Washington), affirming their convictions in the trial court of first degree felony murder and attempted first degree murder while armed with a firearm for one petitioner and aggravated first degree murder and attempted first degree murder while armed with a firearm for the other petitioner. The supreme court granted review.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners entered a bar, fired several rounds one of which struck the security guard and one of which killed another patron, took money, and left the bar. Petitioners challenged the validity of the search warrant arguing that the affidavit (1) did not establish probable cause; (2) did not satisfy the "basis of knowledge" portion of the two-part Aguilar-Spinelli test; and (3) made material misrepresentations which resulted in an invalid search. The affidavit indicated that the confidential informant had personal knowledge of conversations petitioners had concerning commission of a robbery in a manner similar to the manner of the robbery and homicide that was committed. The affidavit, when read in its entirety, provided a substantial basis to support the magistrate's finding of probable cause. Petitioners' allegations of an erroneous date was only a scrivener's error and was not sufficient to require suppression of the evidence. Also, there was substantial evidence to support the trial court's written findings of facts. Additionally, the photomontage of one of petitioners was not impermissibly suggestive because any differences between the photographs were minor.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the appellate court's judgment.

 

393 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



Impoundment of Chevrolet Truck


No. 71848-2


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


148 Wn.2d 145; 60 P.3d 53; 2002 Wash. LEXIS 788


May 23, 2002, Argued  
December 12, 2002, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected December 13, 2002.
Related proceeding at Potter v. Wash. State Patrol, 2007 Wash. LEXIS 580 (Wash., Aug. 30, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In separate cases, defendants had their vehicles impounded pursuant to Wash. Admin. Code § 204-96-010. In defendant one's case, the Superior Court of King County, Washington, held the impoundment was proper, while in defendant two's case, the Superior Court of Cowlitz County, Washington, held the impoundment was improper. Discretionary review was granted to address the validity of § 204-96-010.


OVERVIEW: Defendants' vehicles were impounded when officers discovered that each driver's driving privileges had been suspended. On review, defendant one contended the state patrol's blanket rule, Wash. Admin. Code § 204-96-010, comported with the constitutional requirement that all vehicle impoundments were to be reasonable. Similarly, the state patrol asked whether impoundment of defendant two's vehicle was proper. The supreme court was able to avoid the constitutional issue by resolving the case on statutory grounds. While Wash. Rev. Code § 46.55.113 permitted vehicle impoundments at the discretion of the officer at the scene, Wash. Admin. Code § 204-96-010 required officers to impound regardless of the circumstances; thus, the state patrol's mandatory impoundment rule was invalid, as it exceeded the authority of Wash. Rev. Code § 46.55.113. Because defendants' vehicles were improperly impounded, they were each entitled to costs and attorney fees, pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 46.55.120(3)(e).


OUTCOME: The judgment pertaining to defendant one was reversed and remanded for entry of judgment awarding defendant one compensation and storage costs, as well as the filing fee for the impound hearing. The judgment pertaining to defendant two was affirmed and remanded on the issue of reasonable damages for loss of use. Both defendants were awarded statutory costs and attorney fees at trial and on appeal as the prevailing parties.

Willoughby v. Dep't of Labor & Indus.


No. 71950-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


147 Wn.2d 725; 57 P.3d 611; 2002 Wash. LEXIS 717


June 13, 2002, Oral Argument  
November 14, 2002, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff prisoners who had been injured in accidents for which the State's industrial insurance applied challenged the validity of Wash. Rev. Code §§ 51.32.040(3)(a) and 72.60.102. The Walla Walla County Superior Court, Washington, found the statutes unconstitutional. Defendant Washington Department of Labor and Industries appealed. The Washington Court of Appeals certified the appeals for direct review, which the instant court accepted.


OVERVIEW: The challenged statutes barred disbursing industrial insurance permanent partial disability benefits to prisoners with no statutory beneficiaries and were unlikely to be released from prison. In addition to finding the statutes unconstitutional, trial court held that Wash. Rev. Code §§ 51.32.040(3)(a) and 72.60.102 worked a forfeiture of the prisoners' property contrary to Wash. Rev. Code § 9.92.110. The instant court determined that the challenged statutes did not conflict with Wash. Rev. Code § 9.92.110, but also concluded that Wash. Rev. Code § 51.32.040(3)(a), as applied to the prisoners by Wash. Rev. Code § 72.60.102, deprived them of due process. Wash. Rev. Code § 51.32.040(3)(a), as applied to prisoners by Wash. Rev. Code § 72.60.102, also violated equal protection. The challenged statutes conferred an advantage to prisoners with spouses, children, or dependents, not equally available to prisoners without these statutory beneficiaries. The grounds articulated by the department failed to provide a rational basis for the statutory distinction between prisoners who had a beneficiary or were likely to be released during their lifetimes and all other prisoners.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed. The cases were remanded for proceedings consistent with the instant court's opinion. The prisoners were awarded their costs on appeal.

City of Seattle v. Patu


No. 71842-3


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


147 Wn.2d 717; 58 P.3d 273; 2002 Wash. LEXIS 712


June 13, 2002 Argued  
November 7, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1]
 City of Seattle v. Patu, 108 Wn. App. 364, 30 P.3d 522, 2001 Wash. App. LEXIS 2055 (2001).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a ruling of the Washington Court of Appeals that applied the doctrine of invited error to affirm defendant's conviction for interfering with a public officer in violation of Seattle, Wash., Mun. Code § 12A.16.010.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted in a jury trial in the Seattle Municipal Court. The "to convict" instruction given to jury was drafted by the defense. The instruction was defective under Abercrombie because it did not include the elements of "actual obstruction" and "known public" officer required to prove a violation of the ordinance beyond reasonable doubt. The court of appeals held that the instruction was defective but affirmed defendant's conviction on the grounds that the error was invited. The court agreed. Although the doctrine of invited error should be applied with caution in cases involving the failure of an instruction to include an essential element, the court affirmed its holding in Studd, and held that a party could not request an instruction at trial and then complain on appeal that the instruction was defective.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Moreno


No. 71452-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


147 Wn.2d 500; 58 P.3d 265; 2002 Wash. LEXIS 604



October 10, 2002, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner licensee was cited for speeding under Wash. Rev. Code § 46.61.400 by respondent State. The district court dismissed the licensee's motion to dismiss for failure to prosecute, found the infraction had been committed, and fined her $ 45. The licensee appealed, the superior court affirmed, and the Washington Court of Appeals transferred the licensee's motion for discretionary review to the instant court.


OVERVIEW: The officer's written statement provided that the licensee's speed was determined by radar, and, according to the statement, the licensee accused the officer of stopping her because of her race. At the contested hearing, the licensee and her attorney appeared, along with the officer and the radar expert; no prosecutor appeared. The licensee argued that it was improper for the district court judge to act as a lawyer and judge in the same proceeding when it called and questioned the State's witnesses in the traffic infraction hearing without a prosecutor present, pursuant to Wash. Rev. Code § 46.63.080(3). The reviewing court determined that it had jurisdiction over the licensee's constitutional claims and ruled that the district court did not violate the separation of powers doctrine or due process by adjudicating the licensee's traffic infraction hearing without a prosecutor present. In asking for both parties' evidence, listening to all of it, and deciding whether the infraction was committed, the district court was within the constitutional confines of the judicial power.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the district court was affirmed.

 

397 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



State v. Glas


NO. 71514-9 consolidated with 71571-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


147 Wn.2d 410; 54 P.3d 147; 2002 Wash. LEXIS 596


June 27, 2002, Oral Argument  
September 19, 2002, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted in two separate cases before the Superior Court of King County, Washington, of voyeurism, in violation of Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.115. One defendant petitioned the Washington Supreme Court for review after his appeal was rejected by the Washington Court of Appeals, Division Three. The second defendant appealed directly to the Washington Supreme Court.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were each convicted of engaging in "upskirt voyeurism" in which they would go to a shopping mall and take photographs while lying on the floor in an attempt to photograph the women's undergarments. Defendants each contended that Wash. Rev. Code § 9A.44.115 was overbroad and vague, and that the statute did not criminalize upskirt photography in a public place. The appellate court found that the statute was neither unconstitutionally overbroad or vague, but did agree that the statute, as written, did not apply to actions done in a public place. The plain language of the statute clearly limited its scope to acts done in a place where the person against whom the acts were directed would have a reasonable expectation of privacy, not acts that personally invaded the victim's own right of privacy. The court noted that other states had avoided the problem by adopting different language, but, as written, § 9A.44.115 simply did not criminalize defendants' conduct because a shopping mall was a public place where the women could not reasonably expect to be free from public surveillance.


OUTCOME: The convictions were reversed.
