Medina v. Public Util. Dist. No. 1


No. 70978-5


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


147 Wn.2d 303; 53 P.3d 993; 2002 Wash. LEXIS 593


January 17, 2002, Oral Argument  
September 12, 2002, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a suit for personal injuries and loss of consortium brought by petitioners, husband and wife, against respondent county public utility district, the Washington Court of Appeals affirmed the trial court's dismissal of petitioners' suit. Petitioners filed a petition for review with the instant court.


OVERVIEW: On January 9, 1995, the husband was injured when a car owned and operated by the county and driven by a county employee rear-ended the car the husband was driving. In October 1995, petitioners filed a claim with the county for property damage. This claim was settled in October 1995. On January 7, 1998, two days before the statute of limitations was to expire, petitioners filed a second claim with the county for personal injury damages and loss of consortium. Petitioners' filing of the claim commenced a 60-day waiting period before suing and tolled the statute of limitations for filing suit. The county denied the claim on January 13, 1998. On March 5, 1998, four days before the 60-day waiting period was to expire, petitioners filed the instant complaint in superior court. The instant court held that petitioners' failure to wait 60 days before filing a claim against the county in superior court violated Wash. Rev. Code § 4.96.020(4). Petitioners' claims were separate, and each was subject to the requirements of § 4.96.020(4). The statute was constitutional. Without the benefit of § 4.96.020(4)'s tolling provision, petitioners filed suit well beyond the statute of limitations.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed.

 

399 of 2278 DOCUMENTS



In re Det. of C.W.


NO. 71311-1


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


147 Wn.2d 259; 53 P.3d 979; 2002 Wash. LEXIS 591


February 14, 2002, Argued  
September 12, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  In re Detention of C.W., 105 Wn. App. 718, 20 P.3d 1052, 2001 Wash. App. LEXIS 582 (2001)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a consolidated case, petitioner children were taken to a hospital because of complaints of their strange and/or violent behavior. A designated mental health professionals (CDMHP) detained each child for 72-hour evaluation and treatment. Respondent State's petition for further involuntary detention was dismissed. The Washington Court of Appeals reversed and the children appealed.


OVERVIEW: On review, the children asserted that the appellate court erred in concluding that the six-hour time limit in Wash. Rev. Code § 71.05.050 did not begin until the hospital staff determined that an evaluation by the CDMHP was necessary. The supreme court determined that by its terms, § 71.05.050 required several events to occur before the hospital staff could refer a person to the CDMHP and that the six-hour time limitation began to run when the staff determined that the statutory requirements were met. The supreme court also found that it was necessary to impose due process safeguards on the time between a person's arrival at the hospital and the staff's determination that referral to the CDMHP was necessary to protect against potential deprivation that might occur because of the lack of statutory time restraints on the time it took to evaluate a person. Because the children did not argue, nor could they, that the staff or the CDMHP disregarded the statutory requirements, dismissal was not the appropriate remedy, as two of the children contended. The supreme court concluded that dismissal was not usually the appropriate remedy for violations of § 71.05.050.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

400 of 2278 DOCUMENTS


Public Util. Dist. No. 1 v. Dep't of Ecology


No. 70372-8


SUPREME COURT OF WASHINGTON


146 Wn.2d 778; 51 P.3d 744; 2002 Wash. LEXIS 473


September 25, 2001, Oral Argument  
July 18, 2002, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Superior Court of Pend Oreille County. Docket No: 98-2-00243-0. Date filed: 11/01/2000.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In water quality certification cases, petitioner Washington Department of Ecology and respondent public utilities district sought review of summary judgment decisions of the Washington Pollution Control Hearings Board. The Pend Oreille County Superior Court, Washington, consolidated the petitions for review. The board issued a certificate of appealability. The department filed a motion for discretionary review, which the instant court granted.


OVERVIEW: The issue in this case was whether the department had the authority to condition a water quality certification under the Clean Water Act, 33 U.S.C.S. § 1251 et seq., on maintenance of minimum instream flows, where such conditions affected existing water rights. The case also involved issues concerning Wash. Rev. Code § 90.03.380, the surface water statute governing changes in and transfers of water rights, and issues of abandonment and statutory forfeiture of water rights. In upholding the department's denial of a change in point of diversion of water under a 1980 inchoate water right, the instant court concluded that § 90.03.380 required that water must have been applied to beneficial use before a change in point of diversion was authorized. The department could not deny a change application based upon public interest considerations. The water rights at issue were neither abandoned nor statutorily forfeited. The department had the authority to impose bypass flow conditions in a water quality certification regardless of whether the district, which sought to amend its federal license to allow it to generate hydroelectric power, had existing water rights that could be affected.


OUTCOME: The board's decisions were affirmed in part and reversed in part. Its denial of the change application on the 1980 inchoate right was affirmed, as was its decision that the district had not abandoned its water right, and its decision that it had the authority to impose instream flow conditions under the Clean Water Act. Its decision that it had the authority to consider the public interest when considering a change application was reversed.
